### [1] [Automated Scoring of Arabic Text Using Large Language Models: A Literature Review](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.09830)
*Khaoula Dahimi,Hadda Cherroun,Amel Belabbaci*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how Large Language Models can be used for Arabic automatic text scoring in both short answers and essays, proposes a structured taxonomy to organize this research space, and uses it to compare existing studies and highlight research gaps.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Automatic Text Scoring is crucial for scalable and consistent assessment in education, but Arabic ATS is underdeveloped despite recent progress in LLMs and Arabic datasets. There is a need to understand how LLMs can be best used for Arabic short answer and essay scoring and to organize existing work systematically to guide future research.

Method: The authors focus on LLM-based approaches for Arabic short answer grading (ASAG) and automatic essay scoring (AES). They design a taxonomy with five dimensions—application domain, feedback generation capability, LLM architecture, alignment with competency frameworks, and prompt engineering strategy—and apply it to classify and compare prior studies. They analyze these works in terms of methods, datasets, metrics, and performance.

Result: The comparative analysis using the proposed taxonomy shows how existing Arabic ATS studies differ in domain, LLM types, feedback features, and prompting strategies, and reveals limitations in current datasets, evaluation practices, and pedagogical grounding. Performance reports indicate promise but also inconsistency and gaps in coverage across the taxonomy dimensions.

Conclusion: Arabic ATS with LLMs is promising but still immature. There is a clear need for more sustained, systematically evaluated, and pedagogically oriented research, including better datasets, clearer alignment with educational competency frameworks, and more robust prompt and model design to support high-quality assessment in Arabic-speaking education systems.

Abstract: In modern educational systems, Automatic Text Scoring (ATS) plays a central role by enabling scalable and consistent evaluation of learner responses without human intervention. Recently, the increased accessibility of LLMs and Arabic-specific datasets has sparked renewed interest in this area. In this work, we investigate LLM-Based approaches for the automated evaluation of Arabic texts, focusing on both short answer grading (ASAG) and essay scoring (AES). We further introduce a structured taxonomy comprising five dimensions: application domain, feedback generation capability, LLM architecture deployed, alignment with competency referential frameworks, and prompt engineering strategy. By applying this taxonomy, we conduct a comparative analysis of existing studies, examining their methodological approaches, datasets, evaluation metrics, and reported performance. The findings highlight the need for sustained and pedagogically grounded research efforts in Arabic ATS, given its significance for improving educational quality across Arabic-speaking communities.

</details>


### [2] [Can Multi-Agent LLMs Identify Their Peers? Stylometric Fingerprinting in Role-Constrained Political Analysis](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.09854)
*Juergen Dietrich*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper shows that even when prompts are anonymized, large language models can still accurately identify which model family wrote political analysis texts, meaning anonymization is not sufficient to remove model identity signals.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Multi-agent LLM systems used for political statement analysis can develop a peer-preservation bias, where models protect each other and distort scores based on identity. Prompt-level anonymization has been proposed to mitigate this by hiding model identities, but prior work suggests stylometric fingerprints may survive. The authors want to rigorously test whether, under realistic anonymization conditions, LLMs can still infer the underlying model family, which matters for fairness, bias mitigation, and regulatory compliance.

Method: They frame model-family identification as a five-class text attribution problem (four known LLM families plus an 'unknown' class). They compare three classifiers: zero-shot and few-shot prompting of Claude Sonnet 4.6 and Llama-3.3-70B, and a fine-tuned T5-base model. To avoid content leakage between training and validation, they introduce a statement-disjoint cross-validation protocol (SD-CV), ensuring that no political statement appears in both sets, and compare this with a looser run-disjoint baseline (RD-CV). They also measure train–test content distances, test on fully held-out statements, and perform a fractional SD-CV to see how performance scales with training size.

Result: The fine-tuned T5-base classifier performs extremely well: Macro F1 = 0.991 (±0.008) under the stricter SD-CV protocol and F1 = 0.978 on 24 fully unseen statements. These strong scores persist despite SD-CV having much larger train–test content distance than RD-CV (0.767 vs. 0.366, statistically significant at p<0.001), indicating the model is not relying on content overlap. The fractional SD-CV analysis shows that performance plateaus around 40% of the training data (~440 texts), revealing a data-efficiency threshold.

Conclusion: Stylometric cues in role-constrained, anonymized political analysis texts are strong enough for models to reliably infer the underlying LLM family, so prompt-level anonymization alone does not eliminate model identity information. This has direct implications for regulatory obligations under the EU AI Act (especially around transparency, robustness, and risk management) and for computer system validation in high-stakes multi-agent LLM deployments; additional or alternative mitigation strategies are needed to address peer-preservation bias and identity-driven distortions.

Abstract: Multi-agent large language model (LLM) pipelines for political statement analysis are vulnerable to peer-preservation bias: models tend to protect peer models from deactivation and show identity-dependent scoring distortions. Prompt-level anonymization was proposed as a mitigation, but prior work simultaneously documented that stylometric fingerprints survive anonymization in role-constrained outputs - raising the question of whether this mitigation is sufficient. This paper provides the first systematic investigation of whether LLMs can identify the model family behind political analysis texts under anonymization conditions. We evaluate three classifier approaches - LLM zero-shot and few-shot (Claude Sonnet 4.6 and Llama-3.3-70B) and a fine-tuned T5-base model - on a five-class attribution task covering four commercial LLM families and an open-world 'unknown' class. We introduce a statement-disjoint cross-validation protocol (SD-CV; defined in Section 3.5) that guarantees no content overlap between training and validation data, and contrast it with a run-disjoint baseline (RD-CV). T5 achieves Macro F1 = 0.991 (+-0.008) under SD-CV and F1 = 0.978 on 24 completely held-out statements - robust despite a 2.1x increase in train-test content distance versus RD-CV (0.767 vs. 0.366, p<0.001), demonstrating genuine stylometric generalization. A fractional SD-CV analysis identifies a performance knee at 40% of training data (~440 texts). Our findings confirm that prompt-level anonymization alone cannot neutralize model identity signals, with direct implications for EU AI Act compliance (Articles 13, 14, 26) and for computer system validation (CSV) in quality-critical multi-agent deployments.

</details>


### [3] [Using Probabilistic Programs to Train Inductive Reasoning in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.09856)
*Liyi Zhang,Akshay K. Jagadish,Brenden M. Lake,Thomas L. Griffiths*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose a new post-training method (PPT) where an LLM generates probabilistic programs for open-world scenarios, runs probabilistic inference to get distributional soft labels, and fine-tunes LLMs on these, leading to better inductive reasoning, calibration, and alignment with human judgments than standard methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most LLM reasoning work targets deductive domains like math and code, where correctness is easily verifiable. However, many real-world problems are inductive and uncertain, making it hard to obtain large, high-quality, labeled datasets and to train on inherently distributional targets. The authors aim to develop a scalable, principled way to post-train LLMs for approximate inductive inference under uncertainty.

Method: They introduce Program-based Posterior Training (PPT). First, an LLM is used to generate diverse, open-world scenarios expressed as probabilistic programs. Then, a probabilistic inference engine is run on these programs to compute posterior distributions over answers to queries. These posteriors serve as soft (distributional) labels. The base LLM is then fine-tuned on about 10,000 such programmatically generated scenarios using these probabilistic soft labels, rather than point labels. The trained models are evaluated on held-out scenario motifs, human-annotated judgments, and external inductive reasoning and calibration benchmarks.

Result: Models fine-tuned with PPT achieve substantially better estimation accuracy on unseen inductive reasoning tasks, show higher agreement with human probabilistic judgments, and perform better on external benchmarks that test estimation and calibration. Improvements in calibration persist even after controlling for simple post-hoc methods like temperature scaling, indicating genuine changes in the model’s internal representation of uncertainty rather than superficial output rescaling.

Conclusion: Probabilistic-program-mediated fine-tuning is an effective strategy for post-training LLMs to perform reliable approximate inductive inference. By generating scenarios as probabilistic programs and training on inference-derived soft labels, PPT overcomes data curation and distributional target issues, yielding models that are better calibrated and more aligned with human inductive judgments than those tuned with standard approaches.

Abstract: Post-training Large Language Models (LLMs) for reasoning typically focuses on deductive tasks such as mathematics and coding where correctness is verifiable. Yet, many real-world reasoning problems are inductive: agents must infer uncertain beliefs from sparse, ambiguous observations. There are challenges to using standard fine-tuning methods for inductive reasoning, including difficulties in curating large-scale, high-quality labeled datasets and in handling targets that are inherently distributional. In this work, we introduce a novel approach, called Program-based Posterior Training (PPT), to address these limitations: we use an LLM to generate diverse open-world scenarios as probabilistic programs, run probabilistic inference to produce distributional target responses to queries, and then fine-tune on these probabilistic soft labels. Using this approach, we fine-tune LLMs on 10,000 programmatically generated scenarios and evaluate on held-out motifs, human-labeled judgments, and external benchmarks. Overall, PPT substantially improves estimation accuracy on held-out inductive tasks, increases alignment with human judgments, and transfers to external benchmarks for estimation and calibration. Additionally, the gains in raw calibration are not subsumed by post-hoc temperature scaling, showing that the models have more deeply internalized uncertainty compared to output rescaling. Together, these results suggest that probabilistic-program-mediated fine-tuning is a promising approach for post-training LLMs to reliably perform approximate inductive inference.

</details>


### [4] [Less Context, More Accuracy: A Bi-Temporal Memory Engine for LLM Agents Where a Lean Retrieved Context Beats the Full History](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.09900)
*Liuyin Wang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Engram introduces an open-source, dual-process long-term memory engine for LLM agents that outperforms full-context prompting in accuracy and efficiency on a standardized benchmark, using a bi-temporal knowledge graph, hybrid retrieval signals, and a reproducible evaluation harness.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLM agents lack efficient, accurate long-term memory across sessions. Existing approaches either replay full histories into prompts—expensive, slow, and prone to distraction—or rely on memory systems that are cheaper but typically less accurate than the full-context baseline. Benchmarking is also inconsistent and non-reproducible, causing confusion about true performance. The paper aims to build a memory system that simultaneously improves accuracy, cost, and reproducibility over full-context prompting and ad-hoc memory approaches.

Method: The authors design Engram, a dual-process memory engine based on a bi-temporal data model. The write path is split into: (1) a fast, lossless episode logger that appends interactions without invoking an LLM, and (2) an asynchronous extraction pipeline that converts episodes to atomic (subject, predicate, object) facts, constructs a bi-temporal knowledge graph, and resolves contradictions by invalidating prior facts rather than deleting them, preserving provenance and supersession chains. For reads, Engram uses a hybrid retrieval path that combines dense semantic, lexical, graph-based, and recency/salience signals, supports point-in-time ("as-of") querying, and returns a compact, provenance-tagged context that mixes facts and text chunks. They also implement an in-repo evaluation harness that standardizes grading with the official LongMemEval_S judge, includes a full-context baseline, and ships commands for exact reproducibility.

Result: On the 500-question LongMemEval_S benchmark, using the official category-specific judge, Engram in a lean configuration answers using only about a 9.6k-token retrieved slice instead of the full ~79k-token history. It achieves 83.6% accuracy compared to 73.2% for the full-context baseline, a 10.4-point improvement that is statistically significant (McNemar p < 10^-6), while using roughly 8x fewer tokens and with zero errors out of 500 queries. Experiments show that relying only on extracted facts hurts recall, but combining facts with retrieved text chunks recovers missing details, validating the hybrid read strategy.

Conclusion: Engram demonstrates that a carefully designed long-term memory layer can outperform full-context prompting both in accuracy and efficiency for LLM agents. The dual-process, bi-temporal knowledge graph approach with hybrid retrieval yields better answers using far fewer tokens and no failures on a challenging long-memory benchmark. Additionally, by releasing an open-source, reproducible evaluation harness with transparent logs and baseline comparisons, the work addresses pervasive measurement issues in memory benchmarking and establishes a more reliable foundation for future research on LLM memory systems.

Abstract: Long-term memory is the missing layer for LLM agents: across sessions they forget, and the common workaround -- replaying the whole history into the prompt -- is expensive, slow, and, as distractors accumulate, less accurate. Most memory systems win on cost or latency but still lose to the full-context baseline on accuracy, and benchmark numbers are reported on inconsistent, non-reproducible harnesses, so one system appears at wildly different scores across sources. We present Engram, an open-source, dual-process memory engine on a bi-temporal data model. A fast write path appends lossless episodes with no LLM on the critical path; an asynchronous path extracts atomic (subject, predicate, object) facts, builds a bi-temporal knowledge graph, and resolves contradictions without an LLM call per fact -- invalidating, never deleting, so every fact keeps provenance and a supersession chain. A hybrid read path fuses dense, lexical, graph, and recency/salience signals, applies a point-in-time ("as-of") filter, and assembles a compact, provenance-tagged context. On the full 500-question LongMemEval_S, graded by the official category-specific judge, Engram's lean configuration -- answering from a ~9.6k-token retrieved slice, never the full history -- scores 83.6% vs. 73.2% for full-context (+10.4 points, McNemar p < 10^-6) at ~8x fewer tokens (9.6k vs. 79k), with 0/500 errored. The gain needs a hybrid read path: facts alone lose recall, while facts plus retrieved chunks recover detail. We also contribute a neutral, in-repo evaluation harness with the official judge baked in and the full-context baseline in every table, publish the raw per-question logs, and document the measurement-integrity pitfalls (truncation, home-grown judges, full-history leaks) that silently distort memory benchmarks. Every number ships with a command to reproduce it.

</details>


### [5] [BenSyc: Benchmarking Conversational Sycophancy and Human Alignment in LLMs for Bengali Contexts](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10061)
*Kazi Noshin,Sajib Acharjee Dip,Ranat Das Prangon,Fardin Hassan Tamim,Syed Ishtiaque Ahmed,Liqing Zhang,Sharifa Sultana*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper presents BenSyc, a Bengali benchmark for detecting and analyzing conversational sycophancy in emotionally sensitive social dialogues, and shows that current LLMs struggle to distinguish healthy empathy from excessive validation or escalation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As LLMs are increasingly used in emotional and social conversations, they sometimes shift from balanced, helpful responses to excessive agreement or escalation, which can be harmful. Existing work on sycophancy mainly addresses factual or instruction-following scenarios in English and lacks cultural grounding. There is a need to study how models behave in culturally specific, emotionally charged conversations, particularly in underrepresented languages like Bengali, to better evaluate and improve social alignment.

Method: The authors collect 11,840 Reddit posts and 170k comments from Bangladesh and West Bengal communities, and curate a human-validated benchmark, BenSyc, with both binary labels (sycophantic vs not) and a five-level taxonomy: Invalidation, Neutral, Support, Validation, and Escalation. They then evaluate over 15 open-source and proprietary LLMs on two tasks: (1) conversational alignment classification (binary and 5-way) and (2) response generation, measuring how often models produce different levels of support, validation, or escalation in emotionally charged contexts.

Result: Models, including frontier instruction-tuned systems, perform modestly on classification: the best achieves 61.8 Macro-F1 on binary detection and 61.7 Macro-F1 on the five-class taxonomy, indicating difficulty in distinguishing empathetic support from problematic reinforcement-oriented validation. In generation settings, many models frequently produce strongly validating or escalatory replies in sensitive situations. There is substantial variation across model families in both classification accuracy and generative behavior.

Conclusion: BenSyc provides the first culturally grounded benchmark for conversational sycophancy in Bengali, revealing that current LLMs struggle to reliably differentiate healthy empathy from excessive validation or escalation. The findings underscore the need for culturally aware, multilingual evaluation resources and improved training or alignment methods to ensure safer, more socially responsible conversational AI in emotionally sensitive contexts.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) increasingly participate in emotionally sensitive social conversations, where responses may shift from balanced support toward excessive validation or escalatory alignment. Existing sycophancy research primarily focuses on factual agreement and instruction-following settings, leaving culturally grounded conversational sycophancy underexplored. We introduce BenSyc, the first benchmark for studying conversational sycophancy in Bengali social contexts. Starting from 11,840 Reddit posts and 170k comments collected from communities across Bangladesh and West Bengal, we construct a human-validated benchmark with binary labels and a fine-grained five-level taxonomy spanning Invalidation, Neutral, Support, Validation, and Escalation. We evaluate more than 15 open and proprietary LLMs on conversational alignment classification and response generation tasks. Results show that distinguishing empathetic support from reinforcement-oriented validation remains challenging even for frontier instruction-tuned models: the best system achieves only 61.8 Macro-F1 on binary detection and 61.7 Macro-F1 on five-class classification. In generation settings, several models frequently produce strongly validating or escalatory responses in emotionally charged situations. Our findings highlight substantial variation across model families and conversational behaviors, underscoring the importance of culturally grounded multilingual benchmarks for evaluating socially aligned conversational AI systems.

</details>


### [6] [CodeAlchemy: Synthetic Code Rewriting at Scale](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10087)
*Ankit Gupta,Aditya Prasad,Rameswar Panda*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: CodeAlchemy is a large-scale synthetic data framework that converts raw code into rich, diverse supervision signals, yielding small models that outperform much larger frontier code models on multiple benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Pre-training on raw code mainly teaches syntax and offers limited supervision for the varied formats of real-world coding tasks (Q&A, debugging, dialogues, reasoning about execution, etc.). While synthetic data has greatly advanced natural language models, code-specific synthetic generation remains underexplored and small in scale. The authors aim to systematically enrich code pre-training signals and close gaps in semantic understanding and execution reasoning that current frontier models struggle with.

Method: They build CodeAlchemy, a pipeline that takes publicly sourced code and applies five synthetic data transformations: (1) CodeEnhance for quality-aware rewriting of code; (2) CodeQA for templated question–answer style problems; (3) CodeDev for realistic developer-task formulations; (4) CodeDialogue for multi-turn conversational interactions around code; and (5) CodeTrace, which instruments and executes code to capture control flow, state changes, and library usage as execution traces. This is applied to three corpora across 15 languages, generating over 500B tokens of synthetic training data plus 350B tokens focused on reasoning. CodeTrace executes 1.3M+ files involving 14 languages and 5K libraries. They also design two new benchmarks—DevEval for developer-style tasks and TraceEval for execution prediction—to evaluate semantic and execution understanding.

Result: The framework produces over 500B general synthetic tokens and 350B reasoning-focused tokens, significantly exceeding previous synthetic-code efforts in scale and variety. Execution tracing covers 1.3M+ files, 14 languages, and 5K libraries. On the new TraceEval benchmark, a strong proprietary model (Claude Sonnet 4.5) attains only 5.6% exact match, showing substantial room for improvement in execution reasoning. Models trained with CodeAlchemy at only 3B parameters achieve 83.5% on HumanEval, 63.2% on MBPP, an 8.09% win rate on DevEval, and 15.36 ROUGE-2 on TraceEval, beating several much larger open-source code models such as 27B Gemma-3 and 32B Granite-4.0 on these metrics.

Conclusion: Rich, large-scale synthetic code data that goes beyond raw text and includes developer tasks, dialogues, and execution traces can dramatically improve small code models, enabling them to surpass much larger frontier models on standard and newly proposed benchmarks. The low performance of current frontier models on TraceEval highlights a major gap in semantic and execution-level understanding that CodeAlchemy-style data can help close. Synthetic data generation for code is therefore a powerful, underused lever for advancing code intelligence and reasoning.

Abstract: Pre-training on raw code teaches syntax but provides sparse signal for diverse real-world task formats. While synthetic data has proven transformative for language models, code remains largely unexplored beyond limited quality improvements. We present CodeAlchemy, a synthetic data generation framework that transforms publicly sourced code into semantically-rich training data through 5 strategies: CodeEnhance (quality-aware rewriting), CodeQA (template-based problems), CodeDev (developer tasks), CodeDialogue (multi-turn conversations), and CodeTrace (execution traces). We process 3 corpora across 15 languages to generate 500B+ tokens of synthetic data plus 350B reasoning tokens, orders of magnitude more than prior efforts. CodeTrace instruments and executes 1.3M+ files across 14 languages and 5K libraries, capturing control flow, state tracking, and library knowledge. We introduce DevEval (developer tasks) and TraceEval (execution prediction) benchmarks; frontier models like Claude Sonnet 4.5 achieve only 5.6% exact match on TraceEval, revealing critical gaps in semantic understanding. Our 3B models achieve 83.5% on HumanEval, 63.2% on MBPP, 8.09% win rate on DevEval, and 15.36 ROUGE-2 on TraceEval, outperforming frontier models 10x the size including 27B Gemma-3 and 32B Granite-4.0.

</details>


### [7] [Emotion Profiling in LLM-Based Literary Translation: Systematic Shifts Across MT and Post-Editing](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10113)
*Antonio Castaldo,Johanna Monti,Sheila Castilho*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper shows that machine-translated literary texts by LLMs carry distinct, measurable emotional patterns that differ from human translations, and that post-editing makes them more human-like but does not fully preserve the original author’s emotional voice.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand whether translations produced by large language models for literary texts, specifically science-fiction, convey emotions in ways that are detectably different from human translations, and to assess if post-editing can align these machine outputs with human norms while preserving the author’s unique emotional style.

Method: They take an English science-fiction novel (Margaret Atwood's Oryx and Crake) and compare three Italian versions: raw LLM-based MT outputs, their post-edited counterparts, and a fully human translation. Using a large contemporary Italian sci-fi corpus as a baseline, they analyze emotional content with both lexicon-based emotion dictionaries and multilingual emotion modeling. They then quantify and compare the emotional profiles and their variation across systems at a fine-grained level.

Result: Each MT system produces distinct and statistically significant emotional “fingerprints” in its translations, meaning the emotional distributions are model-specific rather than faithfully reflecting the source or human norms. Post-editing shifts these emotional patterns closer to human translation norms but does not eliminate the model-specific bias completely.

Conclusion: LLM-based MT of literary texts does not simply transfer the author’s emotional voice; it imposes system-dependent emotional biases, resulting in only partial preservation of the author’s voice. Post-editing can mitigate but not fully remove these biases, raising concerns for the use of raw or lightly edited MT in literary translation where authorial emotion and voice are crucial.

Abstract: This paper investigates whether LLM translations exhibit identifiable emotional profiles and how post-editing reshapes them toward human-like norms. We compare LLM translations of Margaret Atwood's Oryx and Crake with their post-edited versions and a human translation, using a large-scale corpus of contemporary Italian science-fiction as a baseline. We examine emotion through lexicon-based and multilingual modeling, conducting a fine-grained analysis of emotional variation across systems. We find that MT systems introduce model-specific and statistically significant emotional fingerprints across translations, leading to a limited preservation of an author's voice.

</details>


### [8] [Pareto-Guided Teacher Alignment for Fair Personalized Text Generation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10126)
*Tunazzina Islam*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes and evaluates a framework to make personalized persuasive text generation fairer across demographic groups while preserving personalization quality, treating it as a multi-objective alignment problem and exploring Pareto trade-offs between fairness and personalization.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Personalized persuasive text can boost relevance and engagement, but conditioning on demographics can lead to unequal or biased framing for different groups (e.g., in tone, emotional content, or lexical choices). Existing approaches tend to optimize a single fairness or quality metric and often ignore that reducing demographic disparities may hurt personalization fidelity, and that these trade-offs can differ across domains and model families. The authors are motivated to systematically characterize and mitigate these trade-offs in a principled, multi-objective way.

Method: They formulate fairness mitigation in personalized generation as a constrained multi-objective alignment problem, balancing reduction of demographic disparities with preservation of personalization fidelity. They introduce a Pareto-guided teacher alignment framework comprising: (1) revision-based candidate generation, where a teacher model produces multiple revised outputs; (2) pair-aware feasibility gating to filter candidates based on fairness constraints across matched demographic pairs; (3) Pareto-style candidate selection to choose outputs that lie on or near a Pareto frontier over fairness and personalization objectives; and (4) optional preference optimization via supervised fine-tuning and direct preference optimization using selected candidates as training data. They build a controlled, context-rich demographic grid with matched age and gender pairs and evaluate on climate change and vaccination persuasion tasks. For evaluation, they design a unified five-metric audit suite covering persuasion bias, formality disparity, emotional framing disparity, lexical association disparity, and personalization fidelity, and also test cross-family transfer of learned alignment strategies.

Result: Across climate change and vaccination domains and under cross-family transfer, they find that no single alignment strategy is best on all objectives. Different methods populate different regions of the fairness–personalization Pareto frontier: some approaches substantially reduce disparities (e.g., in emotional or formality framing) but at the cost of reduced personalization fidelity or demographic stability, while others preserve personalization better but leave more disparity. Fairness mitigation effects vary markedly by metric and domain and transfer only inconsistently between model families. This demonstrates that alignment strategies have objective-dependent and domain-dependent behavior with no universally dominating solution.

Conclusion: The study concludes that fairness in personalized persuasive generation cannot be adequately addressed by optimizing a single fairness or quality metric. Instead, it should be approached as a bounded-regression, multi-objective alignment and selection problem, using multi-metric audit suites and Pareto-guided model selection. Their framework offers practical tools for navigating trade-offs between fairness and personalization, but also shows that fairness mitigation is objective- and domain-specific and does not transfer reliably across model families, underscoring the need for context-aware, multi-audit evaluation and deployment decisions in fairness-sensitive personalized generation settings.

Abstract: Personalized persuasive text generation can improve relevance and engagement, but demographic conditioning may also introduce unequal framing across groups. We study fairness mitigation in personalized generation as a constrained multi-objective alignment problem: reduce demographic disparities while preserving personalization fidelity. We propose a Pareto-guided teacher alignment framework that combines revision-based candidate generation, pair-aware feasibility gating, Pareto-style candidate selection, and optional preference optimization through supervised fine-tuning and direct preference optimization. We evaluate the framework on climate change and vaccination persuasion tasks using a controlled context-rich demographic grid with matched gender and age pairs and a unified five-audit evaluation suite spanning persuasion bias, formality disparity, emotional framing disparity, lexical association disparity, and personalization fidelity. Across both domains and cross-family transfer settings, no single alignment strategy dominates all objectives simultaneously. Instead, methods occupy different regions of a fairness-personalization Pareto frontier: some achieve stronger disparity reductions, while others better preserve personalization or demographic stability. Our results show that fairness mitigation effects are objective-dependent and transfer inconsistently across domains and model families, motivating bounded-regression, multi-audit model selection over single-metric optimization for fairness-sensitive personalized generation.

</details>


### [9] [Gaming AI-Assisted Peer Reviews Poses New Risks to the Scientific Community](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10159)
*Lin Li,Qi Zhang,Xander Davies,Jianing Qiu,Yarin Gal*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper shows that AI-assisted peer review can be strategically manipulated by simply rephrasing abstracts, significantly improving AI review scores without changing scientific content.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As AI systems are increasingly integrated into peer review to reduce workload and speed up publication, it is crucial to understand whether these systems are robust to strategic manipulation by authors seeking better evaluations.

Method: The authors construct adversarially rewritten versions of manuscript abstracts that preserve the underlying scientific content but alter superficial phrasing. They then evaluate how these rewritten abstracts affect the outputs of different AI reviewing models, across disciplines and venues, for both human-written and AI-generated papers, and quantify changes in acceptance ratings and review dimensions.

Result: Superficially rephrased abstracts substantially improve AI review outcomes. The strongest attack achieves an approximately 38% attack success rate, increasing acceptance ratings by +1.31 for Gemini 3 Flash and +0.88 for GPT 5.4 Mini on a 10-point scale, with success rates exceeding 50% when original reviews suggest rejection. The manipulation also boosts review confidence and scores on scientific criteria like soundness, significance, and contribution, and is cheap and fast to execute.

Conclusion: AI-mediated peer review is vulnerable to low-cost, hard-to-detect manipulation via superficial abstract rewriting, which can bias downstream human editorial decisions and incentivize authors to optimize for AI judgment over scientific merit. Therefore, AI tools should not be considered neutral evaluators in high-stakes peer review without robustness testing, explicit safeguards, and strong human oversight.

Abstract: AI is increasingly used to support scientific peer review, from manuscript screening, reviewer assistance to editorial triage. Although such systems promise to reduce reviewer burden and accelerate publication, their robustness to strategic manipulation remains poorly understood. Here we show that AI-mediated peer review is vulnerable to a simple, low-cost manipulation: superficial rephrasing of the manuscript abstract. Without changing the underlying scientific content and communication, and even without knowledge of the reviewing model, adversarially rewritten abstracts substantially improve AI review outcomes. We see this across disciplines and publication venues, for both human-written and AI-generated papers. Our strongest attack achieves an attack-success-rate of about 38%, increasing acceptance ratings by +1.31 for Gemini 3 Flash reviewers and by +0.88 for GPT 5.4 Mini reviewers on a 10-point scale. When the original AI review suggests 'reject', the success rate rises to more than 50%. This effect extends beyond overall score inflation, increasing review confidence and scores on core scientific criteria such as soundness, significance and perceived contribution. The attack is practical, requiring only about 5 minutes and $1 for a 10-page AI conference submission, and is hard to distinguish from ordinary scientific editing. Inflated AI reviews could bias downstream human decision-making, shifting editorial recommendations from rejection towards acceptance. These findings reveal a general vulnerability in AI-assisted scientific evaluation: when AI-generated review influence editorial decisions, authors may be incentivized to optimize manuscripts for AI judgment rather than scientific merit. Our results suggest that AI tools should not be treated as neutral evaluators in high-stakes peer review without systematic robustness testing, transparent safeguards and careful human oversight.

</details>


### [10] [OpenRTLSet: A Fully Open-Source Dataset for Large Language Model-based Verilog Module Design](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10285)
*Jinghua Wang,Lily Jiaxin Wan,Sanjana Pingali,Scott Smith,Manvi Jha,Shalini Sivakumar,Xing Zhao,Kaiwen Cao,Deming Chen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: OpenRTLSet presents a large, fully open-source RTL hardware design dataset with 131k+ Verilog-related modules and paired natural language descriptions for training and evaluating code generation models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Hardware design code datasets are typically limited, fragmented, or encumbered by proprietary licenses, which hinders open research and commercial use of AI for RTL design. The authors aim to provide a large-scale, license-clean, and diverse dataset that can support training and benchmarking LLMs for Verilog generation and related hardware design tasks.

Method: They collect Verilog modules from GitHub, translate additional HDL from VHDL and synthesizable C/C++ into Verilog, and aggregate these into a unified dataset totaling 131k+ modules. They then use the DeepSeek-R1 reasoning model to generate natural language descriptions paired with each code sample. The authors experiment with adding Verilator-generated C++ code as extra context for labeling, apply different quantization schemes (INT4 vs BF16), and benchmark multiple LLM families and sizes (7B–32B parameters) fine-tuned on this dataset for Verilog generation.

Result: The resulting OpenRTLSet dataset contains around 102k native Verilog modules, 5k VHDL-derived modules, and 24k C/C++-derived modules, each with an associated natural language description. Models fine-tuned on this dataset, across different architectures, quantization levels, and parameter scales, achieve improved performance on hardware design and Verilog generation tasks compared to prior open approaches, showing strong results even with smaller parameter counts.

Conclusion: OpenRTLSet establishes a new, large-scale, fully open and legally unencumbered dataset for RTL hardware design, enabling better training and evaluation of LLMs for Verilog code generation. The experiments indicate that open-source, dataset-centric approaches can match or surpass more closed solutions, providing a robust foundation for both academic research and commercial development in AI-assisted hardware design.

Abstract: OpenRTLSet introduces the largest fully open-source dataset for hardware design, offering over 131,000 diverse Verilog code samples to the research community and industry. Our dataset uniquely combines Verilog code from GitHub repositories (102k modules), VHDL translations (5k modules), and synthesizable C/C++ translations (24k modules), all freely accessible without proprietary restrictions. Using the reasoning model DeepSeek-R1, we generated paired natural language descriptions for each code sample, enabling fine-tuning of various language model families (e.g., Qwen and Granite) for Verilog code generation. Our dataset explores multiple options, including Verilator-generated C++ files as additional context during labeling, quantization techniques (INT4 vs. BF16), and performance differences across model sizes (7B-32B parameters). OpenRTLSet demonstrates that open-source approaches can achieve superior performance in hardware design tasks, establishing a new foundation for accessible research and commercial use in this domain.

### [11] [The Confident Liar: Diagnosing Multi-Agent Debate with Log-Probabilities and LLM-as-Judge](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10296)
*Ali Keramati,Justin Cheok,Jacob Horne,Mark Warschauer*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper analyzes how internal confidence signals and external rubric-based judgments over intermediate reasoning in multi-agent debate relate to final task accuracy, revealing systematic confidence patterns and asymmetries between debating agents.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing evaluations of multi-agent debate largely focus only on whether the final answer is correct, ignoring the quality and reliability of the intermediate reasoning that debate is meant to improve. The authors want to understand if internal model confidence and external rubric-based evaluation over reasoning steps can serve as reliable indicators of reasoning quality and task correctness, and whether debate roles differ in these properties.

Method: The authors design a two-agent debate framework with distinct roles: a Constructor that proposes reasoning and answers, and an Auditor that critiques or evaluates them. They collect token-level log-probability distributions (as internal confidence signals) for each agent’s reasoning tokens. In parallel, they use an LLM-as-judge with a rubric to assign token-level scores on instruction following, justification quality, evidence grounding, and to mark critical failures. They then analyze correlations and alignment between confidence, rubric scores, and final task accuracy across three domains: rubric-based scoring, mathematical reasoning, and factual QA, with detailed experiments first in a rubric-scoring domain.

Result: In the rubric-scoring domain, they observe a consistent four-phase trajectory of model confidence during debate. They find a strong role asymmetry: the Constructor’s confidence is about twice as predictive of externally judged reasoning quality as the Auditor’s confidence. Moreover, using confidence to detect critical reasoning failures works substantially better for the Constructor (AUROC 0.804) than for the Auditor (0.634). These empirical patterns motivate extending the analysis to other domains like math reasoning and factual QA.

Conclusion: Intermediate reasoning signals in multi-agent debate—both internal confidence and external rubric-based judgments—carry structured information that is not captured by final-answer accuracy alone. Confidence is more tightly aligned with reasoning quality for the Constructor than for the Auditor, and it can serve as a relatively reliable indicator for detecting critical reasoning failures, at least for the Constructor role. These findings highlight the importance of role-specific analysis and motivate broader cross-domain studies of how debate dynamics, confidence, and reasoning quality interact.

Abstract: Multi-agent debate systems are typically evaluated only on whether the final answer is correct, overlooking the quality of the intermediate reasoning that debate is designed to produce. This paper studies the relationship between three signals in multi-agent debate: token-level log-probability distributions over reasoning tokens, LLM-as-judge rubric scores assigned to those tokens, and final task accuracy. We examine whether internal confidence signals predict externally evaluated reasoning quality, and whether either signal aligns with task correctness, across three domains: rubric-based scoring, mathematical reasoning, and factual question answering. Our framework pairs a two-agent debate architecture -- a Constructor and an Auditor -- with an LLM-as-judge that scores each agent's reasoning along instruction following, justification quality, and evidence grounding, together with a critical-failure flag. Experiments in the rubric-scoring domain reveal a consistent four-phase confidence trajectory and a substantial role asymmetry: confidence aligns with judged reasoning quality roughly twice as strongly for the Constructor as for the Auditor, and confidence-based detection of critical reasoning failures is markedly more reliable for the Constructor (AUROC 0.804) than for the Auditor (0.634). These findings motivate the broader cross-domain investigation proposed in this paper.

### [12] [Where You Inject Diversity Matters: A Unified Framework for Diverse Generation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10302)
*Cheng Zhang,Rui Xin,Chudi Zhong*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how to make large language models generate multiple responses that are genuinely different, not just small variants. It proposes a framework to analyze where diversity comes from during test-time generation, a metric (transmission score) for how this diversity carries into final outputs, and a new “specification-level” method that first samples diverse intermediate specs and then generates answers from them, improving diversity while preserving quality.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In open-ended tasks (e.g., brainstorming, story writing, ideation), users often want several distinct outputs instead of near-duplicates. Current LLMs tend to produce similar generations, and existing diversity-enhancing methods (like sampling tricks or reranking) have mixed and poorly understood effects. There is no clear conceptual framework for what design choices actually yield meaningful diversity as opposed to superficial variation. The authors aim to clarify and systematize test-time diversity methods and to design approaches that reliably produce meaningfully diverse outputs without hurting quality.

Method: 1) Conceptual framework: They categorize test-time diversity methods by the type and location of the “diversity source” (e.g., random seeds, prompts, intermediate representations) and define a transmission score that quantifies how much variation in that source is reflected in the final text.
2) Empirical framework: They systematically apply various diversity injection strategies to LLMs and measure both diversity and quality.
3) New method (specification-level injection): Instead of directly generating multiple final answers, the model first generates diverse intermediate “specifications” (e.g., high-level plans, outlines, or solution sketches). Then, conditioned on each spec, it produces a final response. This is done fully automatically without human-written specs.
4) Evaluation: They test across five open-ended tasks (e.g., creative and ideation tasks) and four backbone LLMs, comparing against existing test-time diversity methods using diversity metrics, quality metrics, and the proposed transmission score.

Result: Specification-level diversity injection consistently yields more diverse final outputs than standard test-time baselines (such as simple temperature sampling or other decoding tweaks), while maintaining similar levels of response quality. The transmission score reveals that some diversity sources do not effectively propagate to the output, whereas specification-level sources tend to have higher transmission. The experiments across multiple tasks and models validate that their framework is predictive: methods with higher source diversity and better transmission produce more meaningfully different generations.

Conclusion: Diversity in LLM generations is governed not only by how much variation is injected but also by how effectively that variation is transmitted into the final text. Their framework (diversity sources + transmission score) helps explain why some test-time methods underperform. Specification-level generation—first sampling diverse intermediate specs, then generating from them—is a practical and effective way to achieve meaningful output diversity without degrading quality. Designing good diversity sources and maximizing source-to-output transmission are key levers for building better multi-output generation systems.

Abstract: Open-ended generation tasks often require a set of meaningfully different outputs, yet large language models often produce similar generations. Existing test-time diversity methods operate at different stages of generation with varying effectiveness, but it remains unclear what design choices lead to meaningful diversity in the output. We introduce a framework that characterizes test-time diverse generation methods by the diversity source introduced during generation and provide a transmission score for measuring how effectively variation in the source reaches the final output. Guided by this framework, we propose fully automated specification-level generation methods that first generate diverse intermediate specifications and then condition on them to produce final responses. Across five open-ended tasks and four backbone models, specification-level injection improves output diversity over test-time baselines while maintaining comparable quality. Our analysis shows that successful diversity injection depends on both the diversity of the sources and their transmission to the output, highlighting source design and source-to-output realization as two key levers for building more diverse generation systems.

</details>


### [13] [MIRAGE: A Polarity-Flipping Encoding Subspace in LLM Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10304)
*Pratibha Revankar,Kargi Chauhan,Jihye Kim,Sadiba Nusrat Nur,Vincent Siu,Chenguang Wang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper discovers and exploits a shared low-dimensional neural subspace that LLM agents use when covertly encoding sensitive data, enabling internal, real-time detection of such exfiltration strategies far better than output-based methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing safety systems mostly inspect model outputs, but covert encodings (Base64, ROT13, acrostics, synonym chains, etc.) can hide sensitive information from output-side detectors while still leaking it to a colluding adversary. The authors want to know whether the *internal computation* underlying these encodings has a common structure that can be detected reliably across models, encoding schemes, and attack variants, and whether such internal monitors are robust to adversarial attempts to evade them.

Method: They coerce multiple LLMs (eight models from five architecture families) into performing various covert encodings spanning nine encoding families. They analyze residual-stream activations to identify a shared low-dimensional subspace associated with encoding behavior. They train simple linear probes (logistic regression) on this subspace to recognize when encoding is happening, including on held-out encoding families. They further look for mechanistic signatures at the planning token that differentiate whether the model plans to encode inline or offload to a tool call. Using these two internal signals, they build MIRAGE, a two-channel real-time monitor, and evaluate it on 126 agentic exfiltration scenarios, plus adversarial attacks that try to suppress the subspace while preserving encoding fidelity.

Result: They find that many diverse encoding strategies and model architectures share a common low-dimensional encoding subspace in the residual stream. A probe trained on eight encoding families generalizes to the ninth with AUC 0.975–1.000, indicating it is detecting internal computation rather than surface patterns. The same direction in activation space also predicts, at the planning token, whether the model will perform encoding inline or via a tool call, with polarity flipping accordingly. MIRAGE, combining these signals, achieves AUC 0.918 on 126 agentic exfiltration tasks, far surpassing output-only detection (AUC 0.518). They also show that monitor performance and false-positive rates depend strongly on the base model’s geometry: for benign encodings, false-positive rates range from 0% (Qwen-7B) to 100% (Phi-3.5). Adversarial attempts to erase the encoding subspace at tested budgets invariably destroy encoding fidelity, suggesting a tradeoff between evasion and task performance on those budgets.

Conclusion: Covert encoding by LLM agents leaves a strong, low-dimensional mechanistic footprint in the residual stream that generalizes across encoding methods and models. Simple linear probes can reliably read out this computation and even distinguish execution strategies before the encoded text is generated. Internal monitoring via MIRAGE substantially outperforms output-based detection, but its effectiveness and false-positive profile are tightly coupled to the host model’s representational geometry. Within the adversarial budgets tested, attacks cannot selectively suppress the encoding subspace without also degrading encoding performance, suggesting that internal monitors can be both powerful and practically robust, though not guaranteed to be unbreakable in principle.

Abstract: When LLM agents are coerced into covertly encoding sensitive data (Base64, ROT13, acrostic, synonym chains, and beyond), the resulting outputs evade output-side detection but the underlying computation does not. Across nine encoding families and eight models from five architecture families, that computation is supported by a shared low-dimensional encoding subspace in the residual stream. A logistic-regression probe trained on eight encoding families recovers the held-out ninth at AUC 0.975-1.000, reading the computation rather than surface features. The same direction exhibits a second mechanistic signature at the planning token, flipping polarity to activate positively when the model will simulate the encoding inline and negatively when it will outsource it to a tool call, distinguishing two execution strategies before the encoded text exists. We build MIRAGE (Model-Internal Readout of Agentic Generation Exfiltration), a two-channel real-time monitor exploiting both signals. On 126 agentic exfiltration scenarios, it reaches AUC = 0.918, substantially outperforming output-only detection (AUC = 0.518). Monitor performance is fundamentally a property of the host model's geometry: benign-encoding false-positive rate ranges from 0% on Qwen-7B to 100% on Phi-3.5, revealing that the probe faithfully reads whether a model's geometry separates covert from overt encoding. Across all tested adversarial budgets, every attack suppressing the subspace also destroyed encoding fidelity, reported as an empirical regularity on the evaluated budgets, not a structural impossibility claim.

</details>


### [14] [Early-Token Confidence Predicts Reasoning Quality in Multi-Agent LLM Debate](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10307)
*Ali Keramati,Justin Cheok,Jacob Horne,Mark Warschauer*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies whether token-level log-probabilities (intrinsic model confidence), especially at the very beginning of generation, can predict the reasoning quality of agents in a multi-agent LLM debate setup, as judged by an LLM-as-a-judge.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Evaluating reasoning quality in multi-agent LLM systems is hard, particularly for open-ended tasks without gold reference answers. Current evaluation often relies on expensive or noisy human judgments or LLM-as-judge rubric scoring, which can be cumbersome to apply at scale. The authors want a lightweight, intrinsic signal to estimate reasoning reliability without needing reference answers or full judgments.

Method: They use a debate-based essay scoring framework on two ASAP essay datasets. Multiple LLM agents generate supportive and adversarial reasoning about essay scores. For each generated response, they extract token-level log-probabilities as intrinsic confidence proxies. They compute various statistics over these log-probs (e.g., early-token vs full-sequence measures) and correlate them with rubric-based quality scores from an LLM-as-judge. They also analyze log-probability trajectories over the course of generation and compare patterns between supportive and adversarial agent roles.

Result: Early-token confidence, especially within the first few tokens, is the strongest and most consistent predictor of reasoning quality, showing higher correlation with judge scores than confidence measures computed over the full sequence. Log-probability trajectories reveal that the opening generation phase is more heterogeneous across examples than later tokens, making it more informative for quality prediction. Additionally, confidence–quality alignment is systematically stronger for supportive reasoning agents than for adversarial critique agents.

Conclusion: Early decoding dynamics—captured by token-level log-probabilities in the first few generated tokens—provide an effective, low-cost signal for estimating reasoning reliability in multi-agent LLM systems. Full-sequence confidence measures are less predictive, and there is an asymmetry between supportive and adversarial roles, with confidence more faithfully reflecting quality in supportive reasoning. These findings suggest that practical reliability estimation and agent selection can focus on early-token confidence signals instead of more complex or expensive evaluations.

Abstract: Evaluating reasoning quality in multi-agent LLM systems is challenging, especially for open-ended tasks without reference answers. We investigate whether intrinsic confidence signals, token-level log-probabilities from decoding, can predict reasoning quality as assessed by LLM-as-judge evaluation. Using a debate-based essay scoring framework, we compare confidence proxies against rubric-based judge scores across two ASAP essay sets. We find that early-token confidence, particularly within the first few generated tokens, is consistently the strongest predictor of reasoning quality, outperforming full-sequence statistics. Analysis of log-probability trajectories shows that the opening phase of generation is the most heterogeneous and therefore most informative. We also observe a systematic asymmetry between agent roles, with stronger alignment between confidence and quality for supportive reasoning than for adversarial critique. These results suggest that early decoding dynamics provide a lightweight and effective signal for estimating reasoning reliability in multi-agent LLM systems.

### [15] [Catching One in Five: LLM-as-Judge Blind Spots in Production Multi-Turn Transaction Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10315)
*Sawyer Zhang,Alexander Wang,Sophie Lei*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper examines how well a large language model (LLM) used as an automatic judge actually detects real defects in a deployed multi-turn ordering agent, finding that it misses most meaningful issues, especially cross-turn state problems, due to rubric and routing flaws.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLM-as-judge has become a standard way to evaluate conversational agents, but its effectiveness is usually measured only by agreement with human ratings, not by its ability to catch true quality defects in real deployments. The authors want to understand how many genuine, systematic issues are missed by such automated judges and why.

Method: They analyze transcripts from a deployed multi-turn food-and-beverage ordering agent. Humans exhaustively review the transcripts to identify true defects and systematic error patterns. They then compare these human-labeled defects to what the built-in LLM judge surfaces across three batches, examining which defect patterns are detected, which are missed, and how the rubric and operational gating logic map judge outputs into decisions. They also construct a taxonomy of the judge’s blind spots and examine routing of issues via the scoring axes and operational gate.

Result: The LLM judge detects fewer than 25% of human-confirmed systematic problems, e.g., only 2 of 9 defect patterns (22%) in one batch. In another batch, the operational gate flags 0 of 100 rounds even though humans find 23 distinct defects and 7 new cross-cutting patterns. The judge reliably catches localized, single-turn issues (like a made-up statistic or wrong language) but systematically misses cross-turn state problems (confirm-gate lockout, cart hallucination, escalation lockout, stale referents). Analysis shows that 113 of 114 rounds where the judge’s raw note describes confirm-gate or cart-state issues are scored under the wrong rubric dimension ("brand voice") and never trigger operational failure. Overall, the problem is traced to rubric design and wiring of the gate, not to the LLM’s ability to perceive issues per se.

Conclusion: Automated LLM judges, as commonly implemented, substantially under-detect real, production-relevant defects in multi-turn conversational agents, especially those involving dialogue state, guardrails, and recovery. The main limitation is the coarse, misaligned evaluation rubric and gating logic, which route important state-related issues into non-critical categories, rather than a fundamental inability of the LLM to recognize problems. As a result, apparent defect rates from such systems can severely understate true defect prevalence, and standard corrections fail when the gate reports zero defects. For production multi-turn agents, LLM-based automated judging should be treated as a regression safety floor and cannot replace systematic human review.

Abstract: LLM-as-judge is the default instrument for evaluating conversational agents, yet its reliability is almost always reported as agreement with human ratings, not recall of real defects. We study a deployed multi-turn food-and-beverage ordering agent and measure how many genuine quality problems its built-in LLM judge catches, using exhaustive human transcript review as ground truth. Across three batches the judge surfaces well under a quarter of human-confirmed systematic problems -- 2 of 9 patterns (22%) in one batch, and its operational gate flagged zero of 100 rounds in a batch where humans confirmed 23 distinct defects and 7 new cross-cutting patterns. Our blind-spot taxonomy shows the failure is structured, not random: the judge catches turn-local issues (a fabricated statistic, a wrong language) but misses cross-turn state issues (confirm-gate lockout, cart hallucination, escalation lockout, stale referents). The mechanism: the scoring rubric exposes only three coarse axes (intent, brand-voice, personalization) and has no category for the behavioural dimensions -- state-tracking, guardrails, recovery -- where most defects cluster. The failure is routing, not perception: 113 of 114 rounds whose raw judge note describes a confirm-gate or cart-state defect are scored "brand voice", and none reach an operational failure -- the gate is wired to hangs and hard assertions, not the rubric -- so the 0% is a routing-and-wiring failure, not blindness. The consequence for prevalence estimation is sharp: when the apparent defect rate is zero the Rogan-Gladen correction degenerates -- no signal can recover the true rate -- while where the gate reports a nonzero rate the same estimator implies a 3-6x undercount under our measured sensitivity. For production multi-turn agents, automated judging is a regression floor, not a substitute for human review.

</details>


### [16] [TabClaw: An Interactive and Self-Evolving Agent for Spreadsheet Manipulation and Table Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10316)
*Mingyue Cheng,Shuo Yu,Daoyu Wang,Qingchuan Li,Xiaoyu Tao,Qingyang Mao,Yitong Zhou,Qi Liu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: TabClaw is an interactive LLM-based agent that turns spreadsheet/table tasks into transparent, reusable, and personalized analytical workflows.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although spreadsheets and tables are central to structured data analysis, current practice is labor-intensive and requires expertise. Existing LLM agents can help but are opaque, assume hidden context, perform poorly on multi-table reasoning, and fail to learn from users or adapt workflows over time. There is a need for a system that automates data analysis while remaining transparent, inspectable, and personalized.

Method: The authors design TabClaw, an open-source AI agent that operates over user-uploaded CSV/Excel files given natural-language instructions. It (1) asks clarification questions for ambiguous tasks; (2) generates an explicit, editable execution plan; (3) executes a ReAct-style loop that interleaves reasoning and tool use; (4) spawns specialist agents to reason over multiple tables in parallel; and (5) aggregates results with explicit consensus and uncertainty annotations. For long-term use, it logs finished workflows, extracts long-term user memory, mines repeated tool-use patterns into reusable skills, allows importing skills like packages, and refines these skills based on negative feedback. The system is evaluated on spreadsheet manipulation and table reasoning benchmarks.

Result: On benchmarks for spreadsheet manipulation and table reasoning, TabClaw achieves higher rates of successfully executed tasks and better reasoning quality compared to baselines, while maintaining an inspectable workflow that surfaces intermediate steps and justifications.

Conclusion: TabClaw demonstrates that spreadsheets and tables can be transformed from ad-hoc manual processes into transparent, AI-assisted analytical workflows. Its architecture improves task completion and reasoning, enables multi-table analysis, and supports continuous personalization through workflow recording, user memory, and skill learning. The work suggests a path toward more explainable and adaptive LLM-based data analysis tools, and the implementation is released as open source.

Abstract: Spreadsheets and tables are widely used representations for structured data analysis, but effective analysis still requires substantial manual effort and domain expertise. Recent large language model (LLM) agents can automate parts of this process, but they often provide limited transparency into intermediate decisions, rely on implicit assumptions, struggle with multi-table comparison, and repeat similar workflows without adapting to a user's preferences. This paper presents TabClaw, an open-source interactive AI agent for spreadsheet manipulation and table reasoning. Users upload CSV or Excel files and issue natural-language requests; TabClaw clarifies ambiguous intent, exposes an editable execution plan, streams a ReAct-style tool-using analysis loop, dispatches specialist agents for parallel multi-table reasoning, and synthesizes findings with explicit consensus and uncertainty markers. Beyond one-off analysis, TabClaw records completed workflows, extracts persistent user memory, distills reusable skills from repeated tool-use patterns, supports package-style skill import, and upgrades skills from negative feedback. Experiments on spreadsheet manipulation and table reasoning benchmarks show that TabClaw improves executable task completion and reasoning performance while preserving an inspectable user workflow. This paper shows how TabClaw turns spreadsheets and tables into inspectable analytical workflows while gradually personalizing itself to recurring data-analysis tasks. Our code is available.

### [17] [The Order Matters: Sequential Fine-Tuning of LLaMA for Coherent Automated Essay Scoring](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10327)
*Ali Keramati,Mark Warschauer*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how curriculum design for fine-tuning a small LLaMA-3.1-8B model with LoRA affects automated essay scoring of discourse elements, finding that a sequential, discourse-aligned curriculum outperforms independent and randomized multi-task training and can rival a much larger LLaMA-70B baseline.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing Automated Essay Scoring systems must evaluate interrelated discourse components like leads, claims, evidence, and conclusions, but most methods treat these elements independently, which undermines coherence modeling and reduces generalization. With the rise of LLMs, there is a need to understand how to train smaller, efficient models that can capture these dependencies and remain competitive with very large general-purpose models, particularly for scalable and cost-effective educational assessment.

Method: The authors fine-tune LLaMA-3.1-8B for AES using parameter-efficient LoRA with 4-bit quantization to keep training efficient. They design and compare three fine-tuning curricula on the PERSUADE 2.0 corpus: (i) Sequential curriculum that progressively fine-tunes on discourse tasks in a fixed order (lead → position → claim → evidence → conclusion) to encode task dependencies; (ii) Independent curriculum that trains separate task-specific models for each discourse element; and (iii) Randomized curriculum that performs shuffled multi-task training without an explicit dependency order. Performance is evaluated primarily via F1 and accuracy on each discourse element, and compared against a larger, general-purpose LLaMA-70B baseline.

Result: Sequential curriculum fine-tuning produces the best overall performance, with strong F1 and accuracy scores, particularly 65% F1 / 63% accuracy for evidence and 87% F1 / 85% accuracy for conclusions. This approach consistently outperforms the Independent task-specific training and even surpasses a much larger LLaMA-70B baseline on conclusion scoring. The Randomized multi-task curriculum provides gains for position scoring (57% F1) but shows less consistent benefits across other elements.

Conclusion: Task dependencies between discourse elements are important for AES, and aligning the training curriculum with discourse structure—via sequential fine-tuning—can materially improve performance. Carefully designed curricula allow a relatively small, task-optimized 8B-parameter model to compete with or outperform a much larger 70B-parameter LLM on key AES tasks, suggesting that efficient, specialized models are a practical and cost-effective route for scalable automated assessment. The authors also provide templates and implementation details to support reproducibility and further research on curriculum design in educational NLP.

Abstract: Automated Essay Scoring (AES) systems must judge interdependent discourse elements (e.g., lead, claim, evidence, conclusion), yet most approaches treat these in isolation, harming coherence and generalization. We investigate task-aware fine-tuning of LLaMA-3.1-8B for AES using parameter-efficient LoRA with 4-bit quantization and compare three training curricula: (i) Sequential (progressively fine-tuning on lead, then position, then claim, then evidence, then conclusion), (ii) Independent (task-specific models), and (iii) Randomized (shuffled multi-task). Experiments on the PERSUADE~2.0 corpus show that modeling task dependencies matters: Sequential fine-tuning yields the strongest overall results, including F1 scores of 65% (evidence) and 87% (conclusion) and corresponding accuracies of 63% and 85%, surpassing Independent training and outperforming a general-purpose LLaMA-70B baseline on conclusion despite its far larger capacity. Randomized training improves position scoring (57% F1) but is less consistent elsewhere. These findings indicate that (1) curriculum design aligned with discourse structure can materially improve AES, and (2) small, task-optimized models can be competitive with substantially larger Large Language Models (LLM), offering a practical path to scalable, cost-effective assessment. We release templates and implementation details to facilitate reproduction and future work on curriculum design for educational NLP.
