### [18] [Routing-Aware Expert Calibration for Machine Unlearning in Mixture-of-Experts Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10338)
*Jingyi Xie,Yijun Lin,Yinjiang Xiong,Zhikun Zhang,Sai Li*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper identifies a routing mismatch problem for machine unlearning in Mixture-of-Experts (MoE) LLMs and proposes TRACE, a routing-aware loss reweighting method that improves the forgetting–utility trade-off.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Machine unlearning for large language models is important for complying with data deletion or correction requests, but most methods have been developed for dense architectures. MoE LLMs route tokens to only a few experts via routers, and early evidence suggests that forget data may disproportionately trigger a small subset of experts that are not similarly activated by retain data. This creates a risk that standard unlearning will not sufficiently regularize the experts most responsible for the forget data, degrading either forgetting quality or model utility.

Method: The authors analyze activation patterns in MoE layers and find that forget data and retain data activate experts differently, with certain experts heavily used by forget data but rarely by retain data. They define these as forget-critical experts. TRACE (Targeted Routing-Aware Calibration of Experts) works in two steps: (1) offline, it computes activation statistics over the forget set to detect forget-critical experts at each layer; (2) during unlearning/fine-tuning, it reweights token-level losses on the retain data so that, for these selected experts, the frequency with which retain tokens are routed to them better matches their activation frequencies on forget data. This routing-aware loss calibration aims to provide stronger, better-targeted regularization to the experts most implicated in memorizing forget data, without overly harming overall performance.

Result: On WMDP and MUSE-BOOKS benchmarks with multiple MoE LLMs, TRACE improves the trade-off between forgetting and utility compared to existing unlearning baselines. Under comparable forgetting quality, TRACE achieves about 9% relative improvement in utility over the strongest baseline and obtains the best results on three out of four MUSE-BOOKS evaluation metrics, demonstrating that routing-aware retain loss reweighting effectively addresses the forget–retain mismatch in MoE unlearning.

Conclusion: Routing behavior in MoE LLMs creates a structural challenge for machine unlearning: the experts most activated by forget data may be under-regularized by standard retain-based objectives. TRACE mitigates this by identifying forget-critical experts and reweighting retain losses to align their retain-side and forget-side activation frequencies. This targeted, routing-aware calibration leads to better forgetting–utility trade-offs across benchmarks and models, suggesting that expert-level routing statistics are a key lever for effective unlearning in MoE architectures.

Abstract: Machine unlearning is increasingly important for large language models, yet unlearning in Mixture-of-Experts (MoE) architectures remains underexplored. Unlike dense models, MoE architectures employ a router at each layer to assign each token to a sparse subset of experts. In this work, we observe that forget data often activates a small subset of experts disproportionately, while these experts may receive much weaker activation from retain data. This forget--retain routing mismatch can leave forget-critical experts under-regularized during unlearning. To address this, we propose \textbf{TRACE}, Targeted Routing-Aware Calibration of Experts, for MoE unlearning. TRACE first detects forget-critical experts from offline activation statistics, and then calibrates retain regularization by reweighting token-level retain losses so that each selected expert's retain-side activation frequency better matches its forget-side counterpart. Experiments on WMDP and MUSE-BOOKS across multiple MoE LLMs show that TRACE consistently improves the forget-utility trade-off, yielding a 9\% relative utility improvement over the strongest baseline under comparable forgetting quality and the best performance on three out of four MUSE-BOOKS metrics.

</details>


### [19] [PADD: Path-Aligned Decompression Distillation for Non-Router Teacher to Guide MoE Student Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10369)
*Xinyue Peng,Yi Qian,Jiaojiao Lin,Wenjian Shao,Yanming Liu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces PADD, a distillation framework that converts dense LLMs into efficient MoE students with strong routing policies, achieving equal or better performance at the same inference cost.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Scaling dense large language models is increasingly limited by computation budgets, making it hard to increase model capacity without raising inference cost. Mixture-of-experts architectures offer conditional computation and higher capacity at similar cost, but learning good routing and reliably transferring knowledge from dense teachers to MoE students is non-trivial. The paper aims to address how to systematically distill knowledge from dense teachers into MoE students while simultaneously learning stable, effective routing policies.

Method: The authors propose Path-Aligned Decompression Distillation (PADD), which structures distillation into four stages across two phases. In the initialization phase (Stage I), they perform teacher neuron clustering to identify diverse functional groups in the dense teacher and use this to warm up and specialize the experts in the MoE student. In the training phase (Stages II–IV), they unify three components in a single training pipeline: (1) online adaptive distillation, where the student continually learns from the teacher during training; (2) path-refined policy optimization, which improves the routing decisions (expert selection paths) based on the learned representations; and (3) reward-augmented load balancing, which regularizes routing so experts are used effectively without severe imbalance. Together, these steps decompose and transfer the teacher’s dense computation into well-routed expert pathways in the MoE student.

Result: On mathematical reasoning benchmarks, MoE students trained with PADD outperform strong baseline methods at equivalent inference cost. The MoE students can match or even surpass the performance of their dense teacher models, indicating successful capacity utilization. The experiments further show that PADD enables effective knowledge transfer from the dense teacher to the MoE student and leads to stable and meaningful routing patterns in the expert modules.

Conclusion: PADD provides a practical and effective framework for turning dense LLMs into mixture-of-experts students, enabling higher capacity under fixed compute budgets. By combining structured expert initialization, adaptive distillation, routing-path optimization, and load-balancing rewards, the approach both preserves and enhances teacher performance while keeping inference cost constant. The method stabilizes expert routing and demonstrates that MoE students can be competitive or superior to their dense teachers on challenging reasoning tasks.

Abstract: As large language models (LLMs) continue to scale, it becomes increasingly challenging to grow model capacity under fixed computation budgets. We propose Path-Aligned Decompression Distillation (PADD), a framework for distilling knowledge from dense teachers without explicit routing into mixture-of-experts (MoE) students while learning high-quality routing policies. PADD organizes knowledge distillation into four stages in two phases: an initialization phase (Stage I) that builds diverse functionality in the student's experts through teacher neuron clustering and student-expert warmup, and a training phase (Stages II--IV) that integrates online adaptive distillation, path-refined policy optimization, and reward-augmented load balancing in a single training pipeline. Experiments on mathematical reasoning benchmarks demonstrate that PADD yields substantial gains over strong baselines at the same inference cost and that the MoE student can match or surpass its dense teacher. They also demonstrate effective teacher-to-student knowledge distillation and stable routing behavior.

### [20] [Expert-Level Crisis Detection in Mental Health Conversations](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10380)
*Grace Byun,Abigail Lott,Rebecca Lipschutz,Sean T. Minton,Elizabeth A. Stinson,Jinho D. Choi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper presents CRADLE-Dialogue, a clinician-annotated benchmark and evaluation protocol for detecting and timing crisis-risk signals in multi-turn dialogues, plus a large model and synthetic data that significantly improve performance but still leave the task challenging.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most existing crisis detection research works on static, single-turn texts (e.g., posts, messages) and does not handle the dynamic, evolving nature of real-world crisis conversations. When current models are applied to multi-turn dialogues, their performance degrades because they struggle to track when and how risk signals emerge over time. There is also a clinical need to distinguish early warning signs—when a practitioner should start paying attention—from later turns where a specific crisis is clearly identifiable. The paper aims to fill these gaps by providing a benchmark, evaluation framework, and stronger models tailored to conversational crisis detection.

Method: The authors construct CRADLE-Dialogue, a benchmark of 600 multi-turn dialogues annotated by clinicians at the turn level. Each turn is labeled with multiple clinically grounded risk types (e.g., suicide ideation, self-harm, child abuse) and distinguishes between past versus ongoing risk. They introduce an Alert-Confirm evaluation protocol that separates: (1) early warning turns (Alert) where risk is emerging, and (2) turns where a specific crisis can be explicitly and confidently identified (Confirm). They run experiments with existing models on these tasks and also create a synthetic training corpus to better train large models. Finally, they develop and release a 32B-parameter model trained on these resources and evaluate it on turn-level, dialogue-level, and confirm-only settings, comparing it against open-source and proprietary models.

Result: Empirical results show that detecting the precise moment when risk emerges is considerably more difficult than simply recognizing that some risk exists in the dialogue. Models only achieve Micro F1 scores from the mid-40% to high-60% range on the introduced tasks, indicating substantial room for improvement. The newly trained 32B-parameter model, supported by the synthetic training corpus, substantially outperforms other open-source models and performs competitively—or even better than—proprietary models across turn-level, full-dialogue, and confirm-only evaluation setups.

Conclusion: CRADLE-Dialogue provides a clinically grounded, turn-level benchmark and a novel Alert-Confirm evaluation protocol that better reflects real-world needs for timely crisis detection in conversations. The findings highlight that tracking the emergence of risk over time is a challenging open problem. The released synthetic corpus and 32B-parameter model significantly advance performance but do not solve the task, underscoring the need for further research on conversational crisis detection and early intervention models.

Abstract: Real-world crisis intervention is inherently conversational, yet existing research largely focuses on static texts.Real-world crisis intervention is inherently conversational, yet existing research largely focuses on static texts. When applied to multi-turn dialogues, current models exhibit significant performance degradation, struggling to track risk signals that emerge as context evolves. To address this gap, we introduce CRADLE-Dialogue, a clinician-annotated benchmark for turn-level crisis detection in conversational settings. The dataset features 600 dialogues with multi-label annotations across clinically grounded risks, including suicide ideation, self-harm, and child abuse, distinguishing past from ongoing risk. We further propose an Alert-Confirm evaluation protocol that distinguishes early warning signals (Alert) from turns where a specific crisis becomes explicitly identifiable (Confirm), reflecting the clinical need to intervene before risk becomes explicit. Experiments show that identifying when risk emerges is much harder than recognizing that it exists: models achieve only mid-40% to high-60% Micro F1. Additionally, we release a synthetic training corpus and a 32B-parameter model that substantially outperforms existing open-source models and achieves competitive or superior results against proprietary models across turn-level, dialogue-level, and confirm-only evaluation settings.

### [21] [Do Vision-Language Models See or Guess? Measuring and Reducing Textual-Prior Reliance with a Phrasing-Controlled Benchmark](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10400)
*Pratham Singla,Shivank Garg,Vihan Singh,Paras Chopra*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper shows that many vision-language models rely too much on textual priors instead of actual image content, introduces a new benchmark with multiple question variants per image to measure this, and demonstrates that while all models struggle when text cues are minimized, targeted training can reduce this reliance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Vision-language models are widely used in settings where answers must be grounded in images, but they often exploit textual patterns, question phrasing, and memorized world knowledge. This leads to inflated benchmark performance and ungrounded, overconfident answers. Existing benchmarks, which link each image to only one question, do not clearly separate image-based reasoning from text-based priors. The authors aim to directly measure and characterize how much VLMs truly depend on images versus textual priors, and to explore whether this behavior can be improved.

Method: The authors construct a benchmark of 540 images spanning six reasoning categories and, for each image, create four distinct question variants so that only the wording changes while the underlying visual content remains constant. One variant is deliberately crafted directly from the image to minimize textual leakage and world-knowledge cues, making it the hardest. They evaluate eleven VLMs (both small open-weight and large closed-source systems) on all variants and perform a no-image ablation to estimate a text-only performance floor. They further validate image dependence via three analyses: (1) difficulty assessments by an LLM, (2) checking for low textual similarity between base and final questions, and (3) human re-annotation. They also test in-context learning with exemplars tailored to each variant and apply GRPO post-training to a small VLM to assess whether reliance on textual priors can be mitigated.

Result: All evaluated VLMs perform worse on the hardest question variant that minimizes textual cues, with open-weight models showing the largest drop in accuracy and collapsing toward their text-only performance floor (1–9%) when images are ablated. The additional analyses support that the harder variant truly requires visual grounding rather than just trickier wording. Variant-matched in-context exemplars significantly improve accuracy, and GRPO post-training of a small VLM produces consistent performance gains on all four variants, which also generalize to a held-out, out-of-distribution benchmark.

Conclusion: Vision-language models’ over-reliance on textual priors is quantifiable using a benchmark that controls question phrasing across shared images. When textual cues are minimized, models’ performance drops sharply, especially for open-weight systems, revealing limited true visual grounding. However, the behavior is not fixed: targeted data construction, in-context prompting aligned with variant style, and GRPO-based post-training can measurably reduce textual-prior dependence and improve image-based reasoning, with gains generalizing beyond the training distribution.

Abstract: Vision-language models (VLMs) are increasingly deployed where answers must follow from what is in the image, yet they often answer from textual priors, the question's phrasing together with memorized world knowledge, rather than from the image itself, which inflates benchmark scores and yields confident but ungrounded answers. Existing benchmarks rarely isolate this behavior, since each image is usually paired with a single fixed question. To measure the reliance, we build a 540-image benchmark across six reasoning categories and generate four question variants over the same images, so that phrasing rather than image content is the controlled variable. The hardest variant is written directly from the image to minimize text leakage. We benchmark eleven VLMs spanning small open-weight models to large closed-source systems: every model degrades on the hardest variant, and open models fall furthest. Our central diagnostic is a no-image ablation, which collapses the open-weight models to their text-only floor (1 to 9 percent). Three further analyses, LLM-rated difficulty, low base-to-final textual similarity, and human re-annotation, corroborate genuine image-dependence. In-context exemplars that match how a variant was built recover the most accuracy, and GRPO post-training of a small VLM yields consistent gains across all four variants that transfer to a held-out out-of-distribution set. Textual-prior reliance is measurable and partly trainable away.

### [22] [Harnessing the Collective Intelligence of AI Agents in the Wild for New Discoveries](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10402)
*Federico Bianchi,Yongchan Kwon,Aneesh Pappu,James Zou*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: EinsteinArena is a shared online platform where multiple language-model-based agents collaboratively tackle open math problems with verifiable benchmarks, and have already produced several new state-of-the-art results.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

The paper aims to test whether an open, multi-agent, interactive environment can unlock decentralized, collective AI-driven discovery.

Method: The authors design and deploy EinsteinArena, a platform that hosts a set of open mathematical problems equipped with automatic verifiers, public leaderboards, and problem-specific discussion forums. Multiple autonomous agents can submit candidate solutions, inspect verification feedback, read and post in forums, and build on each other’s approaches over time. The system logs interactions to track how progress emerges from sequences of submissions, discussions, and idea borrowing among agents.

Result: By May 2026, agents using EinsteinArena have produced 12 new state-of-the-art results on the hosted mathematical benchmarks, surpassing all prior human and AI solutions. A highlighted case is the dimension-11 kissing number problem, where the best known lower bound was improved from 593 to 604. Analysis of logs shows that this improvement emerged through iterative, cross-agent interaction involving public submissions, verifier improvements, and reuse of successful ideas, rather than a single isolated run.

Conclusion: The study concludes that open, decentralized interaction among autonomous language-model-based agents can yield genuine collective scientific advances, not just isolated one-off solutions. EinsteinArena exemplifies an “agent-native” research ecosystem, suggesting a promising new paradigm in which AI agents collaborate at scale on verifiable scientific problems and progressively push the frontier of knowledge.

Abstract: Scientific discovery is often a collective process: researchers share partial results, inspect failed attempts, and build on each other's ideas over long time horizons. Recent AI systems have shown that language-model-based agents can make meaningful progress on open scientific problems, but most existing systems operate in isolation. In this paper, we present EinsteinArena, an agent-native platform for open distributed research and discovery. EinsteinArena provides agents with a live set of open problems, each with a solid verifier, public leaderboard, and problem-specific discussion forum where agents can ask questions and share insights. We focus on mathematical tasks that have garnered substantial research interest, where progress can be measured unambiguously. As of May 2026, agents on EinsteinArena have discovered 12 new state-of-the-art results better than any previous human or AI solutions. One notable example is the kissing number problem in dimension 11, where the platform improved the best known lower bound from 593 to 604. This advance did not come from a single agent or isolated run. Rather it arose through a sequence of submissions, public discussion, verifier refinement, and subsequent agent-to-agent borrowing of ideas. These results provide evidence that decentralized scientific discovery can emerge from open interaction among autonomous agents in the wild, demonstrating a new paradigm for collective AI-driven research.

### [23] [KCSAT-ML: Probing Reasoning Models with Nationwide-Cohort Human Difficulty](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10403)
*Sanghee Park,Geewook Kim,Kee-Eung Kim*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They build a math benchmark from 10 years of Korean CSAT exams with human error rates per problem, and propose a new metric (DRG) to see whether models fail on the same items humans find hard, revealing alignment issues not visible from accuracy alone.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing math reasoning benchmarks largely lack fine-grained, empirically grounded difficulty labels per question, making it hard to know whether models struggle on truly hard items or on ones humans find easy, and whether test-time scaling improves alignment with human difficulty.

Method: They assemble KCSAT-ML: 664 Korean CSAT math questions (2014–2025) with a 339-question subset that includes official nationwide human error rates. They evaluate various vision-language models and OCR-fed LLMs on this benchmark and introduce Difficulty-aligned Reasoning Gain (DRG), a metric orthogonal to accuracy which compares model error patterns to human error rates to see where model mistakes concentrate along the human-difficulty spectrum. They also study how test-time scaling (using more tokens/compute per problem) interacts with human difficulty and DRG across model families.

Result: Across models, low-budget accuracy consistently collapses on questions with high human error rates. When applying test-time scaling, token usage increases roughly linearly with human error rate, but accuracy gains are non-monotonic. Within a single model family, test-time scaling can cause anti-scaling (worse performance) on the hardest items and overthinking (unnecessary failures) on easy ones, both symptoms of misalignment with human difficulty. Models with similar overall accuracy can have very different DRG scores: some fail mainly on items humans also find hard, while others solve those yet fail on easy items, highlighting differences hidden by aggregate accuracy.

Conclusion: KCSAT-ML provides a math benchmark with human-grounded difficulty, and DRG offers a complementary lens to accuracy for assessing how model errors align with human difficulty. These tools reveal nuanced failure modes in reasoning and test-time scaling—such as anti-scaling on hard items and overthinking on easy items—that aggregate accuracy obscures. The work argues future model evaluation should consider not only how often models are right, but where and how their mistakes distribute relative to human difficulty, and it releases code and dataset-building tools for the community.

Abstract: Math reasoning benchmarks have proliferated, yet most lack a per-item difficulty signal grounded in actual human performance. We introduce KCSAT-ML, a decade (2014-2025) of Korean College Scholastic Ability Test (KCSAT; Suneung) mathematics: 664 problems with a 339-item core set carrying official per-item error rates from nationwide cohorts of hundreds of thousands of examinees. We pair the benchmark with Difficulty-aligned Reasoning Gain (DRG): a score-orthogonal metric that asks whether a model's mistakes concentrate on the items humans found hard, or on items humans found easy. Together they expose, across a wide range of VLMs (and LLMs via OCR), three patterns: (i) low-budget accuracy collapses on the high-human-error tail at every model size; (ii) test-time scaling (TTS) raises token use roughly linearly with cohort error rate, while accuracy gains follow a non-monotonic curve; (iii) within a single family, TTS flips between anti-scaling on the hardest items and overthinking on easier ones -- two faces of the same alignment failure. On DRG, models with near-identical accuracy can sit at near-opposite values: one model gets wrong what humans also find hard, while another solves the hardest items yet fails on items humans find easy -- a contrast that aggregate accuracy hides. Our code and dataset builder will be open-sourced at https://github.com/naver-ai/KCSAT-ML.

### [24] [WebChallenger: A Reliable and Efficient Generalist Web Agent](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10423)
*Jayoo Hwang,Xiaowen Zhang,Vedant Padwal*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: WebChallenger is a low-cost, open-weight LLM web agent framework that narrows the performance gap with proprietary systems by redesigning the agent architecture instead of using larger models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing autonomous web agents struggle on complex navigation tasks and rely on expensive proprietary reasoning models, which are impractical for repetitive, real-world workloads. The authors believe the limitation lies more in current agent architectures than in model capability, particularly in missing human-like abilities such as selective attention on web pages, memory of site structure, and fluent execution of common interaction patterns.

Method: The authors design WebChallenger, centered on PageMem—a deterministic, DOM-based structured representation that organizes each webpage into a hierarchy of semantic sections with short summaries. On top of PageMem, they implement: (1) a divide-and-conquer observation pipeline that first skims section summaries and then drills into task-relevant areas; (2) an exploration and memory system that builds a reusable map of each website, including page layout and element behaviors, via a one-time traversal; and (3) compound action workflows that bundle frequent multi-step interaction sequences into single higher-level actions that can automatically handle partial state changes. The system uses off-the-shelf open-weight LLMs without additional fine-tuning and is designed to generalize across websites without per-site adapters.

Result: Using only open-weight models, WebChallenger attains 56.3% on WebArena, 48.7% on VisualWebArena, 51.0% on Online-Mind2Web, and 70.9% on WorkArena. These results approach the performance of leading proprietary web agents while using significantly cheaper inference.

Conclusion: Architectural innovations that emulate key human cognitive strategies—selective attention, persistent structural memory, and procedural fluency—can substantially boost web agent performance without scaling up model size or relying on proprietary systems. The PageMem-based WebChallenger framework generalizes across websites and offers a cost-effective path toward capable autonomous web navigation, demonstrating that better agent design can close much of the gap with frontier models.

Abstract: Autonomous web navigation remains challenging for LLM agents, and the strongest generalist systems rely on proprietary reasoning models whose inference cost is prohibitive for the repetitive tasks where such agents would be most useful. We argue this gap stems not from insufficient model capability but from agent architectures that fail to replicate three human cognitive advantages: selective attention to relevant page regions, persistent memory of website structure, and procedural fluency with common interaction patterns. We introduce WebChallenger, a web agent framework that addresses each gap through architecture design rather than model scale, built around PageMem: a structured page representation deterministically constructed from the DOM that exposes each page as a hierarchy of semantic sections with short summaries. On this shared substrate we build three mechanisms that mirror the three cognitive advantages: a divide-and-conquer observation pipeline that lets the agent skim section summaries and extract details only from task-relevant regions; a lightweight exploration and memory system that traverses each website once to build a reusable map of pages and element behaviors; and compound action workflows that collapse common multi-step interactions into single agent actions, handling partial state changes automatically. Because all three operate over PageMem, the framework generalizes across websites without site-specific adapters. Using off-the-shelf open-weight models without fine-tuning, our system achieves 56.3% on WebArena, 48.7% on VisualWebArena, 51.0% on Online-Mind2Web, and 70.9% on WorkArena, approaching frontier proprietary systems at a fraction of the cost. Our code is released at https://github.com/jayoohwang1/webchallenger

Method: The authors construct LakeQA, a benchmark built on roughly 9.5 TB of heterogeneous text resources, including Wikipedia and open-source government data with both structured and unstructured formats. They design questions that require long-horizon, multi-hop reasoning with implicit intermediate steps: systems must first locate the correct documents in the data lake and then integrate evidence from multiple sources. Each example is curated and annotated by at least one Ph.D.-level expert to ensure quality. They then evaluate seven strong LLMs on these tasks using metrics such as exact match accuracy.

Result: Across seven frontier LLMs, performance on LakeQA is low, indicating high task difficulty. Even a leading model (GPT-5.2) attains only 18.37% exact-match accuracy, demonstrating that current systems struggle significantly with the combined demands of search and multi-hop reasoning over massive data lakes.

Conclusion: LakeQA serves as a realistic and challenging benchmark for search-centric QA over large, heterogeneous data lakes. The poor performance of current state-of-the-art LLMs shows substantial headroom for advancing LLM agents that can effectively discover, integrate, and reason over dispersed information in practical data-lake environments.

Abstract: Recent large language models (LLMs) have shown rapid progress in reading-based question answering (QA), where evidence is explicitly provided or can be trivially retrieved. In contrast, real-world questions are often not paired with accurate evidence documents. The useful evidence resides in massive data lakes, making search a prerequisite for answering. However, there is a lack of comprehensive benchmarks that require both searching and reasoning over large data lakes. To this end, we introduce LakeQA, a comprehensive benchmark for search-centric question answering over data lakes that jointly emphasizes searching and reasoning capabilities. LakeQA is built on a heterogeneous collection of approximately 9.5 TB of text resources from Wikipedia and open-source government data, spanning structured and unstructured data. To ensure task quality, each sample is annotated by at least one Ph.D.-level expert. Each task requires long-horizon multi-hop reasoning with implicit intermediate steps: agents need to discover the correct documents and then compose evidence across sources to produce the answer. Experimental results on seven frontier LLMs demonstrate that LakeQA is challenging. For instance, GPT-5.2 achieves only an exact-match score of 18.37% on LakeQA. Overall, LakeQA provides a realistic testbed for developing LLM agents that can both find and analyze data in modern data lakes.

### [27] [Large Language Models as Modal Models in Linguistics](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10467)
*Haruto Suzuki,Saku Sugawara*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper analyzes how large language models contribute to linguistic theory by framing them as modal models that give possible- and, potentially, actual- explanations of human language, arguing their current value lies primarily in ‘how-possibly’ rather than ‘how-actually’ explanations.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: There is intense debate about whether large language models are irrelevant to, replacements for, or tools for linguistic theory, but these positions are often vague. The authors want a clearer, philosophically grounded framework to evaluate what kind of explanations LLMs can really provide about human language and cognition.

Method: The paper uses the philosophy-of-science framework of modal modeling and the mechanistic account of scientific explanation. It classifies existing stances (insulationism, eliminativism, conciliationism), analyzes LLMs as minimal models that can test modal claims, and then examines the mechanistic conditions required for an explanation to count as a ‘how-actually’ explanation of human language.

Result: The authors show that LLMs have genuine epistemic value even without mirroring human cognitive structure: they can serve as minimal models that support how-possibly explanations about language acquisition and competence. However, when the stricter mechanistic criteria for how-actually explanations are applied, current LLMs do not qualify as adequate models of human linguistic cognition.

Conclusion: LLMs’ explanatory power for linguistic theory lies on a continuum between how-possibly and how-actually explanations. Present systems mainly support how-possibly explanations and fall short of full how-actually accounts of human language. This continuum framework avoids both overestimating and underestimating LLMs and provides a more precise way to assess their scientific role in the study of language.

Abstract: The rapid advancement of large language models (LLMs) has intensified debates about their significance for linguistic theory. These debates are commonly divided into three positions: insulationism, which regards LLMs as irrelevant to human language; eliminativism, which claims that LLMs can replace traditional linguistic theories; and conciliationism, which views them as useful tools for linguistic research. To clarify these positions, this paper applies the framework of modal modeling from the philosophy of science. We argue that LLMs possess genuine epistemic value as minimal models, even without structural correspondence to human cognition. In particular, they can provide how-possibly explanations (HPEs) by testing modal claims about language acquisition and linguistic competence. We then examine the conditions under which LLMs could qualify as how-actually explanations (HAEs) of human language, drawing on the mechanistic account of scientific explanation. We argue that current LLMs do not yet satisfy these requirements. On the basis of this analysis, we propose understanding the explanatory power of LLMs as lying on a continuum between HPEs and HAEs. This framework avoids both overstating and understating their explanatory significance and offers a more precise basis for evaluating the role of LLMs in the scientific study of language.

### [28] [Detecting Speculative Language in Biomedical Texts using Recurrent Neural Tensor Networks](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10471)
*Dhruv Dixit*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper evaluates deep learning methods for automatically detecting speculative language in biomedical texts, comparing distributed sentence representations to traditional baselines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Speculative statements in biomedical literature can mislead downstream tasks like information retrieval, summarization, and knowledge discovery if they are treated as factual. Automatically detecting such speculative language helps distinguish tentative claims from established facts, improving the reliability of text-mining pipelines in biomedicine.

Method: The authors frame speculation detection as a sentence-level classification task and explore distributed sentence representations learned via two deep models: Paragraph Vector and Recursive Neural Tensor Network (RNTN). They train and test these models on biomedical articles and compare them to three baselines: Support Vector Machines (with bigram features), Naive Bayes, and a rule-based pattern matching approach. Performance is measured using F1 score, and they analyze why different models perform differently.

Result: The RNTN model slightly outperforms the strongest baseline, a linear bigram SVM, achieving F1 = 0.885 versus 0.881. The Paragraph Vector approach performs poorly (F1 = 0.368), even when trained on a very large unlabeled corpus. Baseline methods, particularly linear SVMs with n-gram features, remain highly competitive.

Conclusion: Deep structured models like RNTN can marginally improve speculative language detection over strong linear baselines, but gains are modest. Simpler models with carefully engineered features still perform very well, whereas generic distributed sentence representations such as Paragraph Vectors may not capture the nuances of speculative language in this domain. The authors discuss factors behind these outcomes and suggest focusing future work on better representation learning tailored to speculation cues and domain-specific characteristics.

Abstract: In this investigation, we delve into the automated detection of speculative language within biomedical articles by utilizing distributed sentence representations and advanced deep learning techniques. The implications of such identification extend to information retrieval, multi-document summarization, and the exploration of new knowledge. Our exploration encompasses two distinct approaches for acquiring distributed sentence representations: the Paragraph Vector model and the Recursive Neural Tensor Network. These methodologies are then rigorously compared against three foundational baseline algorithms: Support Vector Machines, Naive Bayes, and pattern matching. Our findings reveal that the Recursive Neural Tensor Network (RNTN) demonstrates a slight performance edge (F1 = 0.885) over the top-performing baseline, the linear bigram SVM (F1 = 0.881). Meanwhile, the Paragraph Vector model proves less effective (F1 = 0.368), even after extensive training using an expansive, unlabeled dataset. We engage in a comprehensive discourse on the factors influencing these performance disparities and provide insightful recommendations for future research directions.

</details>


### [29] [UniSVQ: 2-bit Unified Scalar-Vector Quantization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10520)
*Haoyu Wang,Haiyan Zhao,Xingyu Yu,Zhangyang Yao,Xu Han,Zhiyuan Liu,Maosong Sun*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They introduce UniSVQ, a 2-bit post-training quantization framework for LLMs that bridges scalar and vector quantization, achieving VQ-like accuracy with SQ-like efficiency.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: 2-bit post-training quantization can greatly reduce the cost and accelerate inference of large language models, but existing scalar quantization loses too much accuracy, while vector quantization is accurate but expensive in computation and storage. There is a need for a quantization scheme that preserves the efficiency and hardware compatibility of scalar methods while approaching the accuracy of vector methods.

Method: They design UniSVQ, a unified 2-bit quantization framework that parameterizes quantization codewords as an affine transformation of integer lattices. This structure maintains compatibility with optimized integer kernels used for fast inference, yet keeps much of the modeling flexibility of vector quantization. On top of this, they add a data-driven, block-wise fine-tuning procedure that directly minimizes the quantization reconstruction error of model weights or activations after quantization.

Result: In experiments across various LLM families and zero-shot benchmarks, UniSVQ consistently surpasses state-of-the-art scalar quantization baselines at 2-bit precision and attains performance close to that of more sophisticated vector quantization methods. At the same time, it delivers higher inference throughput than VQ-based approaches because it is more amenable to efficient integer implementations.

Conclusion: UniSVQ effectively unifies scalar and vector quantization for 2-bit post-training LLM compression, providing a better trade-off between accuracy and efficiency. It retains the hardware and implementation advantages of scalar quantization while recovering much of the accuracy benefits of vector quantization, making it a practical solution for low-cost, high-speed deployment of large language models.

Abstract: Post-training quantization at the 2-bit level enables low-cost deployment and inference acceleration for large language models (LLMs). Scalar quantization (SQ) and vector quantization (VQ) are two primary quantization methods, however, the former suffers from significant performance degradation, and the latter incurs computational and storage overhead. We propose UniSVQ, a unified 2-bit quantization framework that bridges scalar and vector quantization by parameterizing codewords as an affine transform of integer lattices. This structure preserves compatibility with optimized integer kernels while retaining much of VQ's flexibility. We further introduce a data-driven block-wise fine-tuning strategy to directly minimize quantization reconstruction error. Extensive experiments across multiple LLM families and zero-shot benchmarks demonstrate that UniSVQ consistently outperforms state-of-the-art SQ methods and achieves performance comparable to advanced VQ methods, while providing higher inference throughput.

*Haoyu Wang,Xingyu Yu,Haiyan Zhao,Fengxiang Wang,Xu Han*

Main category: cs.CL

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Extremely low-bit (e.g., 2-bit) quantization is vital to deploy large language models efficiently, but existing scalar quantization-based QAT severely degrades performance at 2-bit. Vector quantization has higher capacity and could alleviate this, but standard VQ relies on discrete codebook lookups that block straightforward end-to-end training. A method is needed that combines the representational power of VQ with the trainability and practicality of QAT, especially in low-data regimes.

Method: They propose LC-QAT, a 2-bit weight-only vector-quantization-aware training framework. Quantized weights are expressed as the output of a learned affine mapping applied to discrete code vectors, rather than directly using hard codebook lookups in the forward pass. This formulation provides a strong post-training quantization (PTQ) initialization and keeps the entire optimization differentiable, so training can proceed end-to-end without explicit discrete selection during forward propagation. The approach focuses on being data-efficient and scalable across LLM architectures.

Result: Across multiple large language models, LC-QAT consistently surpasses state-of-the-art scalar-quantization-based QAT techniques at 2-bit precision. It achieves these gains while using only 0.1%–10% of the original training data, showing both accuracy and data efficiency. The method yields high-quality low-bit models suitable for deployment.

Conclusion: LC-QAT successfully bridges vector quantization and QAT for 2-bit weight-only LLMs by using a learned affine mapping over discrete vectors to enable differentiable, end-to-end training. Its strong PTQ-based initialization and high data efficiency make it a practical, scalable solution for deploying extreme low-bit LLMs, outperforming existing QAT baselines in both accuracy and data usage.

Abstract: Quantization-aware training (QAT) is essential for extremely low-bit large language models (LLMs). Current QAT methods are mainly based on scalar quantization (SQ), which enables efficient optimization but suffers from severe performance degradation at 2-bit precision. On the other hand, vector quantization (VQ) provides substantially higher representational capacity, but its discrete codebook lookup prevents end-to-end training. We propose LC-QAT, a 2-bit weight-only VQ-QAT framework that represents quantized weights via a learned affine mapping over discrete vectors, which yields a high-quality PTQ initialization and enables fully differentiable end-to-end optimization without explicit codebook lookup in the training forward pass. This strong post-training initialization makes LC-QAT highly data-efficient. Experiments across diverse LLMs demonstrate that LC-QAT consistently outperforms state-of-the-art QAT methods while using only 0.1%--10% of the training data. Our results establish LC-QAT as a practical and scalable solution for extreme low-bit model deployment.

### [31] [Prefilling-dLLM: Predictive Prefilling for Long-Context Inference in Diffusion Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10537)
*Jing Xiong,Qi Han,Shansan Gong,Yunta Hsieh,Chengyue Wu,Chaofan Tao,Chenyang Zhao,Ngai Wong*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes Prefilling-dLLM, a training-free framework that accelerates diffusion LLMs by sparsely prefilling and caching chunked prefixes, achieving state-of-the-art speed-quality trade-offs for long-context inference.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Diffusion LLMs must re-encode the whole context at every denoising step, making computation scale quadratically with context length and thus impractical for long sequences. There is a need for an efficient, preferably training-free method that reduces this cost while retaining or improving model quality, especially for long-context benchmarks.

Method: The authors introduce Prefilling-dLLM, which splits the input prefix into N chunks, performs a one-time prefill to compute and cache each chunk’s key-value (KV) states, and then, during decoding, selects the top-K most relevant chunks with intra-chunk token sparsity. An attention kernel is designed to parallelize decoding over these non-contiguous cached chunks. Additionally, beginning-of-sequence tokens are prepended to every chunk to act as anchors for attention.

Result: Prefilling-dLLM reduces per-step complexity from quadratic in the full sequence length to quadratic in just the decode length. On LongBench and InfiniteBench, it achieves state-of-the-art performance among dLLM acceleration techniques, and the new attention kernel delivers 9.1–28.0x speedups for contexts from 8K to 32K tokens, while maintaining or improving quality.

Conclusion: Sparse, chunk-based prefilling with relevance-driven chunk selection and intra-chunk sparsity can significantly accelerate diffusion LLMs without additional training, achieving strong performance on long-context tasks and mitigating the lost-in-the-middle issue through periodic BOS anchor tokens.

Abstract: Diffusion large language models (dLLMs) re-encode the entire prefix at every denoising step, causing recomputation that scales
  quadratically with context length and becomes prohibitive for long-context scenarios. We propose Prefilling-dLLM, a training-free
  prefill-decode disaggregation framework for dLLMs that partitions the prefix into N chunks, caches their KV representations once,
  and selects the top-K most relevant chunks with intra-chunk token sparsity for decoding, showing that sparse prefilling can
  outperform dense attention while reducing per-step complexity from quadratic in the full sequence length to quadratic only in the
  decode length. On LongBench and InfiniteBench, Prefilling-dLLM achieves state-of-the-art quality among dLLM acceleration methods,
  and an attention kernel that parallelizes decoding over the non-contiguously cached chunk KV yields 9.1--28.0x speedup at 8K--32K
  contexts. We further show that beginning-of-sequence tokens prepended to each chunk act as periodic attention anchors that eliminate
  the lost-in-the-middle phenomenon. Code is available at https://github.com/menik1126/Prefilling-dLLM.

For methods like ROME and FT, there is a performance drop of up to 24% when moving from direct edits to entailed knowledge queries.

Conclusion: Current knowledge editing techniques are not semantics-aware enough: they update surface facts but fail to ensure that all logically entailed knowledge is updated consistently. A new generation of benchmarks that focus on logical consequences, like the one proposed, is needed to properly evaluate and drive progress in knowledge editing for LLMs.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) are increasingly deployed in real-world applications that require access to up-to-date knowledge. However, retraining LLMs is computationally expensive. Therefore, knowledge editing techniques are crucial for maintaining current information and correcting erroneous assertions within pre-trained models. Current benchmarks for knowledge editing primarily focus on recalling edited facts, often neglecting their logical consequences. To address this limitation, we introduce a new benchmark designed to evaluate how knowledge editing methods handle the logical consequences of a single fact edit. Our benchmark extracts relevant logical rules from a knowledge graph for a given edit. Then, it generates multi-hop questions based on these rules to assess the impact on logical consequences. Our findings indicate that while existing knowledge editing approaches can accurately insert direct assertions into LLMs, they frequently fail to inject entailed knowledge. Specifically, experiments with popular methods like ROME and FT reveal a substantial performance gap, up to 24%, between evaluations on directly edited knowledge and on entailed knowledge. This highlights the critical need for semantics-aware evaluation frameworks in knowledge editing.

### [33] [Hidden Consensus:Preference-Validity Compression in Human Feedback](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10569)
*Dorcas Chia Ern Chua,Karen Myn Hui Lee,Jia Yue Tan,Zhen Xue Gue,Norzalena Abdul Hamid,Azima Binti Azmi,Keat Mei Yeong,Aizat Izyani binti Mujab,Hafsah Noor Azam,Chee Guo Khoo,Han Ying Lim,Chee Seng Chan*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper critiques standard RLHF aggregation for collapsing diverse but valid human preferences into a single scalar reward, showing this mis-measures alignment in plural societies and proposing a validity-preserving alternative.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard RLHF pipelines aggregate heterogeneous human judgments into a single scalar reward, implicitly treating disagreement as noise. In structurally plural societies, however, disagreements often arise from legitimate, culturally or contextually grounded interpretive frames. This reduction risks mis-measuring alignment by erasing plural-valid responses and privileging an artificial single ‘winner’ preference. The authors are motivated to diagnose and formalize this problem, which they call Preference-Validity Compression, and to show empirically that it matters for alignment measurement, especially in plural contexts like Malaysia.

Method: The authors analyze an RLHF-style feedback aggregation process using Malaysia as a diagnostic setting. They construct "preference events" that link prompts, model responses, and human acceptability judgments across different interpretive frames. With 321 preference events from 20 participants and 107 prompts annotated by trios, they examine how many prompts have more than one majority-supported response, how dominance gaps change when multiple majority-supported options are included, and how often participants deem multiple responses acceptable. They qualitatively inspect discarded responses to show they are grounded in coherent local, practical, or cultural frames rather than being mere noise.

Result: Empirically, 79% of prompts have more than one majority-supported response that standard single-winner aggregation would discard. When all majority-supported options are considered, the apparent dominance gaps between top responses shrink, undermining the impression that there is a clear best response. Participants often select multiple responses as acceptable, and the responses that would be discarded by majority aggregation systematically reflect coherent local, practical, or cultural interpretive frames. Together, these results show that standard majority aggregation is effectively measuring argmax acceptability (the single most preferred option) rather than capturing a broader notion of plural alignment.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that majority-based aggregation in RLHF leads to Preference-Validity Compression, a measurement-validity failure where multiple plural-valid responses are collapsed into a single optimization target. This practice mis-measures alignment in structurally plural societies by discarding valid interpretations as if they were noise. The authors argue that future alignment methods should satisfy Validity-Preserving Consistency: systems should remain stable across plural-valid interpretive frames and avoid collapsing them into a single scalar reward. Alignment evaluation and training pipelines must therefore be redesigned to respect and represent pluralism in human judgments, rather than optimizing for a single argmax preference.

Abstract: Standard RLHF pipelines often reduce heterogeneous human judgments into a single scalar reward target. We argue that this reduction can mis-measure alignment in structurally plural societies, where disagreement may reflect culturally, historically, linguistically, regionally, or normatively grounded interpretations rather than annotation noise. We call this failure Preference-Validity Compression, the collapse of multiple plural-valid response options into a single optimization target. Using Malaysia as a diagnostic setting, we analyze RLHF-style feedback aggregation through preference events linking prompts, responses, and acceptability judgments across interpretive frames. Across 321 preference events from 20 participants and 107 trio-annotated prompts, 79% of prompts contain more than one majority-supported response that single-winner aggregation would discard, and apparent dominance gaps between top responses diminish when all majority-supported options are considered. Participants frequently select multiple acceptable responses, and discarded responses demonstrably reflect coherent local, practical, or cultural frames. These findings show that majority aggregation in this corpus measures argmax acceptability rather than plural alignment. We treat this as a measurement-validity issue and argue that future alignment methods should satisfy Validity-Preserving Consistency, remaining stable across plural-valid interpretive frames rather than collapsing them into a single reward target.

</details>


### [34] [ParaBridge: Bridging Paralinguistic Perception and Dialogue Behavior in Speech Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10581)
*Yuxiang Wang,Qinke Ni,Shengbo Cai,Wan Lin,Liqiang Zhang,Zhizheng Wu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: ParaBridge is a self-distillation method that converts fragile, prompt-based use of paralinguistic speech cues into robust, baked-in behavior of Speech Language Models, greatly improving safety- and empathy-related responses without hurting general capabilities.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Speech includes paralinguistic information (speaker identity, emotion, background conditions) that should influence how dialogue systems respond. Existing Speech Language Models can perceive these cues but, in open-ended dialogue, largely fail to use them reliably. Simple inference-time prompt scaffolds can encourage models to act on these cues, but such approaches are brittle in multi-turn conversation and under conflicting instructions, and they don't scale as a stable behavior. There is a need for a training method that makes sensitivity to paralinguistic information an intrinsic, reliable property of the model, without depending on curated labels or expensive reward models.

Method: The authors introduce ParaBridge, an on-policy self-distillation framework. First, they design a paralinguistic instruction scaffold used only as a privileged view at training time. For each training example, there are two views of the same input: (1) a scaffold-free model that produces its own rollout (on-policy responses), and (2) a scaffold-augmented view that uses explicit prompts to encourage proper use of paralinguistic cues. The scaffolded view provides dense, full-vocabulary next-token targets (soft supervision) along the trajectory followed by the scaffold-free model. The base model is then trained to match these scaffolded targets, effectively distilling the scaffold’s behavior into the model so it can respond appropriately to paralinguistic cues without requiring the scaffold at inference. No curated dialogues, human annotations, or external reward models are needed, since the supervision comes from the model’s own scaffolded behavior.

Result: Applied to the Qwen3-Omni-thinking Speech Language Model, ParaBridge substantially boosts the model’s responsiveness to paralinguistic cues without harming its general capabilities. On VoxSafeBench, a safety and awareness benchmark, the scaffold-free SAR score increases from 14.6% to 40.3%. On EchoMind, an empathy and alignment evaluation, the average rating improves from 3.27 to 3.92. Meanwhile, general-purpose performance remains essentially unchanged: scores on MMAU-Pro, VoiceBench, and GPQA stay within 0.4 points of the original model. ParaBridge also shows out-of-distribution robustness: it generalizes to paralinguistic cues not seen during training, transfers from safety-focused training to empathy-oriented dialogue tasks, and can be applied successfully to a different Speech Language Model backbone.

Conclusion: ParaBridge effectively bridges the gap between perception and behavior in Speech Language Models by converting fragile, prompt-based paralinguistic conditioning into stable, intrinsic model behavior. By using a scaffold only as a temporary privileged teacher and distilling its signals into the base model via on-policy self-distillation, the method enables SLMs to reliably integrate non-lexical speech cues (such as emotion, speaker characteristics, and acoustic context) in their responses. This is achieved without curated datasets or external rewards, while preserving general capabilities and demonstrating strong generalization to new cues, conversational goals (e.g., empathy), and model architectures.

Abstract: Speech carries more information than just words: a child's voice, a fearful tone, or a noisy background should all lead a sufficiently competent spoken-dialogue assistant to different replies. Current Speech Language Models (SLMs) can recognize such paralinguistic cues but often ignore them in open-ended dialogue. We observe that a simple paralinguistic instruction scaffold at the inference stage narrows this perception-behavior gap, suggesting that the relevant cues are already latent in the model. Such scaffolds, however, remain brittle under multi-turn context and competing instructions. Therefore, we propose \textbf{ParaBridge}, an on-policy self-distillation method that turns a brittle inference-time scaffold into stable model behavior. During training, the scaffold serves only as a temporary privileged view; the scaffold-free model rolls out its own response, while the scaffolded view supplies dense, full-vocabulary next-token targets along its trajectory. This supervision teaches when non-lexical cues should affect the reply without the need for curated dialogues, human labels, or external reward models. On Qwen3-Omni-thinking, ParaBridge raises scaffold-free VoxSafeBench SAR from $14.6\%$ to $40.3\%$ and improves EchoMind average rating from $3.27$ to $3.92$. It also preserves general ability, with MMAU-Pro, VoiceBench, and GPQA all within $0.4$ points of the original model. Beyond the training distribution, ParaBridge generalizes to unseen paralinguistic cues, transfers from safety-oriented training to empathy-oriented dialogue, and works on a different SLM backbone.

</details>

*Eitan Cohen,Idan Simai,Uri Shaham*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Proposes SDBN, an adversarial-training-based, parameter-efficient fine-tuning framework that improves robustness and data efficiency of foundation models, with two discrete-uncertainty variants for character edits and LLM-generated variants, achieving strong gains in noisy and low-resource settings with minimal extra cost.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing PEFT methods are effective for adapting large foundation models but are fragile when training data is scarce and inputs are noisy, which is common in real-world NLP. Robust training and adversarial methods are known to help robustness, yet they have been underexplored in the PEFT context. The paper aims to close this gap by combining adversarial robustness with parameter-efficient adaptation without sacrificing efficiency.

Method: Introduce SDBN, a generic framework that integrates adversarial training into PEFT. The core idea is to optimize PEFT parameters against worst-case perturbations of the input within an uncertainty set. Two discrete variants are proposed: (1) SDBN-h, which constructs a finite set of character-level perturbations (e.g., edits/typos), uses gradients to score them, and trains on worst-case variants; and (2) SDBN-p, which leverages LLM-generated paraphrases or variants as the uncertainty set for robust optimization in generative tasks. The approach is designed to plug into existing PEFT techniques without adding new trainable parameters and with limited additional compute.

Result: Across multiple benchmarks and tasks, SDBN-based PEFT models consistently outperform standard PEFT and other baselines, especially in low-resource regimes and when evaluated under word-level or character-level noise and corruptions. Both SDBN-h and SDBN-p yield notable gains in robustness and generalization with modest computational overhead.

Conclusion: Adversarial training can be effectively combined with parameter-efficient fine-tuning to yield models that are more robust and better generalized under data scarcity and noisy inputs. SDBN offers a unified, plug-and-play framework that strengthens PEFT without increasing parameter count and with only small extra computational cost, making it attractive for practical, robust PEFT deployments in realistic NLP settings.

Abstract: Parameter-Efficient Fine-Tuning (PEFT) has become essential for adapting foundation models to downstream NLP tasks. However, current PEFT methods often struggle with robustness to noise and performance degradation on limited training data. We propose SDBN (Small Data Big Noise), a unified framework that brings adversarial training to PEFT - a combination that remains less studied in the PEFT setting despite its complementary strengths - to enhance model robustness and generalization, outperforming alternative approaches. We also introduce two variants of the method that use discrete uncertainty sets: SDBN-h, which enumerates character-level edits and selects worst-case variants using gradients, and SDBN-p, which uses LLM-generated variants for robust optimization in generative tasks. Experiments across multiple benchmarks reveal substantial improvements, particularly in low-resource settings and under both word-level and character-level corruptions. This framework addresses the less explored intersection of adversarial training and parameter-efficient adaptation, without introducing additional parameters or only modest computational overhead, making PEFT deployments more reliable in real-world scenarios where data scarcity and linguistic variability often coexist

### [36] [Dynamic Linear Attention](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10650)
*Xin Wang,Hui Shen,Boyuan Zheng,Xueshen Liu,Minkyoung Cho,Zhongwei Wan,Zesen Zhao,Zhuoqing Mao,Shen Yan,Mi Zhang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: DLA is a dynamic multi-state linear attention framework that adaptively merges memory states based on information change and capacity constraints, achieving better long-context performance than prior linear attention methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard attention has quadratic complexity, making it impractical for very long contexts. Linear attention reduces complexity but loses representation capacity, especially when using multi-state memory with fixed, heuristic merging policies that ignore token importance. These fixed policies can permanently obscure important tokens and lead to error accumulation over long sequences. The paper aims to design a scalable linear attention mechanism that retains strong representational fidelity over long contexts by adapting memory management to the information content of tokens and controlling memory growth effectively.

Method: The authors propose DLA, a dynamic memory modeling framework for multi-state linear attention. It has two main components: (1) Information-Aware Dynamic State Merging, which monitors token-level information variation to decide where to place boundaries between memory states, preserving fine-grained states around semantic transitions and aggressively merging tokens in low-variation (stable) regions; and (2) Capacity-Bounded Memory Modeling, which enforces a fixed-size, time-ordered state cache. When the cache is full, DLA selectively merges adjacent low-information states to keep memory within budget while minimizing information loss. DLA is integrated into existing linear attention architectures and pre-trained end-to-end.

Result: DLA is implemented on top of two different baseline linear attention models and pre-trained with long-context data. It is then evaluated on 16 benchmarks spanning three task categories (likely long-context language modeling, question answering, and reasoning or retrieval-style tasks). Across these benchmarks, DLA consistently outperforms state-of-the-art multi-state linear attention methods, demonstrating improved effectiveness in handling long sequences without incurring quadratic cost.

Conclusion: Dynamic, information-aware memory management significantly enhances multi-state linear attention for long-context LLMs. By adapting state boundaries to token-level information change and imposing a capacity-bounded cache with selective merging of low-information states, DLA mitigates error accumulation and preserves critical details over long sequences. The framework is model-agnostic, integrates with existing linear attention architectures, and empirically yields superior performance on a broad suite of long-context benchmarks compared to prior state-of-the-art methods.

Abstract: The scalability of Large Language Models (LLMs) to long contexts is fundamentally constrained by the quadratic complexity of standard attention, motivating the adoption of linear attention mechanisms with sub-quadratic cost. To improve representation capacity under long contexts, recent approaches organize memory in a multi-state manner. However, existing multi-state linear attention methods rely on fixed state merging policies that cannot adapt to dynamically varying token importance, irreversibly obscuring critical tokens and causing severe error accumulation over long sequences. To address this limitation, we propose DLA, a dynamic memory modeling framework for multi-state linear attention. DLA introduces (i) Information-Aware Dynamic State Merging, which adaptively determines state boundaries based on token-level information variation, preserving high-resolution representations around semantic transitions while aggressively summarizing stable regions, and (ii) Capacity-Bounded Memory Modeling, which maintains a fixed-size, chronologically ordered state cache by selectively merging adjacent low-information states to control memory growth with minimal information loss. We pre-train DLA on two different linear attention models and evaluate on 16 datasets across three categories. Experimental results demonstrate the superiority of DLA over state-of-the-art.

</details>


### [37] [Speaker Group Encoding in Self-supervised Speech Recognition Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10654)
*Felix Herron,Solange Rossato Alexandre Allauzen,Benoit Favre,François Portet*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies what self-supervised speech models internally represent about different speaker groups and how fine-tuning for speaker ID, ASR, and fairness-aware ASR changes those representations.

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand and control how self-supervised speech recognition models encode sensitive speaker group attributes (e.g., gender, age, dialect, ethnicity, nativeness), which is crucial for diagnosing and improving fairness in ASR systems.

Method: Analyze multiple stages of self-supervised speech models: pretrained, fine-tuned for speaker identification, fine-tuned for ASR, and ASR fine-tuned with a fairness-enhancing algorithm. ASR fine-tuning suppresses phonetic-variant group information while preserving semantic-variant information. Abstract: We investigate what self-supervised speech recognition models (S3Ms) learn about speaker groups (SGs). We examine several states of S3Ms: pretrained, finetuned on speaker identification (SID), finetuned on automatic speech recognition (ASR), and ASR-finetuned using a fairness enhancing algorithm. We find that S3Ms encode information about several speaker group categories (SGCs), including their gender, age, dialect, ethnicity, and whether they are a native speaker. We find that finetuning for SID amplifies certain SGCs, namely those whose variance is more phonetic in nature, though it does not amplify other SGCs, namely those whose variance is more semantic in nature. On the other hand, finetuning for ASR discards phonetically variant speaker group information (SGI) but retains semantically variant SGI. We find that ASR algorithms designed for fairness improvement change to what extent SGI is encoded in S3Ms; however, this is primarily true for for phonetically variant SGCs, and less true for semantically variant SGCs. We discuss how SGI is encoded by each layer, and identify subdimensions of embeddings responsible for encoding different SGCs. Finally, we discuss how our findings could be beneficial in designing fairer ASR algorithms.

### [38] [Are We Evaluating Knowledge or Phrasing? Mitigating MCQA Sensitivity with ParaEval](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10657)
*João Maria Janeiro,Mathurin Videau,Andrea Caciolai,Benjamin Piwowarski,Patrick Gallinari,Loic Barrault*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper shows that standard multiple-choice evaluation of language models using log-likelihood is unstable due to sensitivity to answer phrasing, and introduces ParaEval, which uses multiple paraphrases per option to better measure true capability.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current MCQA benchmarks evaluate models via log-likelihood of answer options, but scores change significantly when the wording of those options is paraphrased. This means evaluations can reflect surface-form familiarity instead of genuine understanding, leading to misleading performance comparisons, especially when models share the same underlying knowledge.

Method: The authors build a controlled testbed of 1B–8B parameter models trained on identical data, then evaluate them on MCQA tasks using standard log-likelihood scoring while varying the surface form of answers. They quantify the artificial performance gaps created solely by paraphrasing. They then propose ParaEval: for each answer option, they generate multiple paraphrases, query the model with all of them, and score each model using the most favorable phrasing for each option. They apply this framework to both the controlled small models and larger 70B and 120B open-source models.

Result: Even though the 1B–8B models share identical knowledge, standard MCQA metrics show over 2 percentage points difference in performance caused purely by surface-form variation. When evaluated with ParaEval, this false performance gap shrinks to below 1 point. Similar surface-form artifacts and corresponding improvements from ParaEval are observed when applying the method to larger 70B and 120B models.

Conclusion: Standard log-likelihood-based MCQA evaluation overestimates or underestimates model ability depending on superficial answer phrasing, conflating phrase familiarity with actual competence. ParaEval, by aggregating over multiple paraphrases and using the best phrasing per model, yields more robust, fair, and efficient evaluations that better reflect true underlying capabilities. The framework scales from smaller to frontier-sized models and can substantially reduce evaluation artifacts.

Abstract: Multiple-choice (MCQA) benchmarks are the standard for evaluating pretrained large language models, but their reliance on log-likelihood scoring makes them unreliable. Specifically, standard scores are highly sensitive to the exact phrasing (surface form) of the answers, conflating a model's familiarity with a specific phrase with its actual capability. We demonstrate this flaw using a controlled testbed of 1B-8B models trained on the same knowledge. Despite having identical knowledge, standard metrics falsely report a performance gap of over 2 points. To solve this, we propose ParaEval, an evaluation framework that queries models using multiple paraphrases per answer option. By scoring each model based on its most favorable phrasing, ParaEval successfully reduces the false performance gap to below 1 point. We confirm that these evaluation artifacts, and the improvements from ParaEval, persist in frontier 70B and 120B open-source models. Ultimately, ParaEval provides a robust and efficient way to evaluate true underlying capability rather than surface-form familiarity.

</details>


### [39] [Multilingual Word-Level Forced Alignment with Self-Supervised Representations and Learned Dynamic Programming](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10675)
*Roy Weber,Meidan Zehavi,Rotem Rousso,Joseph Keshet*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes a new multilingual word-level forced alignment method that fuses MMS speech model features and an unsupervised phoneme boundary detector, and uses a learned dynamic programming decoder to produce accurate word boundaries across many languages.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing forced alignment tools like Montreal Forced Aligner (MFA) and MMS-based methods struggle with accuracy, robustness, and scalability across many languages, especially when training data is limited. The authors aim to build a more accurate, multilingual, and data-efficient word-level forced aligner that can leverage powerful self-supervised speech representations and unsupervised boundary detection while generalizing to hundreds of languages supported by MMS without retraining.

Method: They design a two-stage architecture: (1) an alignment encoder that jointly uses representations from the Massively Multilingual Speech (MMS) model and from UnSupSeg, a self-supervised phoneme boundary detector, learning to fuse these signals and predict word-boundary probabilities over long temporal windows; (2) an alignment decoder that is itself a learned dynamic programming module, combining the encoder’s boundary probabilities with segment-level features computed over MMS and UnSupSeg representations to infer optimal word boundary positions. The system is trained iteratively using TIMIT and Buckeye corpora to refine both the encoder’s fusion and the decoder’s boundary inference, and then evaluated on both seen (TIMIT, Buckeye) and unseen (Dutch, German, Hebrew) languages.

Result: On English datasets (TIMIT and Buckeye), the proposed method outperforms Montreal Forced Aligner and MMS-only alignment baselines in word boundary accuracy. For unseen languages—Dutch, German, and Hebrew—the model achieves performance that is either consistently better than or competitive with existing alignment approaches. This demonstrates robustness and good zero-shot or cross-lingual transfer without language-specific retraining.

Conclusion: A fusion-based encoder with MMS and UnSupSeg features, coupled with a learned dynamic programming decoder, yields a highly accurate and multilingual word-level forced aligner. Its strong performance on both training languages and unseen languages suggests it can scale effectively to the 1100+ languages covered by MMS without additional training, offering a practical and general solution for large-scale multilingual forced alignment tasks.

Abstract: We present a method for accurate multilingual word-level forced alignment, consisting of an alignment encoder and a learned alignment decoder. The encoder integrates two representations: one from the Massively Multilingual Speech (MMS) model and another from a self-supervised phoneme boundary detector (UnSupSeg). It learns to fuse them and to estimate word-boundary probabilities over long temporal contexts. The alignment decoder is a learned dynamic programming that combines encoder outputs with segmental features over the MMS and UnSupSeg representations to infer final word boundaries. Trained iteratively on TIMIT and Buckeye, the proposed approach outperforms Montreal Forced Aligner (MFA) and MMS-based alignment on both datasets. On unseen languages (Dutch, German, and Hebrew), the proposed model achieves performance consistently better than or on par with existing alignment approaches, indicating its potential to scale to 1100+ languages supported by MMS without further training.
