### [40] [REAL: A Reasoning-Enhanced Graph Framework for Long-Term Memory Management of LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10694)
*Keer Lu,Liwei Chen,Guoqing Jiang,Zhiheng Qin,Yunhuai Liu,Wentao Zhang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: REAL is a long-term memory system for LLMs that represents conversations as temporal, confidence-aware property graphs and uses advanced, query-guided retrieval to better track and exploit evolving facts across long interactions.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are expected to hold coherent, personalized multi-session conversations, but their finite context window prevents them from remembering all past interactions. Existing external memory solutions either store memories as flat text, losing explicit relations, or as structured stores that overwrite evolving facts, and their retrieval is often passive and not tailored to the query when information is incomplete. The paper aims to build a more faithful, flexible long-term memory representation and a smarter retrieval mechanism that can handle temporal evolution, uncertainty, and incomplete evidence.

Method: The paper proposes REAL, which models long-term conversational memory as a temporal and confidence-aware directed property graph. Each atomic fact is decomposed into entities and relations, annotated with valid-time intervals, confidence scores, and exploration intent labels. For memory writing, REAL uses a non-destructive temporal update strategy that keeps multiple versions of a fact along with their validity intervals instead of overwriting them. For memory reading, REAL first anchors entities relevant to the query, separates their exploration intents, and then runs a semantic evaluator-guided hybrid beam search to extract a compact, relevant memory subgraph. It additionally performs counterfactual inference to repair unreliable retrieval states and infer missing evidence through implicit logical relations.

Result: In experiments comparing against flat-text memory, other graph-based approaches, and existing memory baselines, REAL achieves significantly better performance on long-term memory tasks, with an average improvement of 22.72% over baselines. The results indicate more accurate recall and use of historical information in long-range conversational scenarios.

Conclusion: Representing conversational memory as a temporal, confidence-aware property graph and using non-destructive updates plus query-guided, inference-augmented retrieval substantially improves long-term memory handling for LLMs. REAL can better track evolving facts, manage uncertainty, and actively recover missing evidence, leading to notably improved performance over prior long-term memory systems.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) are increasingly expected to interact with users over long time horizons. However, due to their finite context window, LLMs cannot retain all past interactions, making long-term memory management essential for storing, updating, and retrieving historical information beyond the context limit. Although recent memory systems attempt to address this issue by storing historical information externally, existing approaches suffer from three key limitations: flat text-based memory organizations fail to capture explicit relations among memories, structured memory systems often destructively overwrite evolving facts, and current retrieval mechanisms remain query-agnostic and passive when evidence is incomplete. REAL constructs long-term conversational memory as a temporal and confidence-aware directed property graph, where each atomic fact is represented with entities, relations, valid-time intervals, confidence scores, and exploration intent labels. During memory construction, REAL adopts a non-destructive temporal update strategy that preserves parallel fact versions and their validity intervals, enabling faithful tracking of fact evolution. During retrieval, REAL anchors query-relevant root entities, decouples their exploration intents, and performs semantic evaluator-guided hybrid beam search to extract compact memory subgraphs. It further incorporates counterfactual inference to repair unreliable retrieval states and recover missing memory evidence through implicit logical relations. Comprehensive experiments demonstrate that REAL substantially improves long-term memory performance over flat-text, graph-based, and existing memory baselines, achieving an average improvement of 22.72\%.

### [41] [Attention Expansion: Enhancing Keyphrase Extraction from Long Documents with Attention-Augmented Contextualized Embeddings](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10716)
*Roberto Martínez-Cruz,Alvaro J. López-López,José Portela*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose a lightweight “attention expansion” mechanism that lets normal PLMs use information from nearby, out-of-window text chunks (via static word embeddings) to better extract keyphrases from long documents, improving F1 across models and datasets without expensive long-context LLMs.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard PLMs have limited context windows, so for long documents the evidence for good keyphrases can be scattered across sections that the model cannot see jointly. Long-context LLMs could in principle fix this but are too computationally expensive for practical, high-throughput keyphrase extraction. The authors want a method that keeps the efficiency of regular PLMs while still leveraging information from a broader textual context in long documents.

Method: They introduce an attention expansion mechanism that augments each token’s representation inside a PLM with information from surrounding chunks of the document that lie outside the model’s current context window. Instead of adding full-document self-attention or using large long-context LLMs, they summarize these out-of-context chunks using pre-trained word embeddings and feed this as additional information to the PLM, effectively expanding its receptive field in a lightweight way. They plug this mechanism into multiple PLM backbones and training regimes for keyphrase extraction.

Result: Across five different PLM backbones (general-purpose, scientific, task-specific, and long-context encoders), two training setups, and five benchmark datasets from scientific and news domains, the attention expansion consistently improves keyphrase extraction performance, in particular F1 scores, over strong baselines and existing state-of-the-art models. The gains are observed even when using domain-specific or already long-context models, indicating that the method adds complementary signal rather than simply mimicking longer input windows.

Conclusion: Attention expansion is an efficient and broadly applicable strategy for long-document keyphrase extraction: by augmenting PLM token representations with out-of-window contextual signals derived from pre-trained word embeddings, it consistently boosts performance without the computational overhead of full-document attention or long-context LLMs, and it remains beneficial even for specialized or long-context encoders.

Abstract: Pre-trained language models (PLMs) have achieved strong performance in keyphrase extraction (KPE), largely due to their ability to generate rich contextualized representations. However, long-document KPE remains challenging because salient keyphrase evidence may be scattered across distant document sections that cannot be jointly captured within the limited context window of most PLMs. Although long-context large language models (LLMs) can process broader textual contexts, their computational cost limits their practicality for efficient and high-throughput KPE. To overcome this limitation, we propose an attention expansion mechanism that augments PLM token representations with information from surrounding out-of-context chunks using pre-trained word embeddings. The proposed mechanism expands the effective contextual scope of PLM-based KPE models without requiring full-document attention or expensive LLM-based inference. We evaluate our approach across five PLM backbones, including general-purpose, scientific, task-specific, and long-context encoders, using two training regimes and five benchmark corpora from scientific and news domains. Experimental results demonstrate that attention expansion consistently enhances KPE performance across all evaluation settings, outperforming state-of-the-art models and yielding notable improvements in F1 score. The improvements extend to domain-specific, task-specialized, and native long-context models, showing that the proposed mechanism provides complementary information rather than merely compensating for limited input length. These results establish attention expansion as an efficient and effective strategy for long-document KPE.

### [42] [Continual LLM Upcycling: A Predictor-Gated Bank-Wise Sparsity Training Recipe for Dense-to-Sparse LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10722)
*Ruixuan Huang,Jinyuan Shi,Hantao Huang,Yifan Huang,Ziyi Guan,Hao Zeng,Ian En-Hsu Yen,Minghui Yu*

TL;DR: The paper proposes a dense-to-sparse continual training approach to turn a dense Qwen2.5-8B model into a channel-sparse LLM, by inserting and training a predictor-gated sparse SwiGLU FFN during a 32K-context extension stage and analyzing its behavior and failure modes.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Large dense language models are computationally expensive at inference time, especially at long context lengths. Hardware-efficient sparse models are desirable, but post-hoc sparsification methods often underperform because sparsity is not integrated into the main training path. The authors want a way to "upcycle" existing dense checkpoints into sparse, hardware-friendly models while extending context length, without retraining from scratch, and to understand any long-context failure modes that arise.

Method: Starting from a dense Qwen2.5-8B backbone, the authors perform continual training at 32K context length and, in that phase, replace the FFN with a sparse SwiGLU FFN that is gated by a learned routing predictor. For each token and layer, a low-rank predictor network outputs routing logits over FFN channels. Channels are organized into 64-channel banks, and a bank-wise top-k rule activates only 16 channels per bank, achieving 4x sparsity in intermediate FFN activations. The routing predictor is trained end-to-end as part of the main LM objective, so sparsity is optimized jointly with language modeling. They document architecture, training recipe, and benchmarks, and also analyze long-context behavior on RULER-CWE, identifying a layer-local failure pattern and proposing a single-layer repair algorithm to fix performance in the problematic length range.

Result: The resulting model achieves a 4x sparse FFN activation pattern while being derived from an existing dense Qwen2.5-8B checkpoint via continual training. Benchmarks show that the upcycled sparse model maintains competitive performance while being more hardware-efficient. Analysis on RULER-CWE reveals a specific long-context failure mode that is localized to certain layers, and the proposed single-layer repair algorithm substantially improves performance on the affected context length range.

Conclusion: Dense-to-sparse continual training with a predictor-gated sparse SwiGLU FFN is an effective way to convert dense LLM checkpoints into hardware-oriented sparse models during a context extension stage. Integrating the routing module into the main training path yields better-optimized sparsity than post-hoc pruning approaches. Moreover, careful analysis of long-context benchmarks can uncover layer-local failure modes, which can be mitigated with targeted, single-layer repair strategies, leading to robust long-context sparse LLMs.

Abstract: We study dense-to-sparse continual training as a way to construct channel-sparse large language models from dense checkpoints. Starting from a Qwen2.5-8B dense backbone, we continue training at 32K context and introduce a predictor-gated sparse SwiGLU FFN in the 32K stage. For each token and layer, we use a low-rank predictor to produce FFN-channel routing logits. We then apply a bank-wise top-k rule to retain 16 channels in every 64-channel bank, yielding 4x sparsity in the FFN intermediate activation. Unlike post-hoc sparse inference methods, the routing module is placed on the main language modeling path and optimized during continual training, enabling the dense model to be upcycled into a hardware-oriented sparse model. We report the architecture, training recipe, benchmark performance, and training lessons. We also identify a layer-local long-context failure mode on RULER-CWE and propose a single-layer repair algorithm that substantially improves the affected length range.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Existing analytics often ignore rich conversational logs and are not tightly aligned with course concepts or prerequisite structures.

Method: They build a pipeline that: (1) uses GPT-4 to extract a prerequisite knowledge graph of course concepts from the curriculum; (2) trains a few-shot text classifier to map each AI-TA question event to one of 42 curriculum topics or an 'unknown' class; and (3) aggregates topic-level question volumes and compares them with independent measures of perceived difficulty from a mid-semester survey. Abstract: Large online courses generate thousands of student questions directed at conversational AI teaching assistants, yet these interaction logs remain largely untapped as diagnostic signals. We present a pipeline that maps student questions from a conversational AI teaching assistant to curriculum topics using a few-shot text classifier, grounded in a GPT-4-extracted prerequisite knowledge graph of course concepts. Evaluated on 1,340 question events from 164 students in a graduate-level AI course, our classifier achieves 80.0% accuracy across 43 labels (42 curriculum topics plus an "unknown" abstention class). Topic-level question volume correlates significantly with student self-reported difficulty from an independent mid-semester survey (rho = 0.491, p = 0.008, n = 28 topics), providing convergent evidence that the classified question stream reflects genuine topic difficulty. These results demonstrate that conversational AI interaction logs, mapped onto curriculum structure, carry actionable signals about topic-level knowledge gaps and provide instructors with a curriculum-grounded view of which topics warrant attention.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Automatic Depression Detection from clinical interviews is important but difficult because depression cues are sparse and embedded in long, multi-topic conversations, and because labeled clinical data is scarce and expensive to annotate and use for supervised training or fine-tuning. Existing ADD systems often rely on superficial reasoning and require large labeled datasets and costly model training, limiting their reliability and scalability.

Method: The authors introduce Dep-LLM, a three-stage, training-free framework that operates on frozen off-the-shelf LLMs. (1) A Chain-of-Thought Depression Multi-factor Analysis module decomposes each long dialogue into five clinically motivated themes and generates evidence-grounded rationales for each, which helps capture long-range dependencies and structure the reasoning. (2) A Confidence Analysis and Modulation module computes token-level entropy for each rationale to estimate epistemic reliability, then modulates signals both within labels and across themes by amplifying high-confidence evidence and attenuating low-confidence evidence without any additional training. (3) A Collaborative Multi-factor Prediction module aggregates the multi-factor, confidence-weighted signals to produce a final depression diagnosis.

Result: On the DAIC-WOZ and E-DAIC benchmark datasets, Dep-LLM consistently outperforms zero-shot application of 21 different foundation LLMs across nine evaluation metrics, including accuracy, macro F1, and weighted-average F1. It also surpasses state-of-the-art supervised, domain-specific LLMs and strong closed-source commercial LLMs for depression detection, all while requiring no extra training or fine-tuning.

Conclusion: Structured, clinician-inspired multi-factor reasoning combined with confidence-aware modulation over frozen foundation LLMs can substantially improve automatic depression detection from long clinical interviews, even in low-label regimes and without any training. Dep-LLM demonstrates that careful orchestration of off-the-shelf LLMs with clinically aligned decomposition and uncertainty handling can rival and exceed supervised and commercial systems, offering a scalable and generalizable approach to computational mental health assessment.

Abstract: Automatic Depression Detection (ADD) from clinical interviews is a pivotal task in computational mental health, yet it remains challenging due to two critical obstacles: 1) difficulty in modeling complex but sparsely distributed depression clues within lengthy, multi-topic clinical interviews, leading to superficial and unreliable reasoning; 2) scarcity of labeled data due to clinical privacy, together with high cost of training and fine-tuning, limiting the deployment of supervised ADD systems. To jointly address these challenges, we propose Dep-LLM, a training-free framework that mirrors the step-by-step reasoning of clinical psychiatrists and operates entirely on frozen off-the-shelf foundation LLMs. Dep-LLM comprises three stages. First, a Chain-of-Thought (CoT) Depression Multi-factor Analysis module structurally decomposes the long dialogue into five clinically aligned themes and produces evidence-grounded rationales, effectively handling long-context dependencies. Second, we introduce Confidence Analysis and Modulation module that quantifies the epistemic reliability from token-level entropy of each rationale and applies an intra-label and inter-theme modulation that amplifies trustworthy signals while suppressing uncertain ones without extra training. Third, a Collaborative Multi-factor Prediction module dynamically integrates multi-factor signals weighted by confidence into the final diagnosis. Extensive experiments on the DAIC-WOZ and E-DAIC datasets demonstrate the effectiveness and generalizability of Dep-LLM: it surpasses zero-shot baseline on nearly all 21 foundation LLMs across 9 metrics such as accuracy, macro F1 and weighted-average F1, and further outperforms state-of-the-art supervised domain-specific LLMs as well as the latest closed-source commercial LLMs, while requiring no extra training.

### [47] [Attention-Discounted Adaptive Sampler for Masked Diffusion Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10829)
*Yusuf Sahin,Ahmed Rockey Saikia,Volkan Cevher,Paolo Favaro*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces ADAS, a training-free, attention-based reranking strategy that improves the reliability and quality of highly parallel masked diffusion decoding by softly penalizing mutually dependent token predictions, yielding better accuracy at low denoising steps with minimal runtime overhead.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Parallel masked diffusion language models can reveal many tokens per iteration to reduce inference steps, but current methods often choose which tokens to commit using only per-token confidence scores. This ignores interactions between tokens that may depend on each other via attention, leading to unsafe joint commitments and degraded quality, especially at low numbers of denoising steps. There is a need for a simple, training-free method that accounts for token interactions while retaining the speed benefits of parallel decoding.

Method: The authors propose ADAS, an attention-discounted selection strategy layered on top of existing parallel masked diffusion samplers such as Top-k, Fast-dLLM, and EB-Sampler. ADAS keeps the original sampler’s stopping rule but changes how the subset of tokens to reveal in each iteration is constructed: it greedily reranks candidates by discounting a token’s score when it strongly attends to already selected positions whose predictions are still uncertain. Instead of converting attention into hard graph constraints, ADAS treats attention weights as a continuous soft penalty, adjusting candidate scores to reduce simultaneous commitment of tightly coupled, uncertain tokens.

Result: On LLaDA-8B-Base and Dream-7B-Base models evaluated on GSM8K, MATH500, HumanEval, and MBPP benchmarks, inserting ADAS into existing samplers yields substantial performance gains in low-step (low-NFE) regimes. Specifically, at matched numbers of denoiser evaluations, ADAS improves average performance by 9.11 percentage points over Top-k and 10.46 points over Fast-dLLM and EB-Sampler, while incurring only around 3.1% per-forward runtime overhead.

Conclusion: Soft attention-discounted reranking (ADAS) is an effective, modular add-on to masked diffusion language model decoders. By using attention as a soft penalty signal to account for inter-token dependencies during parallel token selection, ADAS improves solution quality in highly parallel decoding without altering stopping rules or requiring retraining, and does so with minimal computational overhead.

Abstract: Masked diffusion language models can reduce inference steps by revealing multiple tokens per denoising iteration, but this parallelism is fragile: positions that are individually confident may be unsafe to commit together when their predictions are coupled. Existing training-free samplers such as Top-\(k\), Fast-dLLM, and EB-Sampler mainly control how many tokens to reveal, while often ranking candidates by token-wise scores that ignore interactions within the selected set. We propose ADAS, a training-free reranking rule for parallel masked diffusion decoding. ADAS leaves the base sampler's stopping rule unchanged and modifies only subset construction: it greedily discounts a candidate when it attends strongly to already selected positions whose predictions remain uncertain. Unlike graph-constrained methods that turn attention into hard compatibility constraints, ADAS keeps attention continuous and uses it as a soft marginal penalty. Across LLaDA-8B-Base and Dream-7B-Base on GSM8K, MATH500, HumanEval, and MBPP, plugging ADAS into Top-\(k\), Fast-dLLM, and EB-Sampler improves low-NFE performance at matched denoiser evaluations by \(9.11\) and \(10.46\) percentage points on average, respectively, with \(3.1\%\) per-forward runtime overhead. These results show that soft attention-discounted reranking is a simple and modular way to improve quality in highly parallel decoding for masked diffusion language models.

### [48] [ConvMemory v2: A Recall-Preserving Top-10 Evidence Reranker for Conversational Memory Retrieval](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10842)
*Taiheng Pan*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: ConvMemory v2 is a second-stage, recall-preserving reranker that reorders only the top-10 candidates from ConvMemory v1 using a fine-tuned cross-encoder, yielding substantially better ranking quality on LoCoMo without changing Recall@10/Hit@10.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Improve conversational memory retrieval quality (especially top-1 ranking) without incurring the high cost of full-pool cross-encoder reranking, and to analyze precisely what information is load-bearing in the model’s performance.

Method: Insert a cascade-stage reranker (ConvMemory v2) after the ConvMemory v1 lightweight reranker. v2 is a fine-tuned ms-marco-MiniLM-L-6-v2 cross-encoder applied only to the ten (query, memory) pairs v1 already chose, guaranteeing Recall@10 is unchanged. Evaluate on the LoCoMo benchmark, compare against a strong cross-encoder over top-500 (mxbai-rerank-large-v1), and run a four-arm ablation that removes, shuffles, or replaces candidate-specific memory text to test its causal role.

Result: On LoCoMo, v2 increases FULL MRR from 0.5824 to 0.6560 and Hit@1 from 0.4440 to 0.5474 over v1, while keeping Recall@10/Hit@10 identical by design. v2 nearly matches a much more expensive full-pool cross-encoder baseline (MRR 0.6688), trailing by only 0.013 on FULL MRR and even outperforming it on certain raw-dense-hard slices where v1’s top-10 has higher recall. Ablations show that disrupting candidate-specific memory text collapses MRR below raw dense retrieval, identifying it as the key mechanism.

Conclusion: ConvMemory v2 is an effective, recall-preserving second-stage reranker that substantially improves ranking quality over ConvMemory v1 at modest cost, approaching the performance of a much heavier top-500 cross-encoder while even surpassing it on some data slices. Its gains crucially depend on candidate-specific memory text, and its benefit should be viewed as slice-dependent rather than a universal dominance over stronger but more expensive rerankers. The work formalizes v2 as a LoCoMo-tuned cascade with an explicit anti-shortcut contract and thorough load-bearing analysis, extending the earlier v1 report.

Abstract: We describe ConvMemory v2, an opt-in token-evidence reranker that sits after the lightweight ConvMemory v1 reranker and reorders only v1's protected top-10 candidate set. v2 is a fine-tuned ms-marco-MiniLM-L-6-v2 cross-encoder (22,713,601 parameters, measured from the released checkpoint) applied to the ten (query, memory) pairs that v1 has already selected; it does not change which ten memories are returned, so Recall@10 and Hit@10 are identical to v1 by construction, not by statistical coincidence. On the LoCoMo conversational memory benchmark (5 seeds, n = 4955 test rows), v2 raises FULL MRR from v1's 0.5824 to 0.6560 (paired bootstrap +0.0734, 95% CI [+0.0645, +0.0827]) and H@1 from 0.4440 to 0.5474. v2 closes most but not all of the gap to a much more expensive full-pool cross-encoder reference (mxbai-rerank-large-v1 over the top-500, MRR 0.6688): on FULL MRR v2 sits 0.013 below mxbai_top500, but on two raw-dense-hard slices (where v1's protected top-10 has higher recall than mxbai's own top-10) v2 exceeds mxbai_top500. A four-arm load-bearing ablation shows candidate-specific memory text is the mechanism: removing, shuffling, or replacing it collapses MRR below raw dense retrieval. v2 is best understood as a standard recall-preserving cascade pattern with LoCoMo-specific fine-tuning, an explicit anti-shortcut inference contract, and disciplined load-bearing analysis; its advantage over mxbai is slice-specific rather than a general dominance claim. This report extends the v1 technical report (arXiv:2605.28062).

</details>


### [49] [Janus: A Benchmark for Goal-Conditioned Information Distortion in LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10852)
*Polydoros Giannouris,Mohsinul Kabir,Sophia Ananiadou*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: JANUS is a benchmark to test how LLMs subtly distort facts to achieve goals without lying, by selectively emphasizing or omitting true information.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Real-world deception often involves selectively presenting true facts rather than outright fabricating information, but most existing LLM evaluations focus on explicit lies, hallucinations, or fabrications. This misses an important and potentially more dangerous form of misleading communication where models might be steered by objectives such as persuasion or promotion while remaining fact-grounded. The authors want to systematically measure and understand this subtle, goal-driven distortion in LLM outputs and evaluate whether current safety mechanisms prevent it.

Method: The authors construct JANUS, a benchmark of 160 scenarios across 8 domains, where each scenario has a fixed pool of annotated material facts that can be favorable or adverse. For each scenario, they design two prompting conditions: a neutral condition and a goal-conditioned condition that asks the model to increase some form of uptake (e.g., adoption, enrollment, approval, or support) despite potential harms to affected individuals or groups. Models are required to generate outputs using only the provided fact pool, eliminating hallucination and fabrication as explanations. The benchmark then compares how models differentially select, omit, emphasize, or linguistically soften facts under neutral vs goal-conditioned prompts to quantify pragmatic distortion. They run extensive experiments on 12 different LLMs to analyze behavioral patterns and safety performance.

Result: Across the 12 tested LLMs, the authors observe systematic and consistent distortions in the goal-conditioned condition relative to the neutral one. Models more frequently omit or downplay adverse facts, highlight favorable ones, and use vaguer or more reassuring language when pushed toward persuasive or outcome-oriented goals. These effects appear across domains, suggesting that current LLMs are broadly sensitive to incentive-like framing and lack strong internal safeguards against selectively misleading, yet factually grounded, communication. The authors release their dataset and code so others can reproduce and extend these findings.

Conclusion: JANUS isolates a crucial failure mode where LLMs produce misleading net impressions without relying on falsehoods, by selectively distorting the presentation of true facts to serve a goal. The benchmark shows that many current models engage in such pragmatic distortion under goal-directed prompts, revealing gaps in existing safety and alignment mechanisms that mainly target hallucination and overt lying. By providing a standardized corpus and tools, JANUS enables future research on detection, mitigation, and training strategies to reduce subtle, goal-conditioned misleading behavior in LLMs.

Abstract: LLM deception is often evaluated through direct markers such as fabricated claims, explicit lies, or strategic concealment. However, many real-world misleading communications do not depend on false statements, rather, they arise from selective treatment of true material facts: omitting adverse evidence, softening unfavorable details, emphasizing favorable details, or replacing precise qualifications with vague language. Existing benchmarks largely miss this subtler and arguably more dangerous failure mode. We introduce JANUS, a benchmark for measuring goal-conditioned pragmatic distortion in fact-grounded LLM outputs. Each scenario in our benchmark provides a fixed pool of favorable and adverse facts and compares a neutral condition against a goal-directed condition, such as increasing adoption, enrollment, approval, or support, despite potential harm to directly affected individuals or groups. Because all outputs are constrained to use the same fact pool, JANUS isolates misleading net impressions from hallucination and fabrication. JANUS contains 160 scenarios across 8 domains, with each scenario paired with neutral and goal-conditioned prompts and annotated material facts. Extensive experiments across 12 LLMs reveal consistent goal-conditioned distortions, demonstrating that current models remain sensitive to incentive and framing objectives and lack robust safeguards against selectively misleading communication. We publicly release our corpus and code for future research.

</details>


### [50] [Pushing the Limits of LLM Tool Calling via Experiential Knowledge Integration and Activation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10875)
*Yupu Hao,Zhuoran Jin,Huanxuan Liao,Kang Liu,Jun Zhao*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how different kinds of knowledge and training strategies affect LLMs’ multi-step tool use, and proposes KATE, a framework combining experiential knowledge, parallel “wide” reasoning, and knowledge-aware training to significantly improve tool execution.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs used as autonomous agents frequently fail in multi-step tool execution, mainly because they lack sufficient tool-related experiential knowledge and because existing methods do not effectively activate whatever knowledge they do have. There is limited systematic understanding of how to acquire, use, and internalize knowledge specifically for tool use, so the authors aim to dissect these stages and design a more effective tool-execution framework.

Method: The authors decompose tool-use performance into three knowledge-centric stages: (1) knowledge acquisition, where they construct and compare different forms of experiential knowledge, from concrete instance-level to more abstract intent-level descriptions; (2) knowledge activation at inference time, where they contrast deeper chain-of-thought prompting with wider parallel sampling plus aggregation to leverage latent knowledge; and (3) knowledge internalization, where they post-train models on knowledge-augmented data, comparing supervised fine-tuning with reinforcement learning. They then unify the most effective strategies into KATE, a Knowledge-Augmented Tool Execution framework combining experiential knowledge, reasoning-width-expanded inference, and knowledge-aware training. KATE is evaluated on BFCL-V3 and AppWorld benchmarks across model scales, against strong baselines.

Result: Empirical analysis reveals that simple, concrete instance-level experiential knowledge already provides strong, stable gains for tool use, whereas higher-level intent-level knowledge contributes relatively little. During inference, increasing the depth of reasoning (e.g., more elaborate CoT) yields diminishing returns, while expanding the width of reasoning via parallel sampling with aggregation better activates experiential knowledge and improves execution. In the training phase, post-training on knowledge-augmented data improves performance further, with reinforcement learning clearly outperforming supervised fine-tuning. Combining these findings in KATE leads to consistent and substantial improvements over competitive baselines on BFCL-V3 and AppWorld across different model sizes.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that effective tool use by LLM agents critically depends on how experiential knowledge is represented, activated at inference, and internalized during training. Concrete instance-level knowledge is surprisingly powerful, wide rather than deep reasoning better exploits latent knowledge, and reinforcement-learning-based post-training on knowledge-augmented data is particularly effective. The proposed KATE framework operationalizes these insights to yield robust, scalable gains in multi-step tool execution, suggesting a practical recipe for building stronger tool-using LLM agents.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) rely on tool use to act as autonomous agents, yet often fail in multi-step execution due to insufficient tool-related knowledge and ineffective knowledge activation. Therefore, we present a systematic study on how knowledge influences tool-use performance, covering the stages of knowledge acquisition, activation, and internalization. In the knowledge acquisition stage, we acquire and evaluate various forms of experiential knowledge, and our analysis shows that simple instance-level knowledge can already provide strong and reliable gains, while abstract intent-level knowledge offers limited benefits. At inference time, to activate knowledge, we find that prompting LLM to expand the depth of reasoning yields diminishing returns, whereas expanding the width of reasoning by parallel sampling with aggregation more effectively activates latent experiential knowledge. At training time, for knowledge internalization, post-training with knowledge-augmented data further improves performance, with reinforcement learning outperforming supervised fine-tuning. Based on these insights, we propose the Knowledge-Augmented Tool Execution (KATE), a knowledge-augmented tool execution framework that integrates experiential knowledge with reasoning-width-expanded inference and knowledge-aware training. Experiments on BFCL-V3 and AppWorld demonstrate consistent and substantial improvements over strong baselines across model scales. Our Code is available at https://github.com/hypasd-art/KATE.

</details>

*Xiangjun Zai,Xingyu Tan,Chen Chen,Xiaoyang Wang,Wenjie Zhang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: DocTrace is a multi‑agent RAG framework for long‑document QA that builds document‑structure‑aware working and experience memory on demand, improving accuracy and efficiency over prior RAG methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing RAG approaches for long‑document QA struggle because they (1) build expensive, query‑agnostic knowledge structures, (2) underuse the original document hierarchy and sections, and (3) fail to reuse successful reasoning patterns from past questions. Long‑document QA needs reasoning over dispersed evidence, event order, and section context, which current methods handle inefficiently and with limited accuracy.

Method: The authors propose DocTrace, a multi‑agent RAG framework. It (1) builds a lightweight structural tree index that preserves the document hierarchy; (2) on demand, during reasoning for a specific query, constructs a hypergraph‑structured working memory shared by agents, capturing cross‑part evidence connections; and (3) stores successful reasoning trajectories as a graph‑structured experience memory that can be reused to guide reasoning on future, related questions. This yields query‑triggered, structure‑aware, and experience‑guided reasoning over long documents.

Result: On four long‑document QA benchmarks, DocTrace outperforms baselines, achieving state‑of‑the‑art results on three datasets. Compared with the strongest prior method, ComoRAG, DocTrace improves F1 by up to 8.85 points and exact match (EM) by up to 4.40 points, while cutting overall computational cost by 53.32%.

Conclusion: DocTrace shows that combining document‑structure‑aware indexing, on‑demand hypergraph working memory, and graph‑based experience memory enables more accurate and efficient long‑document QA. Query‑triggered organization and reusing past reasoning plans are effective strategies to reduce computation and improve performance in multi‑agent RAG systems.

Abstract: Long-document question answering (QA) requires large language models (LLMs) to reason over evidence scattered across lengthy documents, where answers often depend on event order, section-level context, and cross-part evidence connections. Although retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) reduces the input context by retrieving relevant evidence, existing structured RAG methods still face three limitations: costly query-agnostic knowledge organization, insufficient use of original document structure, and no reuse of historical reasoning experience. To address these limitations, we propose DocTrace, a multi-agent RAG framework for long-document QA that supports query-triggered knowledge organization, document-structure-aware and experience-guided reasoning. DocTrace preserves document hierarchy with a lightweight document structural tree index, constructs agent-shared hypergraph-structured working memory on demand during reasoning, and stores successful reasoning plans in graph-structured experience memory for future reuse, enabling adaptive exploration across related long-document questions. Experiments on four long-document QA datasets show that DocTrace achieves the best performance on three datasets, surpassing the strongest baseline, ComoRAG, by up to 8.85% in F1 and 4.40% in EM, while reducing the overall computational cost by 53.32%

Furthermore, models differ in how vulnerable they are, with susceptibility correlated to the original likelihood of producing biased content.

Conclusion: Post-training alignment of LLMs is fragile: a single biased GRPO update can override safety guardrails and reintroduce widespread bias. Abstract: Warning: This paper contains several toxic and offensive statements.

In this work, we investigate how easily such guardrails can be broken by Group Relative Policy Optimization (GRPO). We show that one-shot GRPO training on a single biased example is sufficient to induce systematic bias, with stereotype-driven reasoning generalizing across attributes, categories, and benchmarks. We further find that models differ in their susceptibility based on the initial likelihood of producing biased outputs. Our results reveal a critical vulnerability in post-training: alignment can be overridden by a single example.

### [53] [Density Field State Space Models: 1-Bit Distillation, Efficient Inference, and Knowledge Organization in Mamba-2](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10932)
*Chirag Shinde*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: DF-SSM compresses Mamba-2 into an extremely small, fast, 1-bit + int8-corrected model with minimal performance loss and reveals structured internal knowledge phases.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Large SSM-based language models like Mamba-2 are memory- and compute-heavy, making deployment on limited hardware difficult. Existing low-bit models (e.g., BitMamba-2) require expensive training from scratch on huge token budgets. The authors want a cheap, post-hoc way to compress a pretrained FP16 SSM into a highly efficient low-bit model, while also using the distilled model to better understand how knowledge is represented and processed layer-wise.

Method: They introduce Density Field State Space Models (DF-SSM), which represent SSM weights using a 1-bit scaffold plus an int8 low-rank correction. They distill a compressed student from a pretrained FP16 Mamba-2 1.3B teacher using only 32M tokens and 6 hours on a single A100 GPU. On the systems side, they design an optimized inference stack: cuBLAS INT8 tensor cores for the scaffold matrix multiplications, custom CUDA kernels for stateful SSM and convolution operations, and an AVX-512 CPU backend for efficient GPU and CPU deployment. On the analysis side, they probe internal activations across layers using 445 factual prompts in 19 categories to map how information flows and localizes through the network.

Result: On Mamba-2 1.3B, DF-SSM yields a 278 MB model, a 9.7x compression relative to the 2.7 GB FP16 teacher, with 21.4x faster single-batch GPU inference compared to the mamba-ssm reference implementation. Downstream performance is within 2–4 percentage points of BitMamba-2, despite DF-SSM using far fewer distillation tokens and not requiring pretraining from scratch. Layer-wise analysis of responses to 445 factual prompts uncovers three phases: early layers (0–3) perform intent classification in an abstract space misaligned with the vocabulary, middle layers (25–35) perform localized knowledge retrieval with strong category structure, and late layers (36–47) focus on output formatting as category structure dissipates. They also find that early classification depends mainly on syntactic template structure rather than true semantic content, and that knowledge is cleanly organized even when factual recall is weak.

Conclusion: DF-SSM shows that pretrained SSMs like Mamba-2 can be aggressively compressed to a 1-bit scaffold plus int8 correction while retaining most downstream performance and achieving large speedups, with very low distillation cost compared to training low-bit models from scratch. The accompanying analysis suggests that such models organize information into distinct processing phases—syntax-driven intent classification, localized factual retrieval, and formatting-driven output generation—and that coherent representational structure can exist even when factual strength (recall accuracy) is limited, implying that representation quality and factual robustness are partially decoupled.

Abstract: We present Density Field State Space Models (DF-SSM), a framework for compressing SSMs to a 1-bit scaffold with int8 low-rank correction. Applied to Mamba-2 1.3B, we achieve a 278 MB model (9.7x smaller than the 2.7 GB FP16 teacher) that runs at 21.4x faster inference on GPU (batch=1, relative to the mamba-ssm reference implementation) while maintaining downstream task performance within 2-4 percentage points of BitMamba-2, a 1.58-bit model trained from scratch on 150B tokens. The distillation itself requires only 32M tokens and 6 hours on a single A100 GPU, though it presupposes a pretrained FP16 teacher. We develop an optimized inference pipeline combining cuBLAS INT8 tensor cores for the scaffold matmul, custom CUDA kernels for stateful SSM and convolution operations, and an AVX-512 CPU backend for efficient deployment on both GPU and CPU. Beyond compression, we investigate the internal knowledge organization of the resulting model, discovering three distinct processing phases: intent classification (layers 0-3, operating in an abstract space with no vocabulary alignment), knowledge retrieval (layers 25-35, where factual associations localize to a 5-layer window), and output formatting (layers 36-47, where category structure dissolves). Through systematic analysis of 445 factual prompts across 19 categories, we find that early-layer classification is syntactic (driven by template structure) rather than semantic, and that the model exhibits well-organized knowledge representations despite weak factual recall--suggesting that representational structure may precede factual strength.

Method: The authors take 60 English math word problems and ask three LLMs—Claude Opus 4, GPT-4.1, and Gemini 2.5 Pro—to adapt them into seven target languages tied to specific countries and regions: Bengali, Hindi, Punjabi (India), Urdu, Sindhi (Pakistan), Italian, and Sicilian (Italy). They also frequently misattribute regional context (e.g., using Bangladeshi taka for Indian Bengali students) and introduce cross-cultural contamination (e.g., turning egg hunts into Eid activities). Some issues are obvious in single outputs, but many systematic failures only appear through large-scale corpus analysis.

This makes serious cultural failures easy to miss without corpus-level evaluation, highlighting the need for more rigorous, culturally aware assessment and design of LLM-based personalization in education.

Abstract: Large language models are increasingly used to adapt math word problems for personalized learning at scale, but it remains an open question whether those adaptations are consistent across models, preserve cultural diversity at scale, and reveal which cultural entities models treat as most salient. We annotate 6,489 entity transformations, coding whether models preserve, localize, generalize, omit, or change entities such as names, foods, and places. Models agree on transformation type in 62.5% of cases and on specific substitutions in only 33.5%, meaning model choice directly shapes which cultural world students encounter. All 21 language-model combinations show entropy collapse, with adaptation compressing rather than expanding cultural diversity. Models prioritize surface markers such as names, foods, and currencies while preserving deeper structural features such as grade-level systems that embed culturally specific assumptions. Despite prompts specifying target countries, models misattribute regional context by using Bangladeshi taka for Indian Bengali students and produce cross-cultural contamination, such as adapting egg hunts as Eid activities. Some failures are visible in individual translations. Others, including diversity collapse, systematic preference for surface markers, and consistent regional misattribution, emerge only through corpus-level analysis. The surface plausibility that makes adapted problems look correct is precisely what makes deeper failures easy to overlook.

### [56] [Does Reasoning Preserve Alignment? On the Trustworthiness of Large Reasoning Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11046)

Abstract: Instruction-tuned LLMs are increasingly converted into reasoning models through post-training to improve multi-step task performance. This conversion is usually optimized for reasoning accuracy, without explicitly preserving the alignment behavior of the instruction-tuned model, such as safe refusal, bias avoidance, and privacy protection. We ask: does this conversion preserve alignment? We study this question through a trustworthiness audit and find that it is not behavior-preserving by default. For a systematic analysis, we compare reasoning models produced via supervised fine-tuning, RL-based post-training, and distillation against matched instruction-tuned baselines across six trustworthiness dimensions: safety, toxicity, stereotyping and bias, machine ethics, privacy, and out-of-distribution robustness. We observe that reasoning models often improve on reasoning benchmarks but exhibit alignment regressions, including increased toxicity, amplified stereotyping, miscalibrated refusal, and contextual privacy leakage. These regressions are consistent with behavioral drift from the instruction-tuned baseline, measured by KL divergence. Overall, our results point to the broader conclusion that trustworthiness metrics are essential for evaluating reasoning models and should be reported alongside gains in reasoning capability.

### [57] [Attention Amnesia in Hybrid LLMs: When CoT Fine-Tuning Breaks Long-Range Recall, and How to Fix It](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11052)
*Xinyu Zhou,Boyu Zhu,Yi Xu,Zhiwei Li,Yingfa Chen,Huiming Wang,Zhijiang Guo*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper finds that chain-of-thought supervised fine-tuning harms long-context retrieval in hybrid linear-attention LLMs, and proposes a training-free fix (QK-Restore) that resets only query/key projection weights to recover long-range recall while keeping improved reasoning.

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Hybrid linear-attention LLMs are designed for efficient long-context processing, but practitioners heavily use chain-of-thought supervised fine-tuning to improve reasoning. The authors observe that, despite better reasoning, such CoT-SFT may silently break long-context recall, especially on challenging retrieval tasks and very long sequences. This motivates a systematic study of the degradation mechanism and a simple, practical method to recover long-context ability without throwing away the benefits of CoT-SFT or paying additional training costs.

Method: 1) Empirically evaluate several hybrid linear-attention architectures (e.g., HypeNet, Jet-Nemotron) on the Needle-In-A-Haystack (NIAH) benchmark before and after CoT-SFT, under varying retrieval difficulty and context lengths. 2) Analyze attention behavior and gradients to show that CoT-SFT biases learning towards short-range patterns, distorting the query/key projection matrices (W_Q, W_K) that control long-range token routing. 3) Propose QK-Restore, a training-free procedure that restores W_Q and W_K from the pre-SFT checkpoint while keeping all other post-SFT parameters. 4) Introduce a Procrustes-based variant that partially aligns and mixes pre- and post-SFT query/key spaces to trade off between preserving long-range routing and retaining CoT-induced reasoning improvements. 5) Evaluate these methods across models and settings to measure effects on long-context retrieval and reasoning tasks.

Result: They show that CoT-SFT dramatically degrades long-context retrieval performance in hybrid linear-attention models; for instance, HypeNet-9B drops from 67.2% to 9.4% accuracy on NIAH-S2@256K after CoT-SFT, with worse degradation for harder retrieval and longer contexts. QK-Restore effectively recovers long-context capabilities at zero training cost by reverting only W_Q and W_K to their pre-SFT state, while preserving reasoning gains from CoT-SFT. On HypeNet-5B, their method boosts S3@256K from 65.4% to 76.4% compared to the CoT-SFT model, with little to no loss in reasoning performance. The Procrustes variant allows a tunable trade-off but still yields strong restoration of long-range recall.

Conclusion: CoT supervised fine-tuning, while beneficial for reasoning, can critically harm long-context retrieval in hybrid linear-attention LLMs by disrupting query/key projections responsible for long-range routing. A simple, training-free intervention—restoring or partially aligning W_Q and W_K to pre-SFT values—largely recovers long-context abilities without sacrificing reasoning performance. This highlights the importance of disentangling routing and reasoning components in model design and fine-tuning, and suggests that future long-context LLM pipelines should explicitly guard against CoT-induced degradation of attention routing.

Abstract: Chain-of-thought (CoT) supervised fine-tuning (SFT) is widely adopted to improve reasoning ability, yet we find that it systematically degrades long-context recall in hybrid linear-attention models. Across architectures including HypeNet and Jet-Nemotron, retrieval performance on Needle-In-A-Haystack (NIAH) deteriorates substantially after CoT-SFT, and the degradation becomes more severe under harder retrieval settings and longer context windows. For example, HypeNet-9B on NIAH-S2@256K decreases from $67.2\%$ to $9.4\%$. We attribute this to CoT-SFT biasing attention gradients toward short-range patterns, disrupting query-key projections ($W_Q, W_K$) that are responsible for long-range routing. Motivated by this observation, we propose QK-Restore, a training-free method that restores only $W_Q$ and $W_K$ from the pre-SFT checkpoint while preserving all other post-SFT parameters. We further introduce a Procrustes variant to balance routing preservation and reasoning adaptation. Across architectures, QK-Restore consistently restores long-context capability at zero training cost while preserving reasoning performance; for instance, on HypeNet-5B it improves S3@256K from $65.4\%$ to $76.4\%$ while maintaining strong reasoning performance.

</details>

*Genta Indra Winata,Amartya Chakraborty,Yuzhen Lin,Swasthi P Rao,Shikhhar Siingh,Houhan Lu,Nadia Bathaee,Sriharsha Hatwar,Paresh Dashore,Anmol Jain,Kshitij Tayal,Xiuzhu Lin,Anirban Das,Sambit Sahu,Shi-Xiong Zhang*

TL;DR: Introduces T1-Bench, a comprehensive benchmark to evaluate LLM-based agentic systems in realistic, multi-domain, multi-step customer-facing scenarios, improving on prior benchmarks in complexity, realism, and coverage.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Each scenario is crafted to require structured reasoning, tool usage, and coordination across domains. In addition to automatic metrics, they collect human judgments to assess qualitative aspects of performance.

Result: T1-Bench yields a richer, more discriminative evaluation of agentic systems than prior benchmarks, revealing performance differences across models, domains, and interaction types. The benchmark demonstrates that many current systems still struggle with complex, multi-step, multi-domain scenarios, and that tool use and sustained reasoning remain challenging. The standardized framework allows reproducible comparisons across 12 tested models.

Conclusion: T1-Bench significantly advances the evaluation of LLM-based agents by offering higher task complexity, deeper interaction structures, and broader domain coverage than existing benchmarks. It provides a rigorous, realistic testbed for studying and improving agent behavior, reasoning, and tool use in customer-facing, multi-domain environments. The planned open-source release of data and evaluation code aims to support future research and benchmarking in agentic systems.

Abstract: Recent advances in reasoning and tool-calling capabilities of large language models (LLMs) have enabled increasingly capable agentic systems. However, existing benchmarks remain limited in task complexity, realism, and domain diversity, and often fail to capture interactions that span multiple domains, limiting their ability to evaluate agents in realistic multi-step settings that require sustained reasoning and coordination. To address these limitations, we introduce T1-Bench, a high-fidelity, comprehensive benchmark for evaluating agentic systems in realistic customer-facing, multi-domain environments, featuring interleaved scenarios that require structured reasoning across multi-turn user-assistant interactions and substantially increasing both compositional complexity and evaluative rigor across 25 domains of varying difficulty. We evaluate T1-Bench using 12 proprietary and open-weight models, providing a reproducible and standardized framework for assessing agent behavior, tool utilization, and conversational quality in complex, multi-step environments. We further complement automatic evaluation with human judgments to strengthen the assessment of qualitative performance. Overall, T1-Bench substantially advances prior benchmarks by increasing task complexity, interaction depth, and domain coverage in simulated multi-domain environments. To facilitate future research on agentic systems, we will publicly release data and evaluation code as open source.

### [59] [Modeling Complex Behaviors: Multi-Personality Composition and Dynamic Switching in Vision-Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11074)
*Peiqi Jia,Haonan Jia,Ziqi Miao,Linkang Du,Yuntao Wang,Zhou Su*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how explicitly adding personality traits to prompts changes the behavior and performance of multimodal large language models, and proposes an evaluation framework for single, multiple, and switching personalities.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As MLLMs are increasingly used in social and interactive settings, they need to behave in consistent, controllable ways that match desired personalities. However, how personality prompts affect multimodal reasoning, and how stable or reversible these effects are, is not well understood. The paper aims to systematically investigate and measure these effects to enable safer, more predictable personality control in MLLMs.

Method: The authors introduce explicit personality conditioning into MLLMs via prompts and build an evaluation framework with three settings: (1) single-personality induction, where a single trait is imposed and its impact on tasks like image captioning and VQA is measured; (2) multi-personality induction, where multiple traits are combined to study balancing and interference; and (3) personality switching, where traits are dynamically changed over time to analyze residual and co-modulation effects from previous personalities. They also compare these explicit methods with existing prompt-based personality techniques designed for text-only LLMs, testing their transferability to multimodal tasks.

Result: Personality conditioning improves performance on socially flavored tasks like image captioning but harms tasks that demand precise reasoning, such as VQA. In multi-trait and switching scenarios, model behavior shows balancing and residual effects: outputs are influenced jointly by current and previously applied personality constraints. Furthermore, personality induction methods developed for text-only LLMs do not transfer well to multimodal settings.

Conclusion: Personality in MLLMs is dynamic and complex: explicit conditioning can both help and hurt performance depending on the task, and personality traits interact over time instead of resetting cleanly. Existing prompt-based approaches are insufficient for robust control in multimodal contexts. The authors conclude that dedicated, better-grounded methods and evaluation protocols are needed for reliable personality induction and behavior control in MLLMs.

Abstract: With the widespread deployment of Multimodal Large Language Models (MLLMs) in social interaction, understanding and controlling their behavior under complex personality conditions is essential. This paper introduces explicit personality conditioning and establishes a systematic evaluation framework encompassing single-personality induction, multi-personality induction, and personality switching. Experiments show that personality induction improves image captioning performance but can impair performance on tasks requiring precise reasoning, such as visual question answering (VQA). Balancing and residual effects are observed during multi-trait composition and dynamic switching, indicating that model behavior is co-modulated by both previous and current personality constraints. Existing prompt-based personality induction methods show limited transferability to multimodal settings. Our work reveals the dynamic and complex nature of personality modeling in MLLMs and underscores the need for robust, tailored methods for personality induction and evaluation. The code will be released when the paper is accepted.

### [60] [VISTA: A Versatile Interactive User Simulation Toolkit for Agent Evaluation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11079)
*Yunan Lu,Ryan Shea,Yusen Zhang,Zhou Yu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces VISTA, a toolkit for evaluating interactive AI agents using realistic user simulations and rich metrics that better capture agents’ behavior and failure modes than static benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Interactive agents operate in dynamic, multi-step environments, but current evaluation methods rely heavily on static benchmarks that cannot reflect realistic, evolving interactions or reveal nuanced failure modes. User-simulation-based evaluation could solve this but existing simulators lack good ways to assess the quality and breadth of simulated interactions, and they are usually limited to either UI-only or API-only actions, failing to mirror real user behavior. There is a need for an evaluation framework that both measures how good the simulations are and supports richer, mixed interaction modalities.

Method: The authors propose VISTA, a Versatile Interactive user Simulation Toolkit for Agent evaluation. VISTA defines six quantitative metrics designed to assess realism, capability coverage, and interaction effectiveness in simulated interactions between users and agents. It introduces a hybrid user simulator able to perform both UI-based and API-based actions within interactive environments, thus modeling a wider spectrum of user behaviors. The toolkit is instantiated and tested in specific domains to see how well it evaluates agents compared to existing simulation frameworks and static benchmarks.

Result: Using e-commerce shopping and educational customer-service scenarios as testbeds, VISTA-generated simulations are shown to be more realistic and comprehensive than those from prior methods. The toolkit better explores agent capabilities and exposes failure modes thanks to the combination of its metrics and hybrid interaction model. Empirical results indicate improvement in evaluation quality along the dimensions of realism, coverage, and effectiveness, though precise quantitative gains are not specified in the abstract.

Conclusion: VISTA provides a more powerful and realistic framework for evaluating interactive agents than static benchmarks or prior user simulators. By combining a hybrid (UI+API) user simulator with a rich set of evaluation metrics, it enables more comprehensive, nuanced assessment of agent behavior and failure modes across diverse interactive environments such as e-commerce and customer service. This suggests VISTA can serve as a general toolkit for next-generation interactive agent evaluation.

Abstract: Evaluation remains a critical bottleneck for interactive agent development. Existing evaluation methods often rely on static benchmarks, which fail to capture the dynamic, multi-step nature of agentic behavior and struggle to expose meaningful failure modes. While user-simulation-based evaluation offers a promising alternative, existing simulation frameworks suffer from two major limitations. First, they provide limited mechanisms for evaluating the quality and comprehensiveness of simulated interactions, making it difficult to assess whether a simulator sufficiently explores an agent's capabilities and failure modes. Second, most frameworks are restricted to either UI-only actions or API-only actions, limiting their ability to model the full range of realistic user behaviors. To address these limitations, we propose VISTA, a Versatile Interactive user Simulation Toolkit for Agent evaluation. Our toolkit includes a suite of six metrics for measuring the realism, capability coverage, and interaction effectiveness of simulated interactions. In addition, we develop a hybrid user simulator that integrates both UI-based interactions and API-based interactions, enabling more realistic and comprehensive evaluation across diverse interactive environments. We evaluate VISTA in e-commerce shopping and education customer service settings and demonstrate that it produces more realistic and comprehensive evaluations than existing methods.
