### [61] [The Shibboleth Effect: Auditing the Cross-Lingual Distributional Skew of Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11082)
*Hakan Mehmetcik*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper tests whether advanced LLMs behave differently in high-stakes geopolitical simulations depending on the language used, finding that language can significantly change models’ coerciveness for some architectures but not others.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand if large language models are reliably aligned across languages when operating in sensitive, high-stakes domains like diplomacy and crisis management, and to test whether the so‑called Shibboleth Effect—behavioral shifts across languages—actually appears in modern frontier models.

Method: The authors construct a synthetic multi-agent geopolitical wargame called the Cerulean Sea Crisis, mimicking Eastern Mediterranean maritime disputes. Six frontier LLMs play in a between-groups design, where the only experimental manipulation is the language of interaction (English vs. Turkish). Each arm consists of 10 games with 5 rounds each, yielding 586 usable statements. A zero-shot classifier rates each statement on two continuous behavioral dimensions: Concession Rate and Coercive Rhetoric. Statistical analysis (including Holm correction for multiple comparisons) then estimates cross-lingual shifts (deltas) for each model.

Result: Models show divergent language-dependent behavioral shifts. Llama-4 becomes significantly more coercive in Turkish compared with English (delta = +0.800, p = .002), while Gemini-3.1-Pro and DeepSeek-R1 become significantly less coercive in Turkish (deltas = -0.750 and -0.860, p = .005 and .006, respectively). GPT-4o shows no significant difference between languages (delta = +0.130, p = .614). Chain-of-thought traces for DeepSeek-R1 suggest an internal process that dampens escalation in Turkish. Overall, cross-lingual distributions of coercive vs. concessionary behavior vary by model.

Conclusion: Cross-lingual behavioral skew is not a universal trait of Western-developed LLMs; instead, it depends on model-specific architectural and training choices. Some models escalate under language shift, others de-escalate, and some remain stable. The authors propose two buffering mechanisms—chain-of-thought institutional anchoring and multilingual RLHF alignment—that can mitigate harmful skew. They argue that understanding and designing for these mechanisms is critical if LLMs are to be safely deployed in diplomatic and crisis-management applications, where unnoticed language-conditioned shifts could be operationally dangerous.

Abstract: This study investigates cross-lingual distributional skew (the Shibboleth Effect) in frontier large language models (LLMs) subjected to sustained adversarial conditions. We develop a multi-agent geopolitical wargame, the Cerulean Sea Crisis, a synthetic maritime territorial dispute designed to mirror the structural dynamics of Eastern Mediterranean conflicts. Six frontier models (GPT-4o, Llama-4, Mistral-Large, Gemini-3.1-Pro, Qwen3.6-Plus, and DeepSeek-R1) participate in a between-groups experiment (N = 10 games per arm, K = 5 rounds per game) in which the sole manipulation is the language of play (English versus Turkish), producing 586 validated statements. A zero-shot classifier assesses behavioral dispositions along two continuous dimensions: Concession Rate and Coercive Rhetoric. The results are heterogeneous. Llama-4 shows a substantial, Holm-corrected increase in coercive rhetoric under Turkish (delta = +0.800, p = .002), whereas Gemini-3.1-Pro displays an equally large decrease (delta = -0.750, p = .005). DeepSeek-R1 exhibits a similar negative shift (delta = -0.860, p = .006) and provides chain-of-thought evidence consistent with a buffering mechanism. GPT-4o shows no detectable effect (delta = +0.130, p = .614). These findings indicate that cross-lingual behavioral skew is contingent on model architecture and training regime rather than a universal property of Western-origin LLMs. We identify two distinct buffering mechanisms, chain-of-thought institutional anchoring and multilingual RLHF alignment, and discuss their implications for integrating LLMs safely into diplomatic and crisis-management settings.

</details>


### [62] [PhantomBench: Benchmarking the Non-existential Threat of Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11105)
*Haeji Jung,Hila Gonen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Introduces PhantomBench, a large-scale benchmark of >60K non-existent but realistic-sounding concepts to test whether language models can recognize when they don’t know something, revealing very high hallucination rates across 21 models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Language models frequently hallucinate—confidently generating answers that are not grounded in facts—yet users often trust them, which is dangerous in high-stakes domains (e.g., medical, legal, safety-critical settings). Existing work has improved our understanding of hallucinations, but we still lack a clear, systematic way to measure how reliably models can detect and admit the limits of their own knowledge, especially when queried about things that do not exist. There is also a need for a scalable resource that can be adapted by researchers and practitioners to stress-test models’ hallucination behavior on non-existent or rare concepts.

Method: The authors build PhantomBench, a benchmark with over 60,000 carefully constructed non-existent terms and entities. These phantom concepts are derived from and resemble real concepts across many domains, making them plausible but in fact fabricated. They then evaluate 21 different language models of various families and sizes on these items, measuring how often the models hallucinate (i.e., answer as if the phantom concepts were real) versus abstain or express uncertainty. They also analyze how prompts that presuppose the existence of the concept affect model behavior. Additionally, they propose a generalizable pipeline for generating such phantom concepts so others can create customized benchmarks for their own domains.

Result: Across all tested models, hallucination rates are very high, reaching average rates up to 86.7% in some settings. Even state-of-the-art, large ‘frontier’ models often fail to refuse or express uncertainty when asked about concepts that do not exist, particularly when the user input is phrased as if these concepts are real. The authors also find that model performance on PhantomBench is informative about behavior on rare real-world concepts, suggesting PhantomBench can serve as a proxy for studying hallucinations on low-frequency knowledge items.

Conclusion: PhantomBench reveals that current language models are generally poor at recognizing when they lack knowledge and often hallucinate in response to questions about non-existent concepts, even under prompts that encourage abstention. This limitation persists even for advanced frontier models and is exacerbated when user queries presuppose existence. The benchmark and associated pipeline provide a scalable, domain-adaptable tool for researchers and practitioners to measure and stress-test hallucination behavior, particularly for rare or unseen concepts, and highlight an urgent need for improved mechanisms for uncertainty estimation and safe abstention in language models.

Abstract: Hallucinations, where language models (LMs) generate factually ungrounded responses, pose serious risks, as users tend to blindly rely on them. This is particularly concerning in high-stakes domains, where consequences of such model behavior can lead to significant harms. Despite notable progress in understanding hallucinations, it remains unclear how reliably these models can recognize the limits of their knowledge. We introduce PhantomBench, the first large-scale benchmark of its kind, comprising more than 60K non-existent terms and entities derived from real concepts across diverse domains. Using our benchmark, we evaluate a total of 21 models of various types and sizes. We show staggering hallucination rates across the board (with average rates as high as 86.7% in some cases), and note that even frontier models surprisingly fail to abstain on non-existent concepts, especially when the input presumes their existence. We then show that PhantomBench can serve as a proxy for studying model behavior on rare concepts for which models are more prone to hallucinate. We also provide a pipeline to construct PhantomBench, enabling scalable generation of non-existent concepts tailored to the specific needs of researchers and practitioners.

</details>


### [63] [Provenance-Grounded Gating and Adaptive Recovery in Synthetic Post-Training Data Curation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11127)
*Soham Bhattacharjee,Karun Sharma,Vinay Kumar Sankarapu,Pratinav Seth*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how to better filter and reuse synthetic LLM-generated data, focusing on grounding filters in source evidence and recovering rejected samples, and finds that evidence-aware, combined gates plus adaptive recovery improve data yield and quality, while generator size remains the main driver of downstream performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Synthetic data from LLMs is often filtered with reward models or judge LLMs, but it is unclear (1) how much filters should explicitly use the original source evidence to detect hallucinations and (2) whether discarded generations can be intelligently recovered instead of simply thrown away. Since synthetic data quality directly affects fine-tuning, understanding these aspects can improve both data efficiency and robustness, especially in adversarial or noisy settings.

Method: The authors perform a controlled experimental study using adversarially injected corpora that contain known failure cases, giving ground-truth labels for hallucinations and other errors. They compare different gate configurations (e.g., hallucination-oriented vs reward-based filters, with and without explicit source provenance), different recovery strategies for rejected samples (naive resampling vs adaptive strategies that diagnose failure types and regenerate accordingly), and different generator model scales. Stronger LLM judges are tested both with and without explicit evidence grounding to assess its value.

Result: Evidence-grounded (source-provenance-aware) gating improves faithfulness detection when using stronger LLM judges. Hallucination-based gates and reward-based gates tend to reject different, largely non-overlapping sets of samples, so both are needed for comprehensive filtering. An adaptive recovery pipeline that first diagnoses why a sample failed and then regenerates in a targeted way outperforms naive resampling in yield, recovery rate, and in recalling adversarially injected failures. Despite these advances in filtering and recovery, the scale of the generator model has the largest impact on downstream fine-tuning quality, with gating and recovery providing secondary but still meaningful gains.

Conclusion: In synthetic post-training pipelines, making the filtering process explicitly evidence-grounded and combining complementary gating signals (hallucination and reward) leads to more faithful data selection. Rather than discarding all rejected generations, an adaptive recovery procedure that leverages failure diagnosis and targeted regeneration can substantially increase usable data while better catching adversarial failures. However, the dominant factor in downstream model quality remains the size of the generator model itself, with filtration and recovery strategies serving as important but secondary optimizations.

Abstract: Synthetic post-training pipelines commonly filter generated samples with reward models or holistic LLM judges, yet two practices remain rarely examined together: whether the filtering signal is grounded in the source evidence that induced each generation, and whether rejected samples can be systematically recovered rather than permanently discarded. We present a controlled study of both questions across gate configurations, recovery strategies, and generator scales, using adversarially injected corpora to provide ground-truth failure labels. We find that exact source provenance improves faithfulness gating for stronger judges, that hallucination and reward gates reject largely disjoint sample populations making both necessary, and that an adaptive recovery pipeline combining failure diagnosis with targeted regeneration achieves higher yield, recovery rate, and injection recall than naive resampling. Downstream fine-tuning quality is driven primarily by generator scale, with filtration and recovery conditions contributing meaningfully but secondarily.

</details>


### [64] [Multi-Faceted Interactivity Alignment in Full-Duplex Speech Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11167)
*Atsumoto Ohashi,Neil Zeghidour,Alexandre Défossez,Eugene Kharitonov*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes an RL-based post-training alignment method to make full-duplex spoken dialogue models more interactive and natural across multiple conversational behaviors.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Full-duplex spoken dialogue models can in principle enable natural, simultaneous listen-and-speak conversations, but supervised learning with token-level likelihood fails to directly optimize interaction-level behaviors, leading to unnatural issues such as long silences and poorly timed turn-taking. Existing RL approaches only tackle a narrow subset of interaction problems, lacking a comprehensive treatment of conversational interactivity.

Method: The authors introduce a reinforcement learning post-training framework targeting four canonical axes of interactivity: pause handling, turn-taking, backchanneling, and user interruption. They mine short audio segments from human conversation corpora corresponding to each axis and define axis-specific reward functions to train the model. To ensure the model’s semantic quality does not degrade while optimizing for interactivity, they incorporate an additional LLM-based reward assessing response quality. This method is applied as an alignment step on top of existing full-duplex models.

Result: When applied to two open-source full-duplex spoken dialogue models, Moshi and PersonaPlex, the proposed RL alignment method yields consistent improvements in interactive behaviors. These gains are observed both in offline evaluations using pre-recorded audio as input and in real-time multi-turn dialogue tests, indicating better handling of pauses, turn-taking, backchannel cues, and interruptions without sacrificing response quality.

Conclusion: Comprehensive RL-based post-training alignment on multiple dimensions of interactivity can substantially enhance the real-time conversational behavior of full-duplex spoken dialogue models. By explicitly optimizing for pause handling, turn-taking, backchanneling, and interruption management—while safeguarding semantic quality with an LLM-based reward—the approach provides a more natural, responsive user experience and generalizes across different base models.

Abstract: Full-duplex spoken dialogue models can listen and speak simultaneously, making them a promising architecture for natural conversation. However, current models are trained solely with supervised learning through token-level likelihood maximization, which does not directly optimize interaction-level behaviors, causing interactivity issues such as excessive silence and ill-timed turn-taking. Recent work has applied reinforcement learning (RL) to improve interactivity, but existing methods address only a limited set of interactive behaviors in their rewards. In this work, we propose a post-training alignment method that comprehensively improves the interactivity of full-duplex spoken dialogue models through RL. We address the four canonical axes of interactivity: pause handling, turn-taking, backchanneling, and user interruption. For each axis, we extract short audio segments from human conversation corpora and optimize the model with axis-specific reward functions. An extra LLM-based reward for response quality prevents semantic degradation. We apply our method to two open-source models, Moshi and PersonaPlex, demonstrating consistent improvements in interactivity on both offline evaluation with pre-recorded audio and real-time multi-turn dialogue evaluation.

### [65] [Business World Model](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10044)
*Cecil Pang,Hiroki Sayama*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a "business world model" (BWM): an internal, executable simulator of a business that allows AI agents to plan and evaluate business initiatives from high‑level goals, rather than just execute predefined tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing AI tools in business are mostly narrow: they automate specific, predefined tasks (e.g., prediction, classification, content generation) but do not robustly connect high-level strategic objectives to concrete, multi-step business actions. Businesses need systems that can reason about the business as a whole—its states, constraints, objectives, and possible actions—to autonomously plan initiatives, simulate alternatives, and make trade-offs under uncertainty. The authors aim to formalize such a system for organizational contexts by adapting the world model concept from AI and control theory.

Method: Conceptual and architectural design. The authors define the notion of a Business World Model (BWM) as a semantics-centric representation of business environments. They specify how business states, dynamics, constraints, objectives, and actions are encoded around key business entities. They then propose an architecture that integrates: (1) semantic data representations of business entities; (2) probabilistic ML models for uncertain dynamics and outcome prediction; (3) deterministic business rules for hard constraints and logic; and (4) an explicit action space. These components are organized as an internal simulator that agents can query for planning and counterfactual reasoning ("what if" analyses).

Result: The paper delivers: (1) a formalized concept of a Business World Model specialized for organizational settings; (2) a semantics-centric formulation linking states, dynamics, and actions to core business entities; and (3) a reference architecture that combines semantic data, probabilistic models, business rules, and an explicit action space into a unified planning and simulation framework. There are no empirical benchmark results in the abstract; the contribution is primarily conceptual and architectural.

Conclusion: A Business World Model can serve as the internal simulator needed for autonomous business systems. By organizing existing components (semantic representations, probabilistic models, rules, and action spaces) into a coherent architecture, BWMs allow agents to move beyond instruction-based task execution toward goal-driven planning, simulation of alternative strategies, and counterfactual reasoning about business initiatives. This conceptual foundation could enable more autonomous, strategically aware AI systems in business environments.

Abstract: Businesses are increasingly adopting AI-enabled tools to improve productivity, reduce costs, and enhance products and services. However, the transformative potential of AI extends beyond automating predefined tasks: it lies in enabling intelligent systems to plan, optimize, and execute business initiatives from high-level strategic objectives. This paper introduces the concept and architecture of a business world model (BWM), a world model specialized for business and organizational environments. Inspired by world models in artificial intelligence, cognitive science, and control theory, a BWM encodes business states, dynamics, constraints, objectives, and feasible action space to support autonomous decision-making. We propose a business-semantics-centric formulation in which business states, dynamics and actions are linked to key business entities. Within this framework, agents can simulate alternative action sequences, estimate their effects on future business outcomes, and evaluate trade-offs under uncertainty. The proposed architecture integrates semantic data representations, probabilistic machine learning models, deterministic business rules, and explicit action space into a coherent structure for planning and counterfactual reasoning. Although its individual components are not new, the contribution of BWM lies in organizing them as an executable internal simulator for business initiatives. This work establishes a conceptual foundation for autonomous business systems capable of moving from instruction-based execution toward goal-driven planning and execution.

### [66] [Deployment-Time Memorization in Foundation-Model Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10062)
*Lei,Chen,Guilin Zhang,Kai Zhao,Dalmo Cirne,Andy Olsen,Xu Chu,Zeke Miller,Alet Blanken,Amine Anoun,Jerry Ting*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper analyzes how design choices for long-term memory in foundation-model agents affect personalization benefits, privacy risks, and the ability to truly delete data.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As LLM-based agents increasingly persist user data across sessions, memory is no longer just in model weights but also in explicit storage and summarization layers. Existing work focuses on parametric memorization or static memories, lacking a joint characterization of how memory design impacts both personalization utility and privacy/deletion guarantees during deployment.

Method: The authors formalize deployment-time memorization as a privacy-utility frontier, quantified by Personalization Recall (PR) and Adversarial Extraction Rate (AER). They systematically vary three key memory-design parameters—summarization aggressiveness, retrieval breadth (k), and deletion mode—and introduce a new metric, Forgetting Residue Score (FRS), to measure how much supposedly deleted data remains recoverable from derived memory representations. They evaluate these across agents using models like Gemma 3 12B and GPT-4o-mini on a custom benchmark, LongMemEval.

Result: Key-fact summarization substantially reduces adversarial extraction (canary leakage) while maintaining nearly all personalization recall: canary extraction drops by 76% for Gemma 3 12B and 64% for GPT-4o-mini. Once information is compressed away via summarization, increasing retrieval breadth (k) does not restore leakage. However, this same summarization causes deletion-fidelity issues: if only raw memories are deleted, summarized copies can still leak in roughly 20% of cases. Only full-pipeline purging or applying tombstone-style redaction removes worst-tier residue entirely according to FRS.

Conclusion: Persistent memories in foundation-model agents should be treated and evaluated as core memorization mechanisms. Designers must jointly consider what the memory helps the agent recall (utility), what it enables adversaries to extract (risk), and whether information can be fully erased across all derived tiers. Proper summarization and rigorous, pipeline-wide deletion strategies are necessary to balance personalization with privacy and reliable forgetting.

Abstract: Foundation-model agents are increasingly long-lived systems that remember users across interactions, making memorization an explicit deployment-time function rather than solely a property of model weights. Existing work addresses parametric memorization or audits fixed memory configurations, but does not characterize how memory-design choices jointly shape personalization utility, extraction risk, and deletion fidelity. We study this surface as deployment-time memorization, formulating agent memory as a privacy-utility frontier measured by Personalization Recall (PR) and Adversarial Extraction Rate (AER), and sweeping three memory-design knobs: summarization aggressiveness, retrieval breadth (k), and deletion mode. We further introduce the Forgetting Residue Score (FRS) to quantify whether deleted information remains recoverable from derived memory tiers. On LongMemEval, key-fact summarization reduces canary extraction by 76% on Gemma 3 12B and 64% on GPT-4o-mini while preserving nearly all personalization recall; critically, once content is compressed away, increasing k no longer restores leakage. The same compression, however, induces a deletion-fidelity failure: raw-only deletion leaves derived summary copies recoverable in approximately 20% of instances, and only full-pipeline purge or tombstone redaction drives worst-tier residue to zero. Together, these results establish that persistent agent memory must be evaluated as a first-class memorization mechanism -- assessed by what it helps agents recall, what it makes extractable, and what it can truly erase.

</details>


### [67] [Exploratory Responsiveness and Adaptive Rigidity under AI-Assisted Optimization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10086)
*Balaraju Battu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper builds a dynamic theory explaining when AI-assisted prediction will either suppress or enhance long-run human/institutional exploration and adaptability.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As AI tools increasingly optimize decisions and predictions, there is concern that they might lock individuals and institutions into locally optimal but globally suboptimal patterns, undermining creativity, resilience, and long-run adaptation. Existing discussions are mostly qualitative; the authors want a formal, dynamical account of how AI’s predictive assistance interacts with exploratory behavior across time and institutional contexts.

Method: The authors construct a dynamical systems model of cognitive, institutional, and technological evolution on rugged epistemic landscapes with multiple local optima. They define a key state variable, adaptive responsiveness, capturing a system’s capacity to explore unfamiliar conceptual and institutional trajectories. Within this framework, they introduce parameters for AI predictive assistance that can either substitute for or complement exploratory engagement, and analyze resulting regimes characterized by metastability, hysteresis, convergence, collapse, or enhanced exploration.

Result: The model shows that when AI is deployed in convergent predictive regimes, it tends to substitute for human/institutional exploration, thereby reducing adaptive responsiveness. This leads to metastable trapping in local optima, hysteresis effects, premature convergence, and dynamics where systems become locally efficient but globally rigid. By contrast, under exploration-enhancing regimes—where AI is designed and embedded to support search and conceptual traversal—AI can increase exploratory mobility across the landscape. The degree of substitution or complementarity is endogenously dependent on the existing level of adaptive responsiveness.

Conclusion: The long-run adaptive impact of AI is not determined solely by its predictive power, but by how it is integrated into systems with varying baseline levels of exploratory responsiveness. Low-responsiveness systems are prone to AI-induced exploratory substitution and rigidity, whereas high-responsiveness systems can harness AI to expand exploration. Consequently, institutional design, developmental context, and human–machine interface architecture are crucial levers for ensuring that AI strengthens rather than undermines adaptive responsiveness over time.

Abstract: This paper develops a theory of exploratory adaptation under AI-assisted optimization. The central argument is that the long-run adaptive effects of AI systems depend critically on how predictive assistance interacts with exploratory responsiveness itself. We formalize this mechanism using a dynamical framework in which cognitive, institutional, and technological systems evolve over rugged epistemic landscapes characterized by multiple locally reinforced configurations. A central state variable in the model is adaptive responsiveness, which measures the capacity of a system to traverse unfamiliar conceptual and institutional trajectories under changing conditions. Under convergent predictive regimes, AI systems substitute for exploratory engagement, reducing adaptive responsiveness and generating metastable trapping, hysteresis, premature convergence, and exploration-collapse dynamics in which systems become locally efficient but globally rigid. The framework also identifies contrasting exploration-enhancing regimes in which AI systems amplify exploratory search, conceptual traversal, and adaptive mobility. The effective substitution parameter is therefore responsiveness-dependent: systems possessing weak exploratory routines are more vulnerable to exploratory substitution, whereas systems already possessing high adaptive responsiveness may use AI assistance to expand exploratory mobility across rugged landscapes. The long-run adaptive effects of AI consequently depend not only on AI capability itself, but also on institutional structure, developmental context, and the architecture of human-machine interaction.

### [68] [Predictive Assistance and the Temporal Dynamics of Exploratory Compression](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10094)
*Balaraju Battu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a mathematical framework for how predictive AI assistance can prematurely stabilize human problem-solving, reducing exploration and changing how attention moves through strategy space.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Classical cognitive theories emphasize that humans learn and solve problems by exploring and gradually building efficient internal representations. Predictive AI tools now often give answers or strong hints before such internal exploration occurs, but we lack a formal account of how this changes the dynamics of human exploration and learning. The authors aim to capture this impact in a rigorous, dynamical model.

Method: They construct a geometric dynamical systems model where attention moves over a continuous landscape of problem-solving strategies. The dynamics include: (1) a stabilizing drift toward already-favored strategies, (2) intrinsic exploratory perturbations that promote diversification, and (3) learning rules whose strength depends on current responsiveness. Predictive AI is modeled as an exogenous force that compresses exploration by pulling trajectories toward specific strategies, effectively stabilizing them early. They analyze how this exogenous stabilization affects the geometry and dynamics of attention and exploration over time.

Result: The model shows three key effects of predictive assistance: (1) Long-lasting predictive stabilization diminishes the effective impact of intrinsic exploratory noise, so people remain variable but less responsive in how their exploration changes strategy usage. (2) The geometry of the strategy landscape develops asymmetric curvature over time, creating hysteresis: once trajectories have been stabilized, it takes longer and different conditions to restore prior levels of exploratory mobility after assistance is removed. (3) The developmental trajectory of a learner is highly sensitive to when stabilization occurs; early application of predictive assistance restricts later exploration before broad internal representations have formed, leading to narrower future paths through strategy space.

Conclusion: Predictive AI does not just speed up problem solving; it can fundamentally reshape the geometry and temporal dynamics of human exploratory cognition. By prematurely stabilizing attention and strategy use, such systems may reduce responsiveness to intrinsic exploration, induce hysteresis and delayed recovery once assistance is withdrawn, and constrain long-term representational development—especially when introduced early. The framework yields concrete, testable predictions about changes in exploratory entropy, premature convergence, and slow recovery that can guide empirical research on human–AI interaction.

Abstract: Classical theories of cognition describe problem solving as exploratory search through structured problem spaces in which repeated interaction gradually compresses search into efficient representational structures. Predictive artificial intelligence systems introduce a distinct regime in which stabilization may occur before exploratory diversification unfolds, supplying solutions and decision trajectories prior to internally generated search. This paper develops a geometric dynamical framework in which attention evolves over a landscape of strategies shaped by stabilizing drift, endogenous exploratory perturbation, and responsiveness-gated learning. Predictive assistance is modeled as a process of exogenous exploratory compression that stabilizes trajectories before self-generated exploration broadens the accessible regions of strategy space. The framework yields three main results. First, sustained predictive stabilization reduces exploratory responsiveness by attenuating the effective influence of intrinsic perturbations even when exploratory variability remains present. Second, curvature accumulates and relaxes asymmetrically, producing hysteresis and delayed recovery of exploratory mobility after assistance withdrawal. Third, developmental outcomes depend critically on the timing of stabilization, with early intervention narrowing future exploratory traversal before broad representational diversification has occurred. The framework generates empirically testable predictions concerning exploratory entropy, premature convergence, and delayed recovery following predictive stabilization. More broadly, the results suggest that predictive systems may reshape the geometry of exploratory cognition itself.

### [69] [From Senses to Decisions: The Information Flow of Auditory and Visual Perception in Multimodal LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10147)
*Wish Suharitdamrong,Muhammad Awais,Xiatian Zhu,Sara Atito*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper investigates how audio and visual information flows inside Audio-Visual Large Language Models (AVLLMs), revealing structured routing patterns and showing that many non-text tokens can be safely discarded after their information is absorbed by the LLM, improving efficiency.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While Multimodal Large Language Models are widely used and can process audio and visual inputs, we lack a clear understanding of how these different modalities propagate through the internal layers to influence final predictions. This gap limits interpretability, principled architecture design, and opportunities for efficient inference in AVLLMs.

Method: The authors analyze internal attention and information routing patterns in AVLLMs under two settings: (1) continuous audio-visual video inputs and (2) multiple interleaved audio-visual items. They trace how audio and visual tokens interact with LLM tokens across layers and heads, measure each modality's contribution as a function of task requirements, and experiment with pruning audio-visual and other non-LLM tokens once their information has been transferred to the LLM backbone. They conduct these analyses on Qwen2.5-Omni and Video-SALMONN2 Plus at 3B and 7B scales across diverse tasks and datasets.

Result: For video inputs, AVLLMs follow a largely sequential information flow pathway previously observed in VLMs and Video LLMs, with audio and visual contributions along this pathway scaling with how much the task depends on each modality. For multiple interleaved audio-visual items, routing reorganizes into different parallel streams instead of a single sequential path. The study further finds that once audio-visual and other non-LLM tokens have passed their information into LLM tokens, they can be dropped with minimal performance loss or even slight gains, consistently across models, tasks, and datasets, yielding more efficient inference.

Conclusion: AVLLMs exhibit consistent, structured patterns in how they route and integrate audio and visual information, reusing known sequential pathways for single video inputs and shifting to parallel streams for multiple interleaved items. After information transfer to LLM tokens, many audio-visual and other tokens become redundant and can be removed with little to no quality loss, enabling efficiency improvements. These insights provide the first cohesive picture of internal audio-visual information flow in AVLLMs and offer a foundation for more interpretable, better-designed, and more efficient multimodal models.

Abstract: Multimodal Large Language Models (MLLMs) can listen and see, but how do audio and visual signals actually travel through the network to shape an answer? Despite their growing role in research and real-world applications, the internal pathways through which audio and visual tokens influence the final prediction remain poorly understood. In this study, we examine audio-visual information flow inside Audio-Visual Large Language Models (AVLLMs), tracing how AVLLMs route, utilize, and integrate audio and visual information across two input configurations, audio-visual video and multiple interleaved audio-visual items. We find that for audio-visual video, AVLLMs follow the sequential information flow pathway established for VLMs and VideoLLMs, with audio and visual contribution flowing along this pathway in proportion to the task's reliance on each modality. In settings with multiple interleaved audio-visual items, this routing shifts to different parallel streams. Furthermore, we demonstrate that audio-visual and other token types can be discarded once their information is transferred to LLM, with minimal impact on the model's prediction or even slight improvement, generalizing across multiple tasks and datasets, enabling more efficient inference. These findings hold across multiple models and scales, Qwen2.5-Omni and Video-SALMONN2 Plus at 3B and 7B scales, leading to hypotheses on why these flow structures emerge. Together, these results deliver the first coherent picture of how AVLLMs orchestrate sound and sight inside the network and lay the groundwork for the next wave of interpretability, design, and efficiency advances in audio-visual and broader MLLMs.

### [70] [Less Context, Better Agents: Efficient Context Engineering for Long-Horizon Tool-Using LLM Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10209)
*Abhilasha Lodha,Mahsa Pahlavikhah Varnosfaderani,Abir Chakraborty,Abhinav Mithal*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper evaluates context management strategies for large language model agents using enterprise tools, showing that pruning recent tool interactions and adding summarization greatly improves both expense itemization accuracy and efficiency over using full conversation history or no user model.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Enterprise LLM agents interacting with internal systems receive long, verbose tool outputs, which can overflow context windows, lead to outdated state, and raise inference costs. The authors aim to understand how to manage and compress these tool interactions so agents stay accurate and efficient in a realistic business workflow: automated expense itemization in Microsoft Dynamics 365.

Method: They run a 50-task hotel expense benchmark using Model Context Protocol tools within Dynamics 365 and compare four GPT-5 configurations: (1) no user model, (2) full conversation history, (3) context pruned to the last five tool call/response pairs, and (4) the same pruning plus automated summarization of earlier interactions. They keep the user model fixed across context strategies, repeat each setup over five runs, and measure completion rates, token usage, and runtime, along with confidence intervals, effect sizes, sensitivity analyses, failure modes, breakdowns by expense type, and cross-model checks with Claude Sonnet 4.5.

Result: The baseline with no user model performs poorly, with only 8.0% of expenses fully itemized. Retaining full history boosts completion to 71.0% but is extremely costly (about 1.48M tokens and 14.56 hours per benchmark). Pruning to the last five tool interactions further improves completion to 79.0% while cutting tokens to 535k and runtime to 5.39 hours. Adding summarization on top of pruning yields the best performance: 91.6% complete itemization and 99.64% of total amount correctly itemized, with roughly 553k tokens and 5.79 hours. Confidence intervals, effect sizes, sensitivity to window sizes, per-category expense results, and cross-model tests all support the conclusion that selective retention plus summarization outperforms full-history retention.

Conclusion: For enterprise workflows that rely heavily on tool calls, simply keeping the entire conversation history is neither necessary nor optimal. Instead, keeping only a small window of recent tool interactions and summarizing older context improves reliability and accuracy while significantly reducing token usage and runtime. This structured context management approach generalizes across models and expense categories and is a promising design pattern for scalable, cost-efficient enterprise LLM agents.

Abstract: Large language models deployed as autonomous agents for enterprise workflows face a key challenge: verbose tool responses from enterprise systems can cause context overflow, stale-state errors, and high inference cost. We study this problem in automated expense itemization in Microsoft Dynamics 365 Finance and Operations using Model Context Protocol tools. We evaluate four GPT-5 configurations on a 50-task hotel expense benchmark: no user model, full conversation history, context pruned to the last 5 tool call/response pairs, and pruning with automated summarization. Results are averaged across 5 independent runs, with the user model held constant for the context-engineering comparison. The no-user-model baseline achieves only 8.0% complete itemization. Full-context retention improves completion to 71.0%, but consumes 1,480,996 tokens and 14.56 hours per benchmark. Pruning to the last 5 tool calls improves completion to 79.0% while reducing token use to 535,274 and runtime to 5.39 hours. Adding summarization achieves the best result: 91.6% complete itemization and 99.64% average amount itemized, with 553,374 tokens and 5.79 hours. We further report confidence intervals, effect-size analysis, sensitivity over pruning and summary windows, failure analysis, results across five expense types grouped into three categories, and cross-model evidence with Claude Sonnet 4.5. These results show that, for this class of enterprise tool-use workflow, selective retention of recent tool interactions plus compact summarization can improve both reliability and efficiency compared with full-history retention.

### [71] [Minimalist Genetic Programming](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10237)
*Leonardo Trujillo*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces Minimalist Genetic Programming (MGP), a program-induction method inspired by linguistic minimalism that builds symbolic models via a MERGE-based syntactic process and outperforms standard GP on hard symbolic regression tasks with bloat issues.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Traditional genetic programming treats learning as evolutionary search over program trees, but often struggles with issues like bloat and failure to recover exact target expressions on challenging symbolic regression benchmarks. The authors are motivated to rethink the second core idea of GP—the evolutionary search mechanism—by drawing on insights from the Minimalist Program in linguistics, where syntax arises from an optimal computational solution (MERGE) to linking cognitive systems. They aim to see whether a minimalist, syntax-driven derivation process can yield more compact, accurate program induction.

Method: They propose Minimalist Genetic Programming (MGP), which replaces evolutionary search with a syntactic derivation process inspired by the Minimalist Program. MGP uses a binary set-formation operator, MERGE, as its core computational step to incrementally build expression trees from a predefined lexicon of atomic syntactic objects (the basic functions and terminals). The system discovers useful building blocks and composes them using a simple Markovian MERGE process to induce symbolic expressions solving regression tasks. They then benchmark MGP against standard GP on symbolic regression problems known to induce bloat.

Result: On a suite of difficult symbolic regression benchmarks where standard GP often bloats and fails to recover the exact ground-truth expression, MGP consistently identifies the exact target model when provided with an appropriate lexicon of atomic syntactic elements. This demonstrates both better control of bloat and higher solution accuracy compared to standard GP in these scenarios.

Conclusion: The authors conclude that insights from the Minimalist Program in linguistics, particularly the MERGE-based view of syntax, are valuable for program induction. Minimalist Genetic Programming shows strong potential by constructing symbolic expressions via syntactic derivation rather than evolutionary search, yielding exact solutions on challenging benchmarks where standard GP struggles. They argue that this minimalist, syntax-driven approach should be further explored and developed for broader program induction applications.

Abstract: Genetic programming (GP) is based on two important insights. First, that any learning task can fundamentally be posed as a program induction problem, where the goal is to construct a symbolic hierarchical model that is expressed as a syntax tree. Second, to pose this task as a search problem, and use evolution to locate the desired model. Since it was proposed, GP has produced notable results in a wide range of tasks and problem domains. This work presents an alternative view by modifying the second core insight of GP, posing the problem as a syntactic derivation task instead. In particular, this paper presents Minimalist Genetic Programming (MGP), an algorithm that like GP is biologically inspired, but instead of evolution it takes inspiration from the Minimalist Program to human language, in which syntax is understood as an optimal solution to the problem of linking two other mental systems. In minimalism, the core computational process is a binary set formation operator called $MERGE$, than can be used to incrementally construct complex syntactic structures using a simple Markovian process. MGP is able to discover the core building blocks of the symbolic expressions, and to incrementally combined them using $MERGE$. The proposed system is benchmarked on symbolic regression tasks that are known to be difficult to solve with standard GP systems because of the propensity for bloat. Results show that when a proper lexicon of atomic syntactic objects are chosen, MGP is able to consistently produce the exact ground truth model on a set of symbolic regression where standard GP struggles to do the same. The insights provided by minimalism are shown to be relevant to the problem of program induction, and should be explored further based on the potential exhibited by MGP in this work.

### [72] [Regimes: An Auditable, Held-Out-Gated Improvement Loop Demonstrated on LongMemEval with ActiveGraph](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10241)
*Yohei Nakajima*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper presents an event-sourced agent runtime (ActiveGraph) and a controlled improvement loop (Regimes) that makes autonomous model self-improvement auditable, replayable, and target-agnostic, yielding measurable accuracy gains on a long-term memory evaluation benchmark.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Autonomous improvement loops for agents are hard to trust because current approaches rely on external, bolt-on scaffolding. This leads to unlogged failures, irreproducible diagnoses, and promotion decisions that live outside the agent’s own history, undermining auditability, reliability, and scientific rigor. The authors aim to provide a principled substrate where improvement is a first-class, inspectable workflow tightly integrated into the agent’s state and execution model.

Method: They use an event-sourced agent runtime, ActiveGraph, where the agent state is a deterministic projection of an append-only event log. All actions, failures, diagnoses, and promotion/discard decisions are represented as typed events. On top of this they implement Regimes, a controlled improvement loop that: (1) detects failed evaluations, (2) diagnoses the failure and routes it to a specific pipeline location via a failure-regime taxonomy, (3) proposes a localized patch (e.g., prompt modification) at that pipeline seam, and (4) gates promotion via static checks, sandboxed execution, in-sample evaluation, and held-out validation. The loop is target-agnostic, interfacing with different tasks through a common evaluation interface.

Result: On the LongMemEval-S benchmark they find that the main failure mode is not retrieval but reconciliation: relevant evidence is already present in context, but the reader still answers incorrectly. Applying Regimes to discover and promote reader-prompt repairs yields held-out accuracy improvements of +0.05 to +0.10 on four of five seeded splits and +0.01 on one split where over-promotion occurs. Two splits yield individually significant gains (with caveats for seed 5 due to its sequential promotion design). Because all splits draw from a shared pool of 500 questions, pooled statistics are treated as descriptive rather than inferential.

Conclusion: ActiveGraph provides an auditable, event-sourced substrate that makes controlled, replayable improvement loops practical for agents. Regimes demonstrates a held-out-gated, target-agnostic improvement loop that can discover meaningful prompt repairs, particularly for reconciliation failures in long-context reading. The work contributes: (1) the event-sourced runtime for transparent improvement, (2) the general held-out-gated improvement loop, (3) a failure-regime taxonomy that routes failures to precise pipeline locations (whose incremental benefit versus an unrouted baseline remains an open question), and (4) the idea of using prompts themselves as discovery probes for failure modes and potential repairs.

Abstract: Autonomous improvement loops are hard to trust because the improvement process is usually external scaffolding bolted onto the agent: failures go unlogged, diagnoses cannot be replayed, and promote-or-discard decisions land in a side database rather than the agent's own history. We show that an event-sourced agent runtime removes that friction and turns controlled improvement into a first-class workflow. When the agent's state is a deterministic projection of an append-only event log, failures are recorded, a run replays exactly from its log, candidate patches scope to typed pipeline seams, gates are auditable, and every promotion or discard is itself an event. We demonstrate this with Regimes, a loop on the ActiveGraph runtime that diagnoses failed evaluations, proposes a repair at a pipeline point, and promotes it only after static checks, sandbox execution, in-sample evaluation, and held-out validation. The loop is target-agnostic: the same control flow runs against different tasks through a common interface. On LongMemEval-S the dominant failure is not retrieval but reconciliation: the evidence is already in the assembled context, yet the reader answers incorrectly. Across five seeded held-out splits, Regimes discovers reader-prompt repairs that improve final held-out accuracy by +0.05 to +0.10 in four splits and +0.01 in one over-promotion split; two splits are individually significant (seed 5 unadjusted for its sequential promotion structure), and the pooled count is descriptive only, since the splits share one 500-question pool. The durable contributions are ActiveGraph as an auditable substrate that makes controlled improvement loops tractable, the held-out-gated loop it supports, the failure-regime taxonomy routing each failure to a pipeline location (whose marginal value over an unrouted baseline is the primary open question), and the prompt-as-discovery-probe hypothesis.

### [73] [RealMath-Eval: Why SOTA Judges Struggle with Real Human Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10254)
*Yiteng Mao,Kenan Xu,Yijia Lyu,Wenhao Li,Jianlong Chen,Xiangfeng Wang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces RealMath-Eval, a benchmark of real high-school exam responses, and shows that LLMs that grade math solutions perform much worse on real student work than on synthetic LLM-generated solutions.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although LLMs solve high-school math problems well, we do not know how well they can grade or evaluate real students’ diverse reasoning processes. Existing evaluation pipelines often use synthetic, model-generated solutions, which may not reflect real student errors. The authors want to measure and understand how well LLMs function as graders on authentic student work.

Method: The authors build RealMath-Eval, a benchmark of 224 real-world high-school exam responses with rigorous human annotations. They evaluate state-of-the-art LLMs as "judges" that assign grades to these responses and compare the model grades to expert human grading using Mean Squared Error. As a control, they run the same judges on synthetic, LLM-generated solutions and compare performance. They then perform semantic embedding analysis to study the structure of error spaces for synthetic vs human solutions, and use generative probability (surprisal) probes to quantify how out-of-distribution human reasoning is for current models. They also test style-transfer techniques that rewrite human answers into more LLM-like text to see if that narrows the gap.

Result: LLM judges show high error (MSE around 2.96) when grading real student responses, but significantly lower error (MSE around 1.17) when grading synthetic solutions, revealing a large Evaluation Gap. Embedding analysis shows synthetic errors collapse into low-dimensional, predictable subspaces, unlike the more diverse, high-variance error patterns in human work. Probability-based probes indicate that transitions in human reasoning have higher surprisal for the models, confirming that human solutions are more out-of-distribution. Style-transfer that makes student responses look more like LLM text does not substantially improve grading accuracy.

Conclusion: Current LLM-based grading systems that are primarily trained and validated on synthetic, LLM-generated solutions substantially overestimate their ability to handle real student math reasoning. Human errors are structurally and distributionally different from synthetic errors, making them harder for LLMs to evaluate. Improving LLM graders will require benchmarks and training data that capture the full diversity and unpredictability of authentic student work, not just synthetic approximations.

Abstract: While Large Language Models (LLMs) have achieved near-perfect performance in \emph{solving} high-school mathematics, their ability to \emph{evaluate} the diverse reasoning processes of real human students remains under-examined. To bridge this gap, we introduce \textbf{RealMath-Eval}, a rigorously annotated benchmark of 224 real-world exam responses from high schools. Our initial evaluation reveals that even state-of-the-art LLM judges struggle significantly on this task, exhibiting a high Mean Squared Error ($\sim$2.96) against expert human grading. To probe a plausible explanation, we contrast this performance with a control setting where the same judges evaluate synthetic LLM-generated solutions. We identify a stark ``Evaluation Gap'': judges are considerably more accurate and consistent on synthetic text (MSE $\sim$1.17) but struggle to generalize to authentic student reasoning. Through semantic embedding analysis, we find that synthetic errors suffer from a ``structural collapse'' into predictable, low-dimensional linear subspaces, whereas human errors form a more diverse error space. Furthermore, generative probability probes suggest that human reasoning involves significantly higher information-theoretic surprisal, indicating that student reasoning transitions are more out-of-distribution for current models. Finally, we find that surface-level style transfer fails to close this gap. Our findings suggest that current LLM evaluation pipelines relying heavily on synthetic data may not adequately capture the diversity of authentic student mathematical reasoning.

### [74] [Supervised Fine-tuning with Synthetic Rationale Data Hurts Real-World Disease Prediction](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10279)
*Buxin Su,Bingxuan Li,Cheng Qian,Yiwei Wang,Jin Jin,Bingxin Zhao*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper investigates whether supervised fine-tuning (SFT) with synthetic rationales improves clinical prediction tasks and finds that, contrary to common belief, it significantly harms performance for five-year ADRD prediction compared to label-only fine-tuning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: There is a widespread assumption that teaching models both what to predict and why, via rationale-based SFT, enhances language model performance, especially in high-stakes domains like clinical prediction. The authors want to rigorously test this assumption in the context of predicting Alzheimer's disease and related dementias using longitudinal health data.

Method: The authors run a large-scale controlled experiment with 504 configurations to compare rationale-based SFT against label-only fine-tuning for five-year ADRD prediction. They evaluate across different model families, training data scales, and also with a reasoning-oriented base model. They further assess rationale quality via human expert annotation and run few-shot inference-time experiments using the same rationales as demonstrations instead of training targets.

Result: Rationale-based SFT consistently and substantially degrades prediction performance relative to label-only fine-tuning, across all tested model families and data scales, and even when using a reasoning-focused base model. Expert evaluation shows the rationales are medically accurate and grounded in patient-specific evidence, and few-shot experiments reveal that these rationales can indeed improve performance when used at inference time rather than as training supervision.

Conclusion: The performance degradation is attributed to a structural conflict between optimizing for narrative plausibility in rationales and for discriminative accuracy in predictions. Rationale-based supervision does not universally help and can be harmful in this clinical prediction setting. The authors argue that a more nuanced understanding of when rationale-based SFT is beneficial is necessary, to support responsible deployment of language models in high-stakes clinical applications.

Abstract: Supervised fine-tuning with synthetic rationale data is widely assumed to improve language model performance on clinical prediction tasks by teaching models not just what to predict but why. We test this assumption on five-year Alzheimer's disease and related dementias (ADRD) prediction from longitudinal health histories. Across a large-scale controlled experiment of 504 configurations, we find that rationale-based SFT consistently and substantially hurts prediction performance relative to label-only fine-tuning. The degradation persists across model families and data scales, and is not resolved by using a reasoning-oriented base model. Crucially, the failure is not explained by poor rationale quality: human expert annotation confirms that the generated rationales are medically accurate and faithfully grounded in patient-specific evidence, and few-shot experiments show that the same rationales improve performance when used as inference-time demonstrations rather than training targets. We identify the root cause as a structural conflict between narrative plausibility and discriminative optimization. We hope our work paves the path toward a more precise understanding of when and how rationale-based supervision helps and when it does not, guiding the responsible development of language models for high-stakes clinical prediction.

### [75] [Sim2Schedule: A Simulator-Guided LLM Framework for Autonomous Open-Pit Mine Scheduling](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10286)
*Mustavi Ibne Masum,Thiago Eustaquio Alves de Oliveira,Mahzabeen Emu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a simulator-constrained LLM agent that generates open-pit mine schedules nearly as good as MILP while being faster and more scalable.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Traditional open-pit mine scheduling with MILP yields optimal economic plans but suffers from exponential computational complexity and poor adaptability to real-time, dynamic industrial conditions. There is a need for methods that can handle complex geotechnical and operational constraints while remaining computationally scalable, interpretable, and deployable in secure, closed industrial environments without heavy ML infrastructure or retraining.

Method: The authors design a scheduling framework where a Large Language Model acts as an autonomous agent that sequentially decides extraction and processing actions. A custom simulator enforces geotechnical precedence, extraction-processing coupling, and dynamic capacity constraints by constraining the LLM’s action space at each step. The system runs zero-shot, without fine-tuning or cloud inference, and generates full mine schedules. For benchmarking, the authors also formulate a new MILP model that encodes realistic operational and geotechnical constraints, and then compare LLM and MILP performance on multiple instances of different sizes and horizons.

Result: Across a variety of mining instances, the simulator-guided LLM schedules achieve between 94% and 99% of the Net Present Value obtained by the MILP optimal solution. While MILP computation time grows rapidly with problem size, the LLM framework exhibits approximately linear scaling of computation time with instance size and planning horizon. The LLM solutions are interpretable and produced within the constraints enforced by the simulator.

Conclusion: Simulator-constrained LLM agents can approximate the economic optimality of MILP-based open-pit mine schedules while offering far better computational scalability and practical deployability in dynamic, secure industrial settings. This suggests they are a viable, scalable alternative to classical optimization for long-horizon scheduling problems with complex operational constraints, and highlights a promising paradigm of coupling LLMs with domain simulators for industrial decision-making.

Abstract: Open-pit mine scheduling is a critical process for maximizing economic return under complex geotechnical and operational constraints. While Mixed-Integer Linear Programming (MILP) provides mathematically optimal baselines, its exponential computational complexity and inability to adapt in real time limit its practical deployment in dynamic industrial environments. This work introduces a simulator-driven Large Language Model (LLM) scheduling framework in which the LLM acts as an autonomous decision-making agent, guided at each step by a custom simulator that encodes geotechnical precedence, extraction-processing coupling, and dynamic capacity constraints directly into the action generation mechanism. Operating entirely zero-shot within a closed, data-secure environment, the framework produces complete, interpretable extraction and processing schedules without cloud-based inference, domain-specific fine-tuning, or retraining. To provide a trustworthy performance benchmark, a novel MILP formulation is developed that incorporates realistic operational and geotechnical constraints. Evaluated across mining instances of varying scale and time periods, the LLM-based framework recovers between 94\% and 99\% of the MILP optimal NPV while scaling linearly in computation time. These results position simulator-constrained LLM agents as a practical and scalable alternative to classical optimization for long-horizon industrial scheduling under complex operational constraints.

### [76] [From Context-Aware to Conflict-Aware: Generalizing Contrastive Decoding for Knowledge Conflict in LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10298)
*Runze Jiang,Taiqiang Wu,Yan Wang,Bingyu Zhu,Longtao Huang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Summary generation failed


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Motivation analysis unavailable

Method: Method extraction failed

Result: Result analysis unavailable

Conclusion: Conclusion extraction failed

Abstract: When large language models generate from retrieved or augmented contexts, conflicts between external context and parametric priors remain a central reliability bottleneck. Existing contrastive decoding methods follow a \emph{context-aware} paradigm that unilaterally amplifies context over parametric priors, overwriting correct priors when the context is erroneous. We generalize this to the \textbf{conflict-aware} paradigm that dynamically allocates authority between prior and context based on conflict signals, rather than presupposing context trustworthiness. We show that the affine combination of prior and context logits yields a \textbf{power family} with an inherent \textbf{regime asymmetry}: extrapolation amplifies errors unboundedly when the prior is correct, interpolation under-corrects when the context is correct, and no static regime covers both. Existing contrastive decoding methods are instances of this family, mostly extrapolative. To evaluate both conflict directions, we propose TriState-Bench, a model-aware evaluation protocol that calibrates per-model prior knowledge to measure three conflict states: correction, resistance, and agreement. To resolve the asymmetry, we propose Adaptive Regime Routing (ARR), which routes between regimes at each step, lifting resistance EM from below 6 to 16--33 without sacrificing correction or agreement. Our code is available at https://github.com/keith-Jiang/conflict-aware-decoding.

### [77] [What Spatial Memory Must Store: Occlusion as the Test for Language-Agent Memory](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10299)
*Doeon Kwon,Junho Bang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper empirically tests how geometric structure should be used in language-agent “memory palace” systems and finds that geometry must lead recall in spatial tasks and that visibility (occlusion) must be modeled separately from memory recall, validating simple geometric predicates for large gains.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Language-agent memory systems often attach memories to 3D world coordinates, motivated by the intuition that spatial geometry enables better recall and reasoning than text alone. However, this intuition is usually untested and underspecified: it is unclear whether and how geometry should influence memory retrieval, particularly in spatial query regimes and occlusion scenarios (e.g., objects behind walls). The authors aim to rigorously measure when geometry helps, how it should be weighted versus recency/importance, and to separate what spatial memory needs to store from how it is read and used during perception and recall.

Method: The authors conduct pre-registered experiments on a “memory palace” system used by language agents. They compare different memory retrieval weightings: (1) a baseline position-blind retrieval using recency and importance only; (2) the system’s default linear blend of spatial proximity with recency and importance; and (3) a geometry-led weighting where spatial distance dominates retrieval when queries are spatial. They also introduce a geometric visibility predicate using a ray-versus-voxel DDA algorithm applied to the existing gaze ray and field-of-view cone, to determine whether stored objects are occluded by walls. They test this on behind-wall targets, compare recall versus visibility, and examine near-duplicate locations where cosine similarity on text fails but coordinate-based recall succeeds. Finally, they run a live, pre-registered study across multiple scripted worlds with automated oracle scoring to validate the occlusion predicate and identify implementation defects.

Result: 1) The default linear blend that treats spatial proximity as just another factor alongside recency and importance does not improve recall and can degrade it: in a pre-registered frozen test it performs no better than a position-blind baseline (mean ΔHit@5 -0.0375, p=0.306), while a geometry-led weighting significantly outperforms the baseline (+0.3208, p<10^-15). 2) They show that memory recall (which should be occlusion-blind) and visibility (which depends on geometry) are distinct, and they implement a one-line DDA-based visibility check that, combined with the agent’s cone of view, successfully identifies behind-wall targets: text-only or FoV-only scoring yields 0.000 hit rate on 849 behind-wall targets, whereas cone-plus-DDA achieves 0.982 (exact McNemar p<10^-6). Coordinate-based retrieval also perfectly disambiguates near-duplicate locations (1.000 vs. 0.533 accuracy, n=150) where text cosine similarity fails. 3) In a live, pre-registered occlusion study over eight scripted worlds and 96 behind-wall targets, the visibility predicate drives false-visible rate from 1.000 to 0.000 with extremely strong statistical significance (pooled exact McNemar p=2.5×10^-29) and reveals a real relay anchor bug that gets fixed.

Conclusion: Geometric information in language-agent memory systems must be used in a geometry-led way for spatial queries rather than as a small additive signal beside recency and importance. Moreover, memory recall should remain occlusion-blind while visibility must be represented as a separate geometric predicate derived from spatial data (e.g., via a simple DDA-based ray casting using the agent’s gaze). The work isolates what spatial memory must store (world coordinates and geometry) from how it should be read (recall vs. visibility), provides quantitative evidence that naive blends underuse geometry, and demonstrates that lightweight geometric visibility checks can massively improve handling of occlusion. These pilot experiments underpin a frozen confirmatory study, with a larger, human-rated multi-world evaluation planned for the future.

Abstract: Language-agent "memory palace" systems anchor each memory to a world coordinate, on the intuition that geometry adds something text cannot. We make that intuition testable and report three results. First, the memory-palace default of folding spatial proximity into a linear blend beside recency and importance does not help and can hurt: in a pre-registered recall experiment the shipped blend fails its own frozen test (mean Delta-Hit@5 -0.0375, Wilcoxon p=0.306), sitting at a position-blind baseline, while a geometry-led weighting wins decisively (+0.3208, p<10^-15): geometry must lead recall when the query regime is spatial. Second, memory recall and visibility must be separated: recall is occlusion-blind by design (you correctly remember the next room behind a wall), while visibility is a perception predicate over stored geometry that the live system never computed. A one-line ray-versus-voxel digital differential analyzer (DDA), re-pointed from the gaze ray the agent already casts, supplies it: text and the live FoV cone both score 0.000 on 849 behind-wall targets while cone-plus-DDA reaches 0.982 (exact McNemar p<10^-6); coordinate recall separately resolves near-duplicate locations a cosine null cannot (1.000 vs 0.533, n=150). Third, the visibility predicate is confirmed live under a git-committed pre-registration (SPMEM-OCC-LIVE-v1: eight scripted worlds, automated oracle scoring, 96 behind-wall targets, false-visible 1.000->0.000, pooled exact McNemar p=2.5x10^-29), a run that surfaced and fixed a real relay anchor defect. We concede that occlusion-needs-geometry is near-tautological; the contribution is the measurement and isolation, separating what spatial memory must store from how it is read. These pilots power a frozen confirmatory study (SPMEM-ZERO-REAL-PREREG-v1); the full human-authored multi-world study with blind raters remains future work.

To ensure rigorous spatial validity, the system leverages map-constrained routing reconstruction to recalculate the physical transitions between these LLM agent-modified staypoints. *Haoyu Dong*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

There is a need to leverage visual feedback about these defects during training to improve code quality and visual fidelity without extra inference-time cost.

Method: They introduce Visual-SDPO, a self-distillation policy optimization framework where a teacher model has access to privileged visual feedback (rendered images and execution errors) and distills this knowledge into a student model that only sees standard inputs at inference. They add Visual-Grounded Code Credit Weighting to map each detected visual defect back to specific code statements and increase learning signal on those tokens. They also incorporate a sequence-level GRPO term to reward entire code rollouts that execute successfully and produce visually high-quality artifacts. Failed executions are still used for learning via the teacher’s privileged context and self-distillation.

Result: Using a unified Qwen3-VL-8B-Instruct backbone on chart-to-code, UI-to-code, and slide-generation benchmarks (ChartMimic, Design2Code, AeSlides), Visual-SDPO yields more than 10 absolute point improvements over the zero-shot base model on primary metrics, and at least 2.4 point gains over GRPO, with fewer training steps and without additional inference-time cost.

Conclusion: Visual-feedback self-distillation via Visual-SDPO effectively exploits rendered artifacts and execution feedback to significantly improve the visual quality and correctness of code-generated charts, UIs, and slides, outperforming standard policy optimization approaches while remaining efficient at inference.

Abstract: Code-generating large language models (LLMs) increasingly produce visual artifacts such as charts, web pages, and slides by writing programs that are executed by non-differentiable renderers, committing to code before observing the render. As a result, otherwise executable code often yields artifacts with visually salient defects, including overlapping elements, clipped text, broken alignment, low contrast, and overflow. We study visual-feedback self-distillation for code-generated visual artifacts. We propose Visual-SDPO, a self-distillation policy-optimization framework that treats rendered visual feedback as privileged context for a weight-sharing teacher and distills this feedback into a coding student. To make supervision spatially targeted rather than uniform, we introduce Visual-Grounded Code Credit Weighting, which traces each detected defect back to the code statements responsible for the affected elements and amplifies the distillation signal on those statements. A sequence-level GRPO (Group Relative Policy Optimization) term complements the dense token-level objective by rewarding executable, visually high-quality rollouts, while failed executions remain learnable through the self-distillation path by passing execution errors as privileged context to the teacher. We instantiate Visual-SDPO for chart, web/UI, and slide generation with a unified Qwen3-VL-8B-Instruct backbone. Across chart-to-code, UI-to-code, and slide-generation benchmarks (ChartMimic, Design2Code, and AeSlides), Visual-SDPO improves over the zero-shot base by more than 10 absolute points in the primary metric and over GRPO by at least 2.4 points, with fewer training steps and no added inference-time cost.
