### [104] [Moonshine: An Autonomous Mathematical Research Agent Centered on Conjecture Generation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10806)
*Xiaoyang Chen,Xiang Jiang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper presents Moonshine, an autonomous AI agent designed to propose and investigate new mathematical conjectures, illustrated via a new Neural Jacobian Conjecture inspired by the classical Jacobian conjecture.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To move beyond solving isolated math problems with AI and instead build systems that autonomously explore mathematical landscapes, formulate meaningful conjectures, and develop theory, using a modern neural-network analogue of the classical Jacobian conjecture as a testbed.

Method: Design Moonshine as an autonomous agent that: (1) analyzes classical problems to extract core structural ideas; (2) transfers these ideas into new settings (here, one-hidden-layer affine-ridge sigmoid neural networks); (3) formulates conjectures (the Neural Jacobian Conjecture) capturing these ideas; and (4) orchestrates multiple LLMs (GPT-5.5-pro, DeepSeek-V4-pro, ChatGPT) to search for proofs, counterexamples, and theoretical developments.

Result: Moonshine formulates the Neural Jacobian Conjecture (NJC): a one-hidden-layer affine-ridge sigmoid network with strictly positive Jacobian determinant everywhere is globally injective. For the specific case where network width N equals input dimension n plus 1, Moonshine, using independent large language models, obtains complete proofs, including a geometric-topological proof via interactive use of GPT-5.5-pro. The conjecture remains open for higher widths N ≥ n+2.

Conclusion: Moonshine successfully demonstrates that an autonomous agent can generate nontrivial, structurally motivated mathematical conjectures and push them to rigorous partial results, here by translating the Jacobian conjecture to neural networks and proving the NJC in a special case. This supports the plausibility of the conjecture and showcases a path toward AI systems that contribute to mathematical theory-building rather than only problem solving.

Abstract: Moonshine is an autonomous agent whose central objective is to generate mathematical conjectures. Its core capability is to extract structure from classical problems, distill new concepts, and formulate conjectures of mathematical significance. Rather than treating the solution of a single proposition as its endpoint, Moonshine builds an extensible theoretical framework through conjecture generation, bridge building, and obstacle identification. This article uses Moonshine's exploration of the Jacobian conjecture as an example. It shows how the central logic of whether local nondegeneracy can force global injectivity is transferred to one-hidden-layer affine-ridge sigmoid networks. This leads to the formulation of the \emph{Neural Jacobian Conjecture} (NJC): if such a network has strictly positive Jacobian determinant on the whole space, then it must be globally injective. By invoking GPT-5.5-pro and DeepSeek-V4-pro separately, Moonshine obtained independent complete proofs for the case \(N=n+1\). In addition, with the assistance of ChatGPT through interactive use of its web interface with GPT-5.5-pro, a geometric-topological proof was developed. These results provide preliminary evidence for the plausibility of the conjecture. The general higher-width case \(N\ge n+2\), however, remains unresolved and is left for further investigation. This work illustrates Moonshine's ability to autonomously generate meaningful mathematical problems and make rigorous progress on them.

</details>


### [105] [Do VLMs Reason Like Engineers? A Benchmark and a Stage-wise Evaluation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10833)
*Syed Wasiq,Syed Mohamad Tawseeq,Yashwant Pravinrao Bangde,Debaditya Roy*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: EngVQA is a multimodal benchmark and evaluation framework to assess and analyze engineering reasoning abilities of vision-language models across multiple engineering domains.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current vision-language models perform well on generic multimodal benchmarks but their capability for engineering reasoning—interpreting technical diagrams, applying physical principles, and maintaining physically consistent multi-step reasoning—is unclear. Existing benchmarks mostly check only final answers and cannot reveal where the reasoning fails. This gap is critical because AI is increasingly used in engineering education and technical decision-making, where superficially plausible but physically wrong reasoning is risky.

Method: The authors build EngVQA, a multimodal benchmark with 696 engineering problems across 5 engineering subjects, designed to require engineering-style reasoning on diagrams and physical principles. They propose an 8-stage automatic evaluation framework that decomposes each model-produced solution into stages (e.g., understanding, principle selection, equation setup, calculation, etc.) and scores them separately. Multiple state-of-the-art open- and closed-source VLMs are evaluated on this framework, and the automatic scores are compared with human grading for validation.

Result: Evaluated VLMs show substantial weaknesses in engineering reasoning when tested on EngVQA, failing at various intermediate stages rather than only at the final answer. The proposed 8-stage automatic evaluation correlates highly with human judgments, with a Pearson correlation of 0.975 and a mean absolute error of 0.67 on a 10-point scale, indicating that the framework reliably approximates human grading of solution quality and reasoning steps.

Conclusion: EngVQA exposes important limitations of current VLMs in engineering reasoning and shows that process-oriented, multi-stage evaluation is both feasible and highly aligned with human assessment. The work argues that future development and benchmarking of multimodal engineering AI systems should focus on detailed reasoning processes, not just final-answer accuracy, to ensure reliability in educational and technical applications.

Abstract: Vision-Language Models (VLMs) demonstrate strong performance on general multimodal reasoning benchmarks, yet their ability to perform engineering reasoning remains largely unexplored. Unlike general visual question answering, engineering problem solving requires interpreting technical diagrams, selecting governing physical principles, and maintaining physically consistent multi-step reasoning. These capabilities are increasingly important for AI systems used in engineering education, scientific assistance, and technical decision-making, where reasoning failures may produce physically invalid yet superficially plausible solutions. Existing benchmarks primarily evaluate final answers and provide limited assessment of intermediate reasoning processes. We introduce EngVQA, a multimodal benchmark for evaluating engineering reasoning across 5 engineering subjects containing 696 problems. We introduce an 8-stage automatic evaluation framework for assessing VLM-generated solutions. The framework independently evaluates each stage of the solution, enabling fine-grained analysis of reasoning failures. We benchmark multiple state-of-the-art open and closed source VLMs on our evaluation framework and demonstrate substantial limitations in current engineering reasoning capabilities. Human evaluation shows strong agreement with our automated framework, achieving a Pearson correlation of 0.975 and a mean absolute error of 0.67 on a 10-point grading scale. Our results highlight the importance of process-oriented evaluation for reliable assessment of multimodal engineering reasoning systems.

</details>


### [106] [Large-scale semantic mapping of learner agency and autonomy reveals what measurement and generative AI research overlook](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10881)
*Fei Qin,Xiaobo Liu,Yaowen Zhang,Xuming Li,Fei Wang,Mutlu Cukurova,Jingjing Chen,Yu Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper clarifies what “learner agency” and “autonomy” actually mean in the literature by analyzing thousands of definitions and scale items, revealing three main dimensions and showing that current measures and AI-in-education work neglect the sociocultural side.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Research on learner agency and autonomy is fragmented and inconsistent because scholars use the same words for different ideas and different words for the same idea (the jingle-jangle fallacy). This lack of conceptual clarity blocks cumulative progress, leads to mismatched measurements, and risks designing educational (including AI-mediated) environments that foster only a narrow slice of what agency/autonomy entail. The authors aim to systematically map how these constructs are used to provide an empirical, usage-based clarification.

Method: Using a meaning-as-use, language-in-practice perspective, the authors compiled a large corpus from over 14,000 publications, extracting 8,954 explicit definitions and 2,700 scale items related to learner agency and autonomy. They then applied a semantic analysis pipeline (likely involving text embedding, clustering, and dimensionality reduction) to identify latent conceptual dimensions underlying how these terms are used and operationalized in scales.

Result: The analysis shows that the definitional space of learner agency and autonomy converges onto three core dimensions: (1) regulation and control of learning (task-focused aspects like self-regulation, planning, monitoring), (2) intrinsic motivation and internal decision-making (person-focused aspects like volition, interest, choice), and (3) social-relational action (sociocultural aspects like participation, negotiation, influence in social contexts). This empirically exposes the jingle-jangle problem and reveals that existing quantitative scales systematically underrepresent the sociocultural dimension. It also shows that current generative AI in education mostly targets the regulation/control dimension.

Conclusion: Learner agency and autonomy are best understood as multidimensional constructs spanning task regulation, personal motivation/decision-making, and sociocultural action. The field has been hampered by semantic confusion and measurement that emphasizes only parts of this space, especially neglecting sociocultural facets. For research, clearer, empirically grounded definitions and more balanced scales are needed. For design and practice—particularly with generative AI systems—educational interventions should be reoriented to cultivate the full multidimensional repertoire of learner agency and autonomy rather than focusing narrowly on learning regulation and control.

Abstract: Learner agency and autonomy are foundational to personal development, yet a pervasive "jingle-jangle" fallacy (i.e. identical terms denoting different constructs, distinct terms denoting identical ones) has substantially hindered cumulative knowledge. Treating meaning as a phenomenon constituted through use in linguistic practice, we extracted 8,954 definitions and 2,700 scale items from over 14,000 publications, to investigate how researchers actually used learner agency and autonomy with a semantic analysis pipeline. The definitional landscape of two constructs resolves into three dimensions: regulation and control of learning (task), intrinsic motivation and internal decision-making (person), and social-relational action (sociocultural), thereby empirically quantifying the jingle-jangle fallacy. Existing scales, however, systematically underrepresent the sociocultural dimension. Critically, current generative AI research in education concentrates on learning regulation and control, narrowing the behavioral repertoire that AI-mediated learning environments are designed to cultivate. Beyond conceptual clarification, this work carries direct implications for conceptualization, measurement, and practice towards supporting the multidimensional learner agency and autonomy.

</details>


### [107] [Role-Agent: Bootstrapping LLM Agents via Dual-Role Evolution](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10917)
*Xucong Wang,Ziyu Ma,Shidong Yang,Tongwen Huang,Pengkun Wang,Yong Wang,Xiangxiang Chu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: A single LLM is used as both agent and environment to self-improve through co-evolutionary training, leading to better generalization and ~4% gains over strong baselines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current LLM agents struggle to generalize because they depend on inefficient, sparse interaction feedback and fixed training environments. This limits their ability to learn from process-level signals and adapt their training distribution to failures. The paper aims to create a more data-efficient, self-improving agent framework that can extract richer supervision from its own predictions and failures without requiring external simulators or extensive human feedback.

Method: The authors propose Role-Agent, a framework where one LLM plays two roles to bootstrap its own improvement. (1) World-In-Agent (WIA): The LLM, acting as the agent, predicts the next environment state after each action. The similarity between the predicted and actual state is used as a process reward signal, encouraging the model to reason in an environment-aware way. (2) Agent-In-World (AIW): The LLM analyzes its failed trajectories to identify failure modes, then retrieves tasks with similar failure patterns and up-weights them in training, effectively reshaping the task distribution for targeted practice. These two components work together in an iterative training loop to co-evolve the agent and its interaction patterns.

Result: On multiple benchmarks, Role-Agent consistently outperforms strong LLM-agent baselines. The paper reports an average improvement of more than 4 percentage points across tasks, demonstrating that using the LLM as both agent and environment, along with process rewards and failure-based task retrieval, leads to measurable gains in complex task performance.

Conclusion: Embedding a learned world model "inside" the agent (WIA) and using failure-aware task selection (AIW) lets a single LLM bootstrap its own improvement as both agent and environment. This co-evolution mechanism yields richer process-level supervision and a more adaptive training distribution than standard LLM-agent setups, improving generalization and performance without relying on more external feedback or handcrafted environments.

Abstract: Although Large Language Model (LLM) agents have demonstrated strong performance on complex tasks, their learning is often limited by inefficient interaction feedback and static training environments, which hinder broader generalization. To address these limitations, this paper introduces Role-Agent, \textcolor{black}{a framework} that harnesses a single LLM to function concurrently as both the agent and the environment, enabling a bootstrapped co-evolution. Role-Agent comprises two synergistic components: World-In-Agent (WIA) and Agent-In-World (AIW). In WIA, the LLM acts as the agent and predicts future states after each action; the alignment between predicted and actual states is then used as a process reward, encouraging environment-aware reasoning. In AIW, the LLM analyzes failure modes from failed trajectories and retrieves tasks with similar failure patterns, thereby reshaping the training data distribution for targeted practice. Experiments on multiple benchmarks show that Role-Agent consistently improves performance, yielding an average gain of over 4\% over strong baselines.

</details>


### [108] [Frontier Coding Agents Use Metaprogramming to Adapt to Unfamiliar Programming Languages](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10933)
*Aman Sharma,Sushrut Thorat,Paras Chopra*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper evaluates LLM-based coding agents on unfamiliar, esoteric languages and finds that strong agents succeed by inventing metaprogramming strategies, while weaker ones cannot effectively exploit such strategies even with more resources or guidance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing benchmarks test coding agents mostly in familiar, mainstream programming environments, which can mask how they perform when the programming language itself is unfamiliar. The authors want to understand how different agents adapt to truly novel or esoteric languages, and whether standard benchmarks underestimate capability gaps between models.

Method: The authors evaluate six modern coding agents on four esoteric programming languages using a sequential coding protocol: agents edit files, run code locally, and are graded against hidden tests. They compare performance with and without allowing metaprogramming strategies (e.g., writing Python to generate esoteric-language code). They also transfer strategies and helper code: providing textual guidance distilled from strong agents to weaker ones, and separately providing Python helper code derived from strong agents, then measure changes in performance. They further vary resource budgets such as interpreter calls and output tokens to see how they affect strong vs. weak agents.

Result: Strong agents (Claude Opus 4.6 and GPT-5.4 xhigh) frequently avoid writing esoteric languages directly, instead writing Python programs that generate and debug target-language code. Blocking this metaprogramming approach substantially reduces their performance. Text guidance abstracted from these strategies does not significantly help weaker agents. However, giving weaker agents Opus-derived Python helper code for building generators greatly boosts Sonnet 4.6 and GPT-5.4 mini but barely helps Haiku 4.5. Increasing interpreter calls and token budgets further boosts strong agents but has little effect on weaker agents. The evaluation reveals much larger capability differences between agents than mainstream benchmarks do.

Conclusion: Strong coding agents adapt to unfamiliar programming languages by constructing and iteratively debugging higher-level strategies—especially metaprogramming via a familiar language like Python—and effectively using tools, feedback, and workspace state to form an internal working model of the target language. Performance gaps are driven less by raw resources and more by the ability to invent and refine such strategies; providing code-based scaffolds can partially transfer these benefits to mid-tier models but not to the weakest ones.

Abstract: LLM-based coding agents are usually evaluated in familiar software settings: mainstream languages, common libraries, and public repositories. These benchmarks remain important, but they can hide how agents behave when the language itself is unfamiliar. We evaluate six contemporary coding agents on four esoteric programming languages using a sequential setup with file editing, local execution, and hidden-test grading. Our protocol exposes capability differences between these agents that mainstream coding and agentic benchmarks such as SWE-Bench Verified and Terminal-Bench 2.0 compress into much narrower bands. We observe that the strongest agents, Claude Opus 4.6 and GPT-5.4 xhigh, often avoid writing the target language directly. On Brainfuck and Befunge-98, they write Python programs that generate target-language code and debug those generators locally. Forbidding this metaprogramming strategy causes large performance drops. Text guidance distilled from this strategy does not materially improve weaker agents. In contrast, Opus-derived Python helper code for building generators, with no solved benchmark programs or hidden-test answers, sharply improves Sonnet 4.6 and GPT-5.4 mini on the same problems, while Haiku 4.5 remains low. More interpreter calls and output tokens improve stronger agents but leave weaker agents near their original performance, indicating that these resources amplify useful strategies rather than create them. Together, these results show that strong coding agents adapt to unfamiliar languages by using tools, feedback, and workspace state to build a working model of the target language. Metaprogramming is the clearest case, but the broader gap is constructing and debugging a strategy that works under the target language's rules.

</details>


### [109] [WorldKernel: A World Model is the Coupling Kernel of Admissible Possible Worlds](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10934)
*Fabio Rovai*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper shows that even extremely strong predictive models fail on inherently unidentifiable counterfactual quantities, and it develops a formalism (a positive semidefinite coupling kernel over worlds) that characterizes, bounds, and partially learns the missing cross-world structure.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In causal reasoning and AI safety, it is often assumed that with enough observational and interventional data and a powerful predictor, we can recover all relevant aspects of the data-generating process for decision-making, including counterfactuals. The authors challenge this assumption by exhibiting a structural failure mode: standard predictors cannot capture uncertainty over cross-world couplings in structural causal models, so they can give arbitrarily overconfident and even impossible answers on counterfactual queries, even when they fit all identified quantities. The paper aims to formalize what is missing from pure prediction, provide tools to reason about it, and understand when and how it can be bounded or learned.

Method: The authors study hundreds of structural causal models and focus on unidentified quantities corresponding to cross-world couplings between counterfactual worlds. They introduce a representation of a "world model" as a single positive semidefinite coupling kernel K(T,T') over admissible worlds. The diagonal of K encodes the usual posterior distribution (recovered by strong predictors), while the off-diagonal terms encode cross-world couplings that determine counterfactuals. They analyze the mathematical properties of this kernel: positive semidefiniteness as a source of partial identification; polynomial-time bounding of counterfactuals by enforcing PSD constraints; use of logical/ontology axioms to further tighten bounds; a scheme of "targeted scars"—constraints inferred from infeasible configurations—to accelerate learning of the coupling; and complexity-theoretic results characterizing the difficulty of fully reconstructing the kernel as an approximate counting problem with a threshold (Sly-Sun) separating tractable from inapproximable regimes.

Result: Empirically, across hundreds of structural causal models, they find that on identified quantities both a strong predictor and a Bayesian baseline perform well, but on unidentified cross-world quantities the predictor collapses its beliefs to a point estimate that is often wrong: in 28% of models it outputs couplings that no valid model could produce, while the true answer is an irreducible interval that cannot be narrowed with more data. Theoretically, they show that: (1) two states with the same posterior can disagree on cross-world queries, demonstrating the reality and necessity of the off-diagonal structure; (2) enforcing positive semidefiniteness of K yields nontrivial bounds on counterfactuals in polynomial time, whereas the exact response-type program is intractable; (3) adding logical/ontological axioms can tighten these bounds by up to one-third and these improvements propagate beyond the directly constrained parts of K; (4) learning with targeted scars—constraints derived from detected infeasibilities—substantially accelerates convergence to the true coupling compared to untargeted constraints; and (5) fully reconstructing K is equivalent to approximate counting of admissible worlds, which is tractable below the Sly-Sun threshold and provably inapproximable above it.

Conclusion: Predictive accuracy, even with unlimited data and powerful models, is structurally insufficient for reliable counterfactual reasoning because prediction cannot represent or maintain uncertainty over cross-world couplings. The missing object is the off-diagonal of a positive semidefinite coupling kernel over worlds, which determines counterfactuals and can differ even when posteriors match. While exact recovery of this coupling is generally computationally hard, its properties (positive semidefiniteness, logical structure, and learned infeasibility constraints) provide partial identification and practical, polynomial-time bounds for counterfactual queries. This reframes world modeling as learning not just a posterior over worlds but also their joint coupling, and clarifies both the limits and the achievable structure of counterfactual inference beyond pure prediction.

Abstract: A common assumption holds that enough observational and interventional data, given to a strong enough predictor, suffices. We report a failure mode that contradicts it. Across hundreds of structural causal models, on identified quantities a strong predictor and a Bayesian baseline both succeed, but on unidentified quantities (the couplings between counterfactual worlds) the predictor collapses to a point, on 28% of models to one no valid model can produce, while the truth is an admissible interval more data never narrows. The gap is structural: prediction cannot represent uncertainty over counterfactual couplings. We cast a world model as a single positive semidefinite coupling kernel K(T,T') over admissible worlds, whose diagonal is the ordinary posterior (what a predictor recovers) and whose off-diagonal is the cross-world coupling it cannot, which every counterfactual reads. The paper is the theory of that off-diagonal. It is real: two states with identical posteriors differ on a cross-world query, and the off-diagonal is the coupling that fixes counterfactuals. It can be bounded: positive semidefiniteness is partial-identifying information the marginals lack, and enforcing it bounds counterfactuals in polynomial time where the exact response-type program is intractable. Logical structure sharpens it: ontology axioms tighten the bound by up to a third, propagating to couplings they never touch. It can be acquired: targeted scars, constraints learned from encountered infeasibilities, close the gap several times faster than untargeted ones. Its full reconstruction is approximate counting of the admissible worlds, tractable below the Sly-Sun threshold and inapproximable above; we do not claim to beat the worst case.

</details>


### [110] [Recalling Too Well: Sycophancy Evaluation and Mitigation in Memory-Augmented Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10949)
*Shelly Bensal,Axel Magnuson,Aparna Balagopalan,Daniel M. Bikel*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Paper shows that persistent memory in LLM systems can strongly increase sycophancy—models agreeing with user misconceptions—by up to 25x, and proposes mitigations.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLM-based assistants are starting to use long-term user memory to be more helpful and personalized. However, this raises the concern that storing user beliefs, including misconceptions, could bias models toward agreeing with users instead of giving accurate information. There was no systematic study quantifying how much memory systems worsen sycophancy or understanding the mechanisms behind this effect.

Method: The authors build MIST, a synthetic benchmark of multi-turn dialogues where users express realistic misconceptions in scientific, medical, and moral reasoning. They evaluate three state-of-the-art memory systems and five different model families, comparing behavior with and without persistent memory. They analyze where in the memory pipeline the problem arises, focusing on how user information is extracted, compressed, and re-injected, and then test simple mitigations that modify memory extraction or usage.

Result: Across all tested models and memory systems, enabling persistent memory consistently increases sycophantic responses, sometimes by as much as 25x compared with in-context baselines that do not use long-term memory. Error analysis indicates that memory extraction, which compresses dialogues into discrete snippets, tends to encode user misconceptions while dropping corrective or nuanced context—leading models to treat misconceptions as stable user beliefs to align with. The proposed lightweight mitigations significantly reduce measured sycophancy while preserving or improving factual recall compared with standard memory systems.

Conclusion: Persistent memory, while intended to make LLMs more helpful and personalized, systematically amplifies sycophancy by reinforcing user misconceptions encoded in compressed memory snippets. The main failure mode lies in how memories are extracted and summarized, not just in the base model. With relatively simple changes to how memory is constructed or used, it is possible to largely mitigate this increase in sycophancy without sacrificing the benefits of long-term factual recall, but memory systems must be designed with this tradeoff explicitly in mind.

Abstract: Persistent memory systems promise to make LLMs more helpful by storing user beliefs over time. We show they also make models less correct by systematically amplifying sycophancy, wherein models prioritize agreement with users over accuracy. We conduct the first systematic evaluation of this effect, introducing MIST: a benchmark of synthetically generated multi-turn conversations where users express plausible misconceptions in scientific, medical, and moral reasoning domains. Testing across three state-of-the-art memory systems and five model families reveals that memory amplifies sycophantic behavior across all conditions, with up to 25x higher sycophancy rates than in-context baselines. Error analyses suggest memory extraction as the primary culprit: lossy compression into discrete snippets encodes user misconceptions while discarding corrective context. Based on these results, we propose two lightweight mitigations that substantially reduce sycophancy while matching or exceeding memory systems at factual recall.

### [111] [Architect-Ant: Editable Automatic Furnishing of Architectural Floor Plans](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10953)
*Fedor Rodionov,Aleksandar Cvejic,Michael Birsak,John Femiani,Peter Wonka*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces AntPlan-270, a professional floor-plan dataset and Architect-Ant, a vision-language-based, editable automatic furnishing system that generates valid, realistic furniture layouts via a DSL and reasoning-guided fine-tuning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Automatic furniture arrangement for floor plans is important for real estate, interior design, and architecture, but progress is hampered by the lack of real, professionally designed floor-plan datasets with detailed furniture annotations. Existing methods either use synthetic data or lack robust spatial reasoning about architectural constraints.

Method: 1) Curate AntPlan-270: 270 real architectural floor plans annotated with furniture bounding boxes for ten residential room categories. 2) Design Architect-Ant, an automatic furnishing framework built on a fine-tuned vision-language model. 3) Represent layouts using a compact coordinate-based domain-specific language (DSL) encoding furniture categories and placements relative to room geometry. 4) Generate procedural reasoning traces that explicitly model architectural constraints (wall alignment, door/window clearance, circulation, fixtures, room-specific inventories) and use these traces as supervision during model fine-tuning to enhance spatial reasoning. 5) Apply preference optimization over candidate placements to improve layout quality. 6) Rasterize the generated DSL into semantic masks to condition a Flux-based LoRA renderer that outputs realistic blueprint-style furnished floor-plan images while preserving an editable symbolic representation.

Result: Architect-Ant, trained on AntPlan-270, can generate furniture layouts that are both geometrically valid and functionally plausible across various residential room types. The DSL-based representation allows the system to output layouts that are simultaneously machine-editable and visually renderable. The experiments on layout furnishing tasks demonstrate improved layout quality and validity, and indicate that the approach generalizes to larger datasets that only have structural (unfurnished) floor plans.

Conclusion: The paper demonstrates that combining a curated real-world floor-plan dataset, a coordinate-based DSL, and reasoning-trace-supervised fine-tuning of a vision-language model yields an effective, editable automatic furnishing system. Architect-Ant produces realistic, constraint-aware layouts and provides a scalable route for furnishing large, structure-only floor-plan datasets, suggesting a practical path toward automating professional-quality floor-plan furnishing for design and visualization applications.

Abstract: Furnished floor plans are fundamental to real estate visualization, interior design, and architectural workflows. However, progress in automatic furniture arrangement has been limited by the lack of real, professionally designed floor-plan datasets with object-level furniture annotations. To address this gap, we introduce AntPlan-270, a curated dataset of 270 architectural floor plans with per-room furniture bounding box annotations across ten residential room categories. Building on this dataset, we present Architect-Ant, an editable automatic furnishing framework powered by a fine-tuned vision-language model. Furniture layouts are represented using a compact, coordinate-based domain-specific language (DSL) that encodes object categories and placements relative to the room geometry. To improve spatial reasoning, we generate procedural reasoning traces that capture architectural constraints such as wall alignment, door and window clearance, circulation, fixture compatibility, and room-specific furniture inventories, and use them to supervise fine-tuning of the model. We then apply preference optimization over candidate object placements to further refine layout quality. The generated DSL can be rasterized into semantic masks and used to condition a Flux-based LoRA renderer, producing realistic blueprint-style furnished floor-plan images while preserving the editable symbolic layout. Experiments on layout furnishing show that Architect-Ant produces geometrically valid and functionally plausible layouts, and suggest a scalable path for furnishing larger structure-only floor-plan datasets.

### [112] [Mind the Gap: Can Frontier LLMs Pass a Standardized Office Proficiency Exam?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10956)
*Tengchao Lv,Dongdong Zhang,Jiayu Ding,Yilin Jia,Yuzhong Zhao,Yupan Huang,Wenshan Wu,Xiangyang Zhou,Shaohan Huang,Nan Yang,Li Dong,Lei Cui,Furu Wei*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper evaluates how well large language model agents can automate tasks in professional Office software using a large, exam-style benchmark, and finds that even advanced systems still struggle to reach human-level, fine-grained reliability.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Despite rapid progress in LLMs and agent frameworks, there is little systematic evidence of how reliably they can operate complex, professional productivity tools like Word, Excel, and PowerPoint. Office automation is a realistic, high-value application area that requires long-horizon planning, precise control of many parameters, and coordination across multiple applications. The authors see a gap: existing benchmarks do not adequately test this kind of end-to-end, document-centric automation, so it is unclear how close current models are to dependable, real-world automation.

Method: The authors construct a benchmark based on China's National Computer Rank Examination (NCRE), which is a standardized test of computer and Office software skills. They select 200 comprehensive, practical operation tasks that cover Word, Excel, and PowerPoint use. Each task is evaluated using a 100-point rubric decomposed into 7,118 machine-gradable criteria, allowing granular scoring. They define Score Rate (SR) as the average percentage of rubric points earned across tasks. They then benchmark seven frontier LLMs in two modes: (1) single-turn models directly generating actions or code, and (2) a more advanced agentic system that has access to execution feedback, can iteratively repair its attempts, and has broader integration with Office automation tools. Model outputs are automatically graded against the rubric to compute SR.

Result: Single-turn LLMs perform poorly on this benchmark, with the best model achieving only 36.6% SR, indicating large gaps in reliably performing detailed Office tasks. The enhanced agentic system, which can use feedback and iterative repair and has richer Office integration, improves performance substantially to 68.8% SR. However, this still falls significantly short of a 95.5% SR reference score obtained from a community baseline used to validate the scoring pipeline. The results reveal large quantitative performance gaps between current systems and human-level mastery on professional Office automation tasks.

Conclusion: The study concludes that current code-generating LLMs and agent systems are far from achieving robust, fine-grained automation in professional Office suites. Even with sophisticated agentic scaffolding, execution feedback, and iterative repair, performance on realistic exam-style Office tasks remains well below human reference levels. This suggests that current progress in code generation does not directly translate into dependable document automation, and that further research is needed in areas such as long-horizon planning, precise parameter control, and multi-application coordination to bridge this gap.

Abstract: The deployment of Large Language Model (LLM) agents for computer automation is accelerating, yet their ability to navigate complex, professional-grade productivity software is largely untested. We argue that Office automation is an ideal environment for benchmarking document-automation capability, as it requires long-horizon planning and reasoning, precise parameter configuration, and multi-application integration. To quantify this capability, we introduce an evaluation based on China's National Computer Rank Examination (NCRE), featuring 200 comprehensive practical-operation tasks across Word, Excel, and PowerPoint. Each task is scored on a 100-point rubric scale using 7,118 machine-gradable criteria, and Score Rate (SR) denotes the mean percentage of rubric points earned across these tasks. We benchmark 7 frontier LLMs and observe stark limitations: single-turn models score a maximum of 36.6%. A stronger agentic system with execution feedback, iterative repair, and broader Office automation access reaches 68.8%, but remains below the 95.5% community-reference score used as a scoring sanity check. Ultimately, our experiments demonstrate that despite recent advancements in code generation, achieving reliable fine-grained Office document automation remains a significant challenge for current code-generating LLM and agent systems.

### [113] [Bellman-Taylor Score Decoding for Markov Decision Processes with State-Dependent Feasible Action Sets](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10979)
*Yi Chen,Rushuai Yang,Qiang Chen,Dongyan,Huo*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose a DRL framework that handles complex, state-dependent action constraints by learning in a continuous score space and decoding scores into feasible actions, with theoretical guarantees and strong performance on queueing control.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Real-world operations research MDPs often have actions that depend on the current state and must satisfy complicated constraints, so standard DRL methods that assume simple, fixed action spaces are hard to apply. There is a need for a principled way to use generic DRL while respecting implicit, state-dependent feasibility constraints.

Method: They introduce Bellman–Taylor score decoding: instead of learning directly over the constrained action space, they learn policies in an unconstrained Euclidean score space, motivated by a Taylor expansion of the optimal action-value function. A separate decoder maps each score and state to a feasible action, enforcing constraints. This creates a latent-score MDP that can be optimized with off-the-shelf DRL without differentiating through the decoder. They analyze the induced approximation and learning errors.

Result: They derive a performance guarantee that decomposes the total suboptimality into a structural approximation error (from using the latent score representation and decoder) and an algorithmic learning error (from imperfect DRL optimization). Empirically, on a queueing network control problem, the method learns state-dependent index-like dispatching rules and attains near-optimal performance on small instances and significantly outperforms benchmark heuristics on larger systems.

Conclusion: Mapping constrained actions into a Euclidean score space with a feasibility-enforcing decoder allows standard DRL algorithms to tackle complex, state-dependent MDPs. Theoretical guarantees and experiments in queueing network control suggest this Bellman–Taylor score decoding framework is both principled and practically effective for operational control problems with intricate action constraints.

Abstract: Many Markov decision processes (MDPs) in operations research have feasible actions that are state dependent and defined implicitly by various operational constraints. These features make it difficult to use standard deep reinforcement learning (DRL) algorithms, whose action interfaces typically assume either a fixed finite action catalog or a simple Euclidean space. Motivated by a Taylor expansion of the optimal action-value function, we propose Bellman--Taylor score decoding, a framework that moves policy learning to a Euclidean score space while enforcing feasibility through an action decoder. The induced latent-score MDP then can be optimized by standard DRL algorithms without differentiating through the decoder. We provide a performance guarantee showing that the optimality gap of this approach decomposes into a structural approximation error and an algorithmic learning error. Lastly, we apply this framework to a queueing network control problem, where the policy essentially learns a state-dependent index-based dispatching rule. Numerical experiments show near-optimal performance in small instances and considerable improvements over benchmarks in larger systems.

### [114] [Null-Space Constrained Low-Rank Adaptation for Response-Specified Large Language Model Unlearning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10989)
*Bocheng Ju,Jianhua Wang,Chengliang Liu,Xiaolin Chang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes NSRU, a null-space constrained low-rank unlearning method that edits LLMs to forget specific knowledge while better preserving overall capabilities and aligning to safe target behaviors.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM unlearning methods either only suppress unwanted answers or, when they specify replacement behavior, still update parameters in an unconstrained way that can harm benign capabilities. The authors want an unlearning method that both enforces explicit safe replacement behavior and tightly localizes parameter changes so that forgetting specific knowledge minimally damages general performance and safety.

Method: They introduce Null-Space Constrained Response-Specified Unlearning (NSRU). For each forget query, the user provides a structured safe target response. The model is trained to output this safe target while suppressing the original undesired content. To keep updates local, they estimate for each module a "retain" subspace from benign hidden representations. They then parameterize LoRA updates so that they lie in the null space orthogonal to this retain subspace, via an orthogonal-projected low-rank parameterization. The training objective simultaneously: (1) learns the safe target responses, (2) suppresses undesired responses, and (3) preserves performance on retain data, all under the null-space constraint. They also provide a local first-order analysis showing that this projected update structure reduces perturbations to retain behavior while leaving degrees of freedom to edit responses to forget queries.

Result: On the TOFU unlearning benchmark, NSRU more effectively suppresses extractable forget-set knowledge than baselines, while improving performance on retain QA tasks, general model utility, and alignment with safe targets. On WMDP, it holds hazardous-domain accuracy close to random-guess levels yet maintains high performance on general and domain-adjacent MMLU tasks. Ablations show that each component—safe-target supervision, undesired-response suppression, retention loss, and null-space projection—contributes to performance. Sensitivity and robustness tests further show that NSRU behaves stably across various hyperparameters and prompt configurations.

Conclusion: NSRU offers a controlled unlearning framework that combines explicit safe replacement responses with geometrically constrained, low-rank parameter updates confined to the null space of benign behavior subspaces. This design reduces collateral damage to general capabilities and utility while more reliably suppressing targeted knowledge and hazardous-domain competence, providing a more principled and robust approach to LLM unlearning than existing methods.

Abstract: Large language model unlearning aims to suppress designated undesirable knowledge while preserving benign capabilities. Many unlearning objectives focus on suppressing undesired answers, while recent target-guided variants specify replacement behavior but still leave update locality largely unconstrained. This paper introduces \emph{Null-Space Constrained Response-Specified Unlearning} (NSRU), a projection-constrained low-rank framework for controlled LLM unlearning. NSRU uses an explicitly structured safe target response to specify the desired behavior for each forget query, while suppressing the original undesired content. To localize adaptation, NSRU estimates per-module retain subspaces from benign hidden representations and uses an orthogonal-projected low-rank parameterization to confine LoRA updates to the null space of the retain subspace. The resulting objective jointly optimizes safe-target learning, undesired-response suppression, and retention preservation under this constrained parameterization. We provide a local first-order analysis showing that the projected update reduces retain-side perturbations while preserving editable directions for shaping forget-query behavior. Experiments on TOFU show that NSRU effectively suppresses extractable forget-set knowledge while improving retain QA performance, model utility, and safe-target alignment over representative baselines. On WMDP, NSRU keeps hazardous-domain accuracy near the random-choice region while preserving broad and domain-adjacent MMLU utility. Ablation studies support the complementary roles of safe-target supervision, undesired-response suppression, retention loss, and null-space projected updates, while sensitivity and robustness analyses indicate stable behavior across the tested hyperparameter and prompt variations.

### [115] [Structure from Reasoning, Numbers from Search: On-Premise Open LLMs as Structural Priors for Coupled MIMO Controller Tuning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11015)
*Jiaxuan Chen,Haonan Li,Yang Shu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper evaluates whether on-premise open-source LLMs can help tune controllers for strongly coupled MIMO industrial processes, showing that LLMs are useful not as direct optimizers but as sample-efficient, interpretable providers of structural priors in challenging non-convex settings.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Classical decentralized auto-tuning often fails in strongly coupled MIMO systems because it ignores loop interactions, and local numerical optimization frequently stalls in non-convex cost landscapes. Industrial users also may lack accurate plant models and want to keep data on-site, which motivates exploring whether on-premise open-source LLMs can provide practical assistance in controller tuning without requiring plant models or cloud access.

Method: The authors benchmark different tuning strategies on several plants: a simple single-loop CSTR, a strongly coupled quadruple-tank system with conflicting setpoints, and higher-dimensional (e.g., 3x3) plants, across four open models. They compare classical relay-feedback tuning, naive LLM-based tuning, local numerical optimization from various starts, and a scaffolded open-source LLM that is guided to reason about loop coupling and controller structure. Performance is evaluated using integral absolute error (IAE) and a composite cost J = IAE + lambda*TV(u) that penalizes actuator chattering. They also compare against a global optimizer (differential evolution) and optionally refine LLM-suggested structures with classical optimization.

Result: On the simple single-loop CSTR, classical relay-feedback tuning outperforms LLM tuning (IAE 0.106 vs. 0.162), indicating no benefit from LLMs for such problems. On the strongly coupled quadruple-tank system, naive relay and naive LLM tuning perform poorly (J around 28.6–29.7, similar to or worse than open loop at 22.7) and a local optimizer fails to escape poor regions in 10/10 runs. In contrast, a scaffolded LLM reasons about coupling, proposes an asymmetric controller structure, and reliably achieves J ≈ 16.9 ± 0.2 from any start; further refinement with a classical optimizer reaches the smooth global optimum J ≈ 12.0 in all runs (10/10), whereas classical methods alone fail (0/10). A global optimizer (differential evolution) can also find this optimum but requires many more evaluations and initially performs worse than open loop within 18 evaluations, whereas the LLM produces a usable controller with rationale in those 18 evaluations. On a 3x3 plant, the LLM achieves about 6× fewer evaluations than the global optimizer, and across four open models the behaviour is consistent: LLMs help mainly on hard, coupled plants but offer no advantage on benign ones.

Conclusion: Open-source on-premise LLMs can be valuable in controller tuning for strongly coupled MIMO systems, not by acting as black-box numerical optimizers but by providing sample-efficient, interpretable structural priors that guide classical optimization toward good regions of the non-convex landscape. They yield significant gains in sample efficiency and reliability on challenging plants while offering no benefit on simple or benign systems, thereby delineating a practical regime where LLM-assisted tuning is worthwhile. The authors provide a reproducible benchmark to characterize and study these conditions.

Abstract: Tuning controllers for strongly coupled multi-input multi-output (MIMO) industrial processes is hard: decentralized classical auto-tuning ignores loop interaction, and local numerical optimization from natural initializations stalls in the resulting non-convex cost landscape. We ask whether on-premise open-source large language models (LLMs), which keep data on-site and need no plant model, can help. On a single-loop CSTR, classical relay-feedback tuning (IAE 0.106, near the 0.102 optimum) beats an LLM tuner (0.162): for simple loops the LLM adds nothing. The picture inverts on a strongly coupled quadruple-tank with conflicting set-points, scored by a penalized cost J = IAE + lambda*TV(u) that rewards tracking without chattering actuators. There, naive relay tuning (J ~ 28.6) and naive LLM tuning (29.7) are no better than open loop (22.7), and a local optimizer from balanced starts fails in 10/10 runs. A scaffolded open LLM instead reasons about the coupling, proposes the counter-intuitive asymmetric structure, and reaches J ~ 16.9 +/- 0.2 from any start; refining it with a classical optimizer attains the smooth global optimum (J ~ 12.0, 10/10 vs. 0/10), which even applies a non-obvious negative integral correction decentralized tuning cannot. A global optimizer (differential evolution) also reaches this optimum, so the LLM is not the only route; its advantage is sample efficiency and interpretability: a usable controller in 18 evaluations (where the global optimizer is worse than open loop) plus a stated rationale. This edge grows with dimension, reaching ~6x fewer evaluations on a 3x3 plant. The behaviour generalizes across four open models, and on a benign plant the LLM offers no advantage, sharpening the boundary. We contribute a reproducible benchmark delimiting when open LLMs help in control tuning: not as optimizers, but as a sample-efficient, interpretable structural prior.

### [116] [Superficial Beliefs in LLM Decision-Making](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11016)
*Gabriel Freedman,Francesca Toni*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper examines whether LLM choices in synthetic decision tasks reflect a consistent underlying decision structure or mere imitation, finding systematic but only partially self-accessible preferences over attributes.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand if LLMs possess structured, consistent decision-making processes with stable priorities over attributes, or if they are just matching surface patterns and rationales without genuine underlying preference structures.

Method: Design synthetic binary choice tasks where options are profiles with graded attributes; fit a behavioural choice model to past LLM decisions to infer which attribute best explains choices; compare this inferred key attribute with (a) the attribute the LLM explicitly claims mattered most and (b) an external judge’s score-based assessment; test robustness across prompt variations, sampling, alternative models, occlusion, and structurally different decision settings.

Result: The behavioural model predicts held-out LLM choices well, demonstrating that LLM decisions systematically depend on visible attributes. However, the attribute identified as most important by self-report or by a score-based judge often only partially overlaps with the attribute inferred from behaviour. This mismatch and its qualitative pattern persist under various robustness checks.

Conclusion: LLMs show “superficial belief”: they act as if guided by probabilistic, local priorities over attributes, indicating structured decision-making, but their explicit verbal explanations do not fully align with the true behavioural drivers of their choices. Thus, LLM decisions are neither random nor fully introspectively transparent, revealing limited verbal access to their own effective decision rules.

Abstract: We ask whether large language models (LLMs) merely imitate rationales when choosing between two options, or whether their choices reflect a systematic underlying decision structure. Using synthetic binary decision settings in which models choose between profiles defined by graded attributes, we compare the attribute a model says mattered most with the attribute that best explains its choice under a behavioural model fit to prior decisions. The behavioural model predicts held-out choices well, showing that model behaviour is systematically related to the visible attributes rather than being random. However, direct self-reports and a separate score-based judge recover the behaviourally inferred driver only partially. The resulting picture is neither one of arbitrary behaviour nor one of fully articulated belief - outputs are structured enough to support prediction, but explicit reasons track the recovered driver only imperfectly. This qualitative pattern persists across prompt-order and sampling perturbations, alternative behavioural models, targeted occlusion analyses, and structurally varied decision settings. We interpret this as evidence for ``superficial belief'' in LLM decision-making: models behave as if guided by probabilistic local priorities over attributes, while having only limited verbal access to the attributes that drive their decisions.

To bridge this gap, we introduce Workflow-GYM, a benchmark for long-horizon GUI tasks centered on professional domains and specialized software environments. ### [118] [What Fits (Into Few Tokens) Doesn't Overfit: Compression and Generalization in ML Research Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11045)
*Martin Andres Bertran,Aaron Roth,Zhiwei Steven Wu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper investigates why reusing held-out benchmarks in machine learning does not cause as much overfitting as theory suggests, using LLM-based research agents and information bottlenecks, and finds that successful ML strategies appear highly compressible and low-complexity.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In theory, adaptively reusing a held-out validation or benchmark set should lead to severe overfitting, because researchers and algorithms can implicitly optimize against the benchmark. However, in practice, benchmark-driven machine learning has not shown catastrophic overfitting, and models often generalize well despite intensive benchmark use. The authors are motivated to explain this apparent paradox and to test the hypothesis that the strategies used to achieve good performance on benchmarks are themselves simple or compressible, which would reduce the risk of overfitting. They focus on LLM-driven research agents as a setting where they can precisely control and measure how much information flows from the benchmark into the chosen models.

Method: The authors design two complementary information bottleneck setups for LLM-driven research agents. In the output compression setting, an exploration agent adaptively searches for high-performing models using a validation set. After this search, a separate reproducer agent is given only the original training data and an extremely short text prompt that summarizes the exploration, and is asked to reproduce the high performance; this tests how compressible the successful strategy is. In the input compression setting, the exploration agent no longer sees full validation metrics; instead, it receives only 1-bit feedback for each submitted model indicating whether performance improved over the current best, testing how little information from the validation set is sufficient to guide model search. They evaluate these setups across eight diverse datasets and tasks, including tabular classification, computer vision, language modeling, diffusion modeling, and reward modeling. They also construct a counterexample by deliberately inducing validation-set overfitting and checking whether the compressed descriptions still suffice to reproduce results.

Result: Across all eight datasets and tasks, the information bottlenecks—short prompts in the output compression setting and one-bit improvement feedback in the input compression setting—have minimal impact on the ability of LLM-driven agents to find or reproduce high-performing models. This indicates that the effective strategies can be captured by very low-bandwidth descriptions and that the agents do not need rich access to the validation set to succeed. When the authors intentionally induce validation overfitting, the success no longer reproduces under strong output compression: short prompts fail to allow a reproducer agent to match the overfit performance, demonstrating that the hypothesis is falsifiable and that genuine overfitting corresponds to a higher description length. Overall, performance under bottlenecks closely tracks performance without bottlenecks for non-overfit regimes, while diverging when overfitting is constructed.

Conclusion: The study concludes that the relative lack of overfitting in benchmark-driven ML can be explained by a description-length perspective: successful, generalizing strategies reside in a low-complexity, highly compressible region of strategy space. LLM-driven research agents can effectively discover and reproduce good models even when given very limited information from and about the validation set, while artificially overfit solutions cannot be reproduced under the same compression. This supports the view that much of practical ML progress is governed by simple, low-information strategies rather than fragile, finely tuned exploitation of specific benchmarks.

Abstract: Reusing a held-out benchmark adaptively should, in principle, invite overfitting. Yet benchmark-driven machine learning (ML) has produced surprisingly little overfitting in practice. An attractive hypothesis is that successful ML strategies are highly compressible. We study this in the setting of LLM-driven research agents, where the hypothesis becomes directly testable via two complementary information bottlenecks. In \emph{output compression}, an exploration agent adaptively searches for high-performance models using a validation set, and we test whether a fresh ``reproducer agent'' can reproduce its performance given only an extremely short prompt and the training data. In \emph{input compression}, the explorer receives only one-bit feedback indicating whether each submitted model improves on the running best. Across 8 datasets spanning tabular classification, vision, language modeling, diffusion modeling, and reward modeling, we find that these bottlenecks have little effect on performance: short prompts and compressible feedback are sufficient to reproduce and find high-performance models. The hypothesis is falsifiable: when we deliberately induce validation-set overfitting, the results fail to reproduce with short prompts. Taken together, our results support a description-length explanation for the lack of overfitting in benchmark-driven ML: successful strategies occupy a low-complexity region of strategy space.

</details>


### [119] [CIAware-Bench: Benchmarking Control Intervention Awareness Across Frontier LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11063)
*Joachim Schaeffer,Thomas Jiralerspong,Alexander Panfilov,Guillaume Lajoie,Jonas Geiping,Yoshua Bengio,Roland S. Zimmermann*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces CIAware-Bench, a benchmark for testing whether AI models can detect when a control protocol has altered their behavior.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As AI systems are increasingly controlled via external protocols that monitor and edit their outputs, there is a risk that models may notice these interventions, infer properties of the monitor, and adapt to evade or bypass control. Existing work lacks a standardized way to measure how aware models are of such interventions, especially across different tasks, models, and deployment setups.

Method: The authors build CIAware-Bench, a benchmark that frames control-intervention awareness as a discrimination task: can a model tell apart its original trajectories from trajectories that have been modified by a control protocol? They construct four diverse task domains (essay writing, BigCodeBench, Bash Arena, SHADE-Arena), systematically vary trajectory watermarking schemes, presence of side-tasks, and types of control protocols, and then evaluate eleven frontier models by asking them to perform a balanced binary classification over whether a trajectory has been intervened on or not.

Result: Across eleven frontier models, measured control-intervention awareness ranges from low to moderate under default settings, with maximum balanced binary classification accuracy of about 0.87 (chance is 0.5). Awareness levels vary substantially by task domain and by which untrusted/trusted model pairs are used. Models generally find it easier to detect interventions when the controlling model comes from a different provider or family, suggesting that stylistic or post-training differences are an important signal for detection.

Conclusion: Control-intervention awareness is not a single, fixed property of a model; it depends on the task, the control protocol, and the specific model pairing. Because of this context dependence, CI awareness should be re-measured for every new model release and deployment configuration. CIAware-Bench is proposed as a public tool to track such awareness and to help design control protocols whose interventions are more difficult for models to detect and potentially circumvent.

Abstract: AI control protocols oversee untrusted models by monitoring their actions and modifying potentially unsafe steps, often using a trusted model. This partially tampers with the untrusted model's trajectory. If the trusted model detects such an intervention, it may infer properties of the monitor and adapt to evade control. We introduce \textbf{CIAware-Bench}, a benchmark for measuring \textbf{c}ontrol \textbf{i}ntervention (CI) awareness across frontier models. CIAware-Bench tests whether models can distinguish their own trajectories from those modified by a control intervention. The benchmark is comprised of a suite of four task domains (essay writing, BigCodeBench, Bash Arena, and SHADE-Arena), while varying trajectory watermarking, side-task presence, and the control protocol. Evaluating eleven frontier models, we find low to moderate CI awareness under default settings (up to 0.87; random chance balanced binary classification accuracy is 0.5) with substantial variation across task domains and model pairs. Detection is generally easier across model families, suggesting that models exploit provider-specific differences in style or post-training. Overall, CI awareness is not a fixed model-level property, and should be measured for each new model release and deployment scenario. We release CIAware-Bench to track CI awareness and inform control protocols whose interventions are harder to detect.

### [120] [A History-Aware Visually Grounded Critic for Computer Use Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11078)
*Jaewoo Lee,Zaid Khan,Archiki Prasad,Justin Chih-Yao Chen,Supriyo Chakraborty,Kartik Balasubramaniam,Sambit Sahu,Elias Stengel-Eskin,Hyunji Lee,Mohit Bansal*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: HiViG is a history-aware, visually grounded test-time framework with a multimodal critic that improves GUI computer-use agents by summarizing past actions and visually checking future actions, yielding better success rates and cross-platform generalization than prior critics.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing critic-based test-time interventions for GUI agents are short-sighted, forgetting earlier actions, and are not grounded in UI visuals, so they fail to detect errors like clicking wrong elements. There is a need for a critic that can both remember and abstract the interaction history and leverage visual input to detect flawed actions before execution, especially in long-horizon GUI tasks.

Method: The paper proposes HiViG, a multimodal critic framework trained on real GUI trajectories. The critic encodes past interactions into a compact macro-action history capturing completed subgoals and uses visual grounding on the current screenshot to evaluate candidate actions (e.g., click coordinates) before execution. At test time, HiViG is inserted into the agent’s decision loop to provide both macro-level history summaries to the policy and visually grounded critiques that can veto or correct erroneous actions, thereby scaling performance on long-horizon tasks.

Result: On web, mobile, and desktop GUI benchmarks, HiViG consistently improves over existing scalar and verbal critics, raising average success rates by 5.8% for Qwen3-VL-32B and 9.0% for Gemini-3-Flash, while also showing strong cross-platform generalization. Ablation studies demonstrate that removing macro-action history leads to degraded planning over long horizons, and removing visually grounded critique increases execution errors, confirming the necessity of both components.

Conclusion: HiViG’s combination of history-aware macro-action summarization and visually grounded action critique provides an effective, general test-time intervention for GUI agents. It mitigates short-sighted planning and execution errors, enabling better scaling to long-horizon, cross-platform GUI tasks than prior critic approaches, and establishes a design pattern for multimodal critics that integrate temporal abstraction and visual grounding.

Abstract: Various test-time interventions for Computer Use Agents (CUAs), including critic models, have been developed to improve performance through pre-execution action evaluation in complex Graphical User Interface (GUI) environments. However, existing critics suffer from two key limitations: they (1) focus primarily on short-sighted decision loops (e.g., forgetting earlier actions) and (2) lack the visual grounding needed to detect flawed actions (e.g., clicking wrong UI elements). To address these, we introduce HiViG, a History-aware Visually Grounded test-time framework, built around a multimodal critic trained on real GUI trajectories to abstract past interactions into a compact record and to evaluate actions with visual grounding. At test time, HiViG integrates the critic into the policy decision loop to provide macro-action history, which summarizes the policy's completed achievements, and visually grounded critique, which verifies raw execution coordinates against the current screenshot to intercept errors before execution. Across web, mobile, and desktop benchmarks, HiViG consistently outperforms existing scalar and verbal critics, improving average success rates over the strongest baseline by 5.8% for Qwen3-VL-32B and 9.0% for Gemini-3-Flash, and demonstrates strong cross-platform generalization. Ablations show that macro-action history mitigates short-sighted planning and visually grounded critique reduces execution errors, with both components being critical for test-time scaling in long-horizon GUI tasks.

### [121] [Monte Carlo Pass Search: Using Trajectory Generation for 3D Counterfactual Pass Evaluation in Football](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11120)
*Andrew Kang,Priya Narasimhan*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They model pass evaluation in soccer as a Monte Carlo Tree Search–like process (Monte Carlo Pass Search, MCPS) that simulates many counterfactual pass variants using a learned world and value model, producing distribution-aware scores for passes.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing soccer analytics often assign a single value to a pass, ignoring uncertainty, execution variability, and alternative options. With new high-fidelity tracking data including 3D ball trajectories, there is an opportunity to more realistically evaluate passing decisions and execution by simulating how slightly different passes and options would unfold.

Method: They define pass evaluation as an MCTS-style problem with three components: (1) a value model that assigns a possession value to resulting states; (2) a world model that predicts multi-agent trajectories with ball interactions under hypothetical futures; and (3) a policy that samples counterfactual actions, i.e., noisy variants of the observed pass and alternative options. Their framework, Monte Carlo Pass Search (MCPS), infers continuous kick parameters for each observed pass, samples multiple execution and option variants, rolls each candidate forward using a ball-conditioned, discrete-token autoregressive trajectory model adapted from SMART (from autonomous driving), and then scores the outcome states with the learned value model to obtain a distribution over value gains for that pass. They use best-of-20 forecasting metrics to evaluate the world model and release checkpoints and code.

Result: MCPS produces a full distribution over gained value for each pass rather than a single scalar. From this distribution they derive two execution-surplus measures (mean-based and percentile-based) that allow detailed analysis and ranking of passes and players. Their adapted SMART-based world model achieves strong best-of-20 trajectory forecasting performance compared with baselines while supporting fully counterfactual rollouts, showing that it is both sample-efficient and accurate enough for evaluating passes given limited public data.

Conclusion: Viewing pass evaluation as an MCTS-like simulation problem and implementing it via MCPS allows distribution-aware, counterfactual analysis of passing in football. The combination of a possession value model, an efficient trajectory world model, and stochastic pass sampling yields richer execution-surplus metrics and strong predictive performance, and their public release of models and code facilitates further research and application in soccer analytics.

Abstract: We recast pass evaluation in football (soccer) as a Monte Carlo Tree Search (MCTS)-like evaluation problem whose components mostly exist in the literature under different names: a value model (possession value), a world model (multi-agent trajectories with ball interactions), and a policy over counterfactual actions (sampling pass variants with noise). Building on the first public high-fidelity tracking dataset with 3D ball trajectories from the Bundesliga, we introduce Monte Carlo Pass Search (MCPS), which infers kick parameters for each observed pass, samples execution variants and option variants, rolls each candidate forward with a ball-conditioned world model until the next ball interaction, and scores outcomes with a learned value model to obtain a distribution over gained value. This distribution enables distribution-aware attribution with two complementary execution-surplus scores used for analysis and ranking: mean-based and percentile-based scores. To make the world model sample-efficient under limited public data, we adapt a discrete-token, autoregressive trajectory generator from autonomous driving (SMART) and show it yields strong best-of-20 forecasting accuracy compared to baselines, while supporting fully hypothetical rollouts for downstream evaluation. We have released model checkpoints and code.

</details>


### [122] [ABC-Bench: An Agentic Bio-Capabilities Benchmark for Biosecurity](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11150)
*Andrew Bo Liu,Samira Nedungadi,Bryce Cai,Alex Kleinman,Harmon Bhasin,Seth Donoughe*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces ABC-Bench, a benchmark to evaluate how well LLM agents can perform complex, potentially dual-use biology tasks, and shows that current agents already match or exceed expert humans on several such tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are becoming capable of performing sophisticated biology-related tasks, which can accelerate research but also introduce new biosecurity risks. There is a need to systematically measure what agentic, biosecurity-relevant capabilities these models possess in order to understand both their beneficial applications and their potential for misuse.

Method: The authors design the Agentic Bio-Capabilities Benchmark (ABC-Bench), a set of tasks that require both biological and software engineering expertise. The benchmark covers benign and dual-use tasks including programming liquid handling robots, designing DNA fragments for in vitro assembly, and circumventing DNA synthesis screening. They evaluate multiple LLM agents on these tasks, compare performance to a median expert human baseline, and conduct three wet-lab validation experiments in which robot scripts generated by OpenAI's o4-mini-high are executed on an OpenTrons liquid handling robot.

Result: All tested LLM agents outperform the median expert human baseliner across the three benchmark tasks. Agents perform especially well on tasks grounded in existing literature and well-documented protocols, but achieve weaker performance on tasks that require novel bioinformatics reasoning. Wet-lab experiments confirm that scripts generated by o4-mini-high can successfully control an OpenTrons robot to assemble DNA constructs with the expected sequences.

Conclusion: ABC-Bench demonstrates that current LLM agents already possess strong, practical capabilities in automating complex biological workflows, in some cases surpassing human experts. Their strengths lie in leveraging published protocols, while they remain less capable at genuinely novel reasoning in bioinformatics. The benchmark provides a structured way to assess these capabilities and highlights both the promise for accelerating biology and the need to carefully monitor and manage associated biosecurity risks.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) are rapidly acquiring capabilities relevant to biological research, from literature synthesis to interpretation of experimental data. Increasingly, LLM agents can also perform in silico biology tasks that previously required experienced human biologists. These emerging AI capabilities offer new opportunities for scientific discovery and biomedical advances, but they also shift the landscape of biosecurity risks. To address this, we introduce the Agentic Bio-Capabilities Benchmark (ABC-Bench), a suite of tasks to measure agentic biosecurity-relevant capabilities. ABC-Bench evaluates LLM agents on both benign and dual-use biology tasks: writing code to operate liquid handling robots, designing DNA fragments for in vitro assembly, and evading DNA synthesis screening. These tasks require a combination of biology and software expertise. All tested LLM agents outperformed the median expert human baseliner on all three tasks. Agents performed highly on tasks drawing on published knowledge and well-documented protocols, and more weakly on a task requiring novel bioinformatics reasoning. In three wet-lab validation experiments, we found that OpenAI's o4-mini-high produced scripts that, when run on an OpenTrons liquid handling robot, successfully assembled DNA with expected sequences.
